I. THE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR IN VENEZUELA
A. Country Overview
1. 1.  Venezuela, situated on the north coast of South America and bordered by
Colombia, Brazil and Guyana, is the continent's sixth largest country with an area
estimated at 916,442 square kilometers. In addition to its mainland, Venezuela possesses
72 islands and a maritime area of 362,000 square kilometers (the country's declared
exclusive economic zone). The total population was estimated in mid-1994 at about 21
million inhabitants, some 83% of whom live in urban areas located mainly in the northern
states. Venezuela's per capita income was estimated to be about US$ 2,760 in 1994. Oil
and several minerals account for approximately 90% of total exports. Oil production,
mining, petrochemicals and energy have in recent years generated up to 30% of Gross
Domestic Product (GDP); other manufacturing activities have accounted for another
12%, while agriculture has accounted for approximately 5% of GDP.
B. Macroeconomic Linkages
1.2.  Crisis and Reform. In Venezuela, since the late 1960s, abundant oil earnings
have traditionally accounted for 70 to 80% of govemment revenues. This has encouraged
large public spending characterized by low efficiency and low cost-recovery levels in the
public utilities sectors. In 1989, following ten years of economic decline due to falling oil
prices, deficient fiscal policy and insufficient development of non-oil activities, the
government engaged in a comprehensive structural adjustment program, including sectoral
and public administration reforms. The program included the removal of price-controls
and non-tariff barriers to trade, the liberalization of interest and foreign exchange rates and
the implementation of a schedule to increase the price of gasoline, electricity and other
public services and goods, including water. Although the program resulted in a fairly
liberalized economic environment, with an attempt to decentralize public services and
utilities (although keeping high subsidies for petroleum products), it stalled in 1992.
1.3.   The new Government, which took office in February 1994, abandoned the
liberalizing reform approach of the previous administration. In June 1994, in an attempt to
respond to an un-sustainable economic situation, foreign exchange controls were put in
place, along with other price controls, and the bolivar was fixed at a rate of 170 to the
US$. These policy changes contributed further to destabilizing the economy. Faced with
eroding oil prices, a banking sector crisis, and a resulting foreign exchange crisis, the
economy deteriorated significantly in 1994 as the fiscal deficit grew to about 14% of GDP
(including the costs incurred from the banking crisis), inflation accelerated to about 70%
and GDP contracted by about 3%.



2
1.4.   Although the Government responded with some corrective economic measures,
such as increasing premium fuel prices by 140% and opening the oil prospecting market to
foreign companies to stabilize the economy, preliminary data for 1995 indicate that the
economic performance was poor (although slightly better than in 1994): the fiscal deficit
was about 9% of GDP (including the cost associated with the banking crisis), the inflation
rate was estimated at about 60%; and GDP grew by only 2%. To stabilize the economy,
the government initiated discussions in late 1995 with the International Monetary Fund
(IMF) to formulate a comprehensive reform program. It also devalued the bolivar to 290
per US$ in December 1995. However, Venezuela's economy continued to worsen during
the first quarter of 1996: non-oil activity remained stagnant and unemployment high
(about 11 percent); financial policies have been lax (the underlying fiscal deficit has been
about 4.5 percent of GDP); and pressures on the parallel exchange rate and prices
continued to intensify--by the end of the first quarter the parallel exchange rate
represented about twice the official exchange and monthly inflation was about 8 percent in
January-February and 6 percent in March.  To correct this un-sustainable economic
situation, the Government announced on April 15 the adoption of a major reform
program, developed in consultation with the IMNF.
1.5.  Policies. Reduction of inflation and re-establishment of confidence in the economy
are the two main objectives of the reform program. To achieve these objectives, key
policies of the program include: (i) elimination of almost all controls on foreign exchange
allocation; (ii) liberalization of the exchange and interest rates; (iii) increasing gasoline
prices---by five times on average; and (iv) increasing the wholesale tax rate from 12.5
percent to 16.5 percent.  In addition, key policies to restructure the financial sector
(including the development of a banking system reform program) and to protect the most
vulnerable groups (including an extended safety net program) are being considered. The
intention is to implement these programs as soon as possible within a feasible fiscal
program.
1.6.   Prospects. As a consequence of the program, the exchange rate increased initially
to about Bs 500 per US$ (from Bs 290 in the official market as indicated above) but has
decreased to about Bs 460-470 lately. Inflation remains high, but is expected to decrease
to about 1.5 percent monthly during the second half of 1996.  For the entire year,
however, inflation is projected to reach 80 to 90 percent, the fiscal deficit is expected to be
about 2 percent of GDP (consistent with limiting the domestic borrowing requirement
from the public sector) and the economy will likely contract (-1 . 1 percent GDP growth is
predicted). To support the exchange rate, interest rates have reached positive levels in
real terms, as expected. As a consequence of both the higher real rates and economic
contraction, the financial sector will likely deteriorate further. However, by developing
and implementing a coherent financial sector reform program, the Government expects to
achieve: (i) an orderly resolution of the banking crisis within a feasible fiscal program; and
(ii) in the medium term, the emergence of a strengthened financial system. At the same
time, the recently announced economic policies together with the implementation of a
sound fiscal and monetary policy are expected to lead to a more stable economy in the



3
medium term (inflation is expected to decrease from the 1996 peak) growing at an average
of 4-5 % over the 1997-2000 period.
1.7.  Impact on the Sector. As a result of the structural adjustment reforms and
macroeconomic situation, investment in the water sector has decreased' from 0.18% of
GDP in the 1990-1993 period to 0.05% in 1994. Similarly it decreased from an average
of about 5% of public sector investment prior to 1989 to 2.2% since. As expected, this
reduction has had a negative impact on service coverage and quality.
1.8.   Government Strategy. The need to reduce public spending has led the
Government to seek alternative means of providing services that had been traditionally
publicly funded and heavily centralized. Inefficient pricing policies, aborted attempts at
private sector involvement, and regulatory deficiencies have contributed to make public
utilities a growing drain on national government resources. The current administration has
given high importance to the water and sanitation sector because of the acute problems in
service delivery and the heavy fiscal burden of present centralized management. The
Government's policy for the sector, articulated in a letter to the Bank (Annex A), has as its
key elements: i) accelerating the decentralization process and building local institutional
capacity for management; ii) developing a modern institutional and regulatory framework;
iii) improving cost-recovery and achieving financial self-sufficiency; iv) increasing sector
investment to an average annual 4% of total public investment, concentrating on
rehabilitation, expanded sewerage and treatment coverage and greater access by the poor
to water supply and sanitation (WS&S) services; and v) improving efficiency and
involving the private sector in service delivery. The Govemment has requested World
Bank assistance in implementing the above reforms and supporting the first case of
decentralization to the state of Monagas. Project preparation work and initial actions for
institutional and regulatory reform at the national level and in the state of Monagas are
being supported through grants from the governments of Japan, Austria and Spain. The
Inter American Development Bank (IDB) is working closely with the Bank to support the
Government's sector reform efforts.
C. Sectoral Issues
1.9.   Overall Service Quality. With more than eighty percent of the urban population
receiving piped water supply, Venezuela has one of the highest coverage rates in Latin
America. It is also estimated that close to eighty percent of the rural population has
access to drinking water supply. This shows that the significant urban/rural disparities that
underlie water supply in many Latin American countries have been largely eliminated in
Venezuela. Coverage of sanitation is also high by Latin American standards, but is not as
extensive or even as that of water supply. More than sixty percent of the urban population
has connections to a sewerage system, in contrast with only forty percent of the rural
population.
See Programa de Ajuste del Sector de Agua Potable y Saneamiento, prepared by HIDROVEN in
September of 1994.



4
1. 10. Despite relatively good overall coverage rates, the quality of WS&S services in
Venezuela is poor, and the sector has largely failed to provide reliable and efficient
service: water supply is characterized by widespread recurring rationing during the dry
season months (however, the sector delivers 2,800 million cubic meters of water annually,
which should be sufficient to cover the national demand), low pressures, questionable
water quality, high unaccounted-for-water and inadequate customer service. Wastewaters
are generally not treated and most sewage is discharged raw into the receiving waters.
1.11. The centralized management of water and sanitation services and the treatment of
these services as a public good have resulted in very low cost recovery rates. As the
national and state governments have had to reduce their budgets in order to bring the
macroeconomic crisis under control, funding for the sector has been dramatically curtailed.
The reduction of funds has impacted on system maintenance and investment, and this in
turn has resulted in water rationing and deteriorating water quality.  Although no
significant increases in gastro-intestinal diseases have been recorded yet at the national
level, the deficient water and sewerage service is regarded as a public health problem in
states such as Monagas, where all health districts have reported increases in the incidence
of water-borne diseases.
1.12.  Sector Entities, Institutions and Regulation. The structural adjustment reforms
initiated in 1989 included the restructuring of water supply services by abolishing the
Instituto Nacional de Obras Sanitarias (INOS), decentralizing the sector to regional
agencies and establishing adequate cost-recovery policies for the sector. A new national
institution, responsible for the implementation of policy and the coordination of the sector,
C.A. Hidrol6gica Venezolana (HIDROVEN), was created in 1991. While INOS went
into liquidation, 10 regional water companies (HIiDROS) began to operate. INOS was
formally abolished in 1993, and its liquidation process is currently in its last stages.
Despite the HIDROS regional character, WS&S services remain de facto highly
centralized with a complex organizational structure. The main sector entities are:
* the Ministry of the Environment and Renewable Resources (MARNR),
responsible for resources management, bulk-production of potable water,
setting policy and regulating tariffs;
a the Ministry of Development (Ministerio de Fomento), responsible for
regulating tariffs (jointly with MARNR);
*  the Ministry of Health and Social Assistance (MSAS), responsible for setting
drinking water quality standards;
*  the Municipalities, which  hold  constitutional responsibility  for water
distribution and sewerage services, although only few actually perform this
function;
*  HIDROVEN, the national water corporation (100% of the shares are owned



5
by MARNR), responsible for translating sector policy into operational plans,
establishing criteria and directives for the operation, maintenance, expansion
and reconstruction of the systems and subsequently supervising compliance,
and coordinating activities in the sector;
*  the  regional water companies, HIDROS, fully-owned  subsidiaries of
HIDROVEN, which are responsible for the provision of service in one or more
states. The distribution of states among the ten HIDROS is detailed in the
table 1.1. (see also Map 1);
Table 1.1. Distribution of States among the HIDROS
HIDROS                 States under their Jurisdiction
HIDROCARIBE            Anzoategui, Monagas, Sucre and Nueva Esparta
HIDROFALCON            Falcon
HIDROCAPITAL           Distrito Federal (Caracas) and Miranda
HIDROANDES             Merida, Trujillo and part of Bannas
HIDROCENTRO            Aragua, Carabobo and Cojedes
HIDROCCIDENTAL         Lara, Yaracuy and Portuguesa
HIDROLAGO              Zulia
HIDROSUROESTE          Tachira, Municipality of Zamora and part of Barinas
HIDROPAEZ              Guarico
HIDROLLANOS            Apure
*  the Corporaci6n Venezolana de Guyana (CVG), a state-owned oil, gas and
mining company, which is responsible for providing WS&S services in the
states of Bolivar, Amazonas and Amacuro; and
*  the state governments, which typically manage the rural systems in the
corresponding state.
1.13. Although sector policy formulation is the responsibility of the MARNR, this
function is normally exercised through HIDROVEN.  In practice, the sector has
functioned without clear policies and strategies. With the notable exception of potable
water quality, there have been no quality standards or performnance indicators for the
different government-owned companies that operate under the umbrella of HIDROVEN.
Moreover, there is neither an independent regulatory agency, nor a transparent and formal
regulatory framework.
1.14. Pricing Policy and Cost Recovery. On March 1, 1993, the Government issued a
Ministerial Resolution, introducing significant increases in water prices and a new tariff
structure. The Government's objective was to increase the revenues of water companies



6
to achieve financial self-sufficiency (an increment of 300 to 800 % was expected), and, on
this respect, this new Resolution represented a serious effort to adjust prices to costs.
1.15. Some of the new Resolution's salient features are that it:
* imposes increasing block tariffs;
* defines 7 users categories (" residential", "residential social", "commercial",
"industrial A", " industrial B", " bulk" and " trunk delivered" ), minimum
consumption to be billed for un-metered users and six reference tariff levels2;
*  sets the water consumption (15 m3/month) to be billed to the residencial social
consumers and limits charges to them to less than 3% of the minimum salary;
.  establishes an automatic correction system  to adjust tariffs based on price
variations and on the contribution to the total cost of the system from labor,
chemicals, energy and maintenance; and
o  introduces charges for sewerage and wastewater treatment as a proportion of
the water tariff, although at an unrealistically low level (10% of water tariffs for
each sewerage and wastewater treatment).
1. 16. While this national pricing policy states that tariffs must cover operation,
maintenance, depreciation and investment, and while it incorporates an automatic
adjustment clause, it has not been applied by the local agencies of the regional water
companies. This is mainly due to the lack of incentives for the local agencies to apply a
tariff at the upper end of the set, or to bill or collect, since: (i) all collections are managed
centrally by each regional water company; (ii) deficits are substantially covered through
transfers from the national government; and (iii) local agencies operate on the basis of
monthly budgetary allocations. In addition, these agencies are subject to local political
interference and are reluctant to charge higher tariffs when the service is deteriorated.
This leads to a vicious circle by which they cannot recover the costs of improving the
system, which deteriorates further.
1. 17. As a result, overall cost-recovery remains seriously deficient, and all the regional
companies incurred operational deficits in 1994 (currently, tariffs cover only about 40% of
operational costs). The sector has financed itself largely through non-payment of its
suppliers and contractors, namely the power subsector with which it accumulated a debt of
Bs 21,800 million in the 1991-1993 period. HIDROVEN has estimated that if the sector
were to cover its operational costs, including minor maintenance, but excluding
depreciation, 1994 tariffs would have to increase two to threefold in real value. Clearly,
2  For each system, the corresponding regional water company chooses which reference tariff level to
apply, depending on their perception of customer reaction and estimated operation and maintenance
(O&M) costs (no rules on how to do this selection are provided though).



7
tariffs would have to rise even more if they are to cover depreciation, debt service and a
reasonable percentage of the investment program.
1. 18. Rural Water Systems. Over the past 30 years, the management of rural water
services has undergone significant institutional and organizational transformations, with
varying degrees of decentralized and community-based management practices. In the
early 1 960s, the Ministry of Health and Social Assistance (MSAS) took responsibility for
water supply in communities with fewer than 5,000 residents. Over ten years, MSAS built
approximately 4,000 new rural water systems and established a management system
involving elected water committees in each town, payment for water services, and paid
community labor for constructing new systems. These committees drew on internal
community peer pressure and community trust to collect fees and organize group efforts
for routine repairs and maintenance. More technically complex O&M was carried out by
the zone supervising engineer and his/her staff, with support from the central MSAS's
O&M office in Maracay. Towards the end of the 1960s, federal transfers to cities and
towns became more common because of oil revenues, and MSAS stopped charging for
services. This brought about the decline of the water committee structure, new system
construction slowed and cost recovery measures at the local level ceased.
1.19. In the mid 1970s MSAS systems serving between 1,000 and 5,000 residents were
transferred to INOS which contracted out the O&M at the state level. In 1980, INOS
began charging symbolic tariffs in some states, but collections were usually interrupted
during periods preceding elections, which reflected the political vulnerability of the system.
INOS did not charge for rural WS&S services in the state of Monagas.
1.20. During the 1980s, systems managed by INOS and MSAS began to deteriorate.
Federal transfers to MSAS decreased and INOS' overriding focus on new construction to
the detriment of operation and maintenance led to the deterioration of the existing
infrastructure and contracting arrangements. In 1991, MSAS transferred all its systems to
State Public Works departments, and INOS to the regional HIDROS. In many cases, the
deficient level of rural water services provided by those two entities in each state has been
compensated by activist municipal governments, especially since mayors began being
elected, instead of appointed, in 1989. Still, rural WS&S service levels remain very
uneven across the country, and billing for those services is virtually non-existent.
1.21. Water Resources Management. Although Venezuela lies entirely in the tropics,
there is considerable variation in climate due principally to differences in elevation and
topography. Average annual rainfall ranges from less than 500 mm in the north Caribbean
coast to more than 4,000 mm in parts of the Orinoco delta and the eastern part of the
Cordillera de Merida. Humidity is generally high with average values exceeding 80% in
the Amazon, eastern planes (Ilanos) and the Maracaibo lowlands. Annual average
temperatures range from 24 °C at elevations below 800 m to about 0 °C at elevations over
3,000 m. The Orinoco river system drains some 80% of the national territory.



8
1.22. The management of water resources in Venezuela is centralized in the MARNR,
which formulates policies, sets water quality standards and controls most water-related
infrastructure (particularly water supply and sanitation investments) as well as their
operation and maintenance. Several other national, state and local agencies are also
involved in water resources management, but these operate without coordination, a
comprehensive management approach or a clear policy or strategy for integration between
them. As a result, although water policy formulation is technically the responsibility of the
MARNR, the Ministry of Agriculture and Breeding (Ministerio de Agricultura y Cria,
MAC) separately plans, constructs and manages irrigation. Similarly, although water
quality standards are established at the national level by the MARNR according to the type
of water body, MSAS sets water standards whenever issues related to the protection of
public health are involved. With respect to water supply and sanitation, some of the
MARNR's centralized control responsibilities have been delegated to HIDROVEN when
sector restructuring and decentralization began in 1990.
1.23. As part of the sector restructuring initiative, the government of Venezuela created
two river basin commissions in 1993 to decentralize and coordinate water resources
management in the Tuy River and Lake Valencia. However, these have failed to meet the
objectives set for them: they have not achieved sustainability and they have remained fully
financially dependent on the MARNR. The commissions were created following a new
institutional construct to address problems of water and the environment with a more
decentralized approach, where public participation was presented as a key component of
the institutional framework (public meetings were indeed held in the basins to involve
stakeholders in some planning decisions).  However, the decision-making has been
maintained at the national level in the MARNR. Moreover, the basin's stakeholders have
not been properly involved in their management and have remain un-bound to financial
requirements or decisions.
1.24. Project Focus on Sectoral Issues at the National Level. To address the issues
described above, the proposed project contains a national institutional component (para
3.10) which will support the Government's efforts to develop a new institutional and
regulatory framework for the sector, create and/or strengthen sector national institutions
and finance studies, equipment and consultant services to develop and implement new
policies on shared water resources management and related infrastructure, rural water
systems management, and private sector participation in water services delivery.
D. Rationale for Bank Involvement
1.25. Past Bank involvement in Venezuela. Between 1961 and 1992, the Bank
approved 23 loans to Venezuela, totaling US$ 2,425.2 million. The FY 1992 lending
program amounted to US$ 329 million. The Bank reduced lending to a " core" program
of no more than US$ 200 million/year for FY94-95, owing to a deterioration in the
macroeconomic framework, with the proviso that lending could only be increased should a
number of macroeconomic conditions be met. The core program will target human



9
resources, institutional stref-thening, and infrastructure rehabilitation with emphasis on
poverty alleviation and privatization. The project focuses on these target interventions.
1.26. Bank Strategy. According to the last Country Assistance Strategy presented to
the Executive Directors on November 4, 19933, the Bank has five strategic objectives in
Venezuela: (a) advance the structural reform process and improve macroeconomic
management; (b) strengthen human resource development and poverty alleviation
programs; (c) strengthen government institutions at the central and local levels, including
project preparation and implementation capacity; (d) reform and rehabilitate key
infrastructure sectors; and (e) promote privatization and private sector development.
Efforts in the infrastructure sectors consistent with these objectives seek to: (a) strengthen
the ministerial, state and local units charged with designing and implementing strategies
and programs; (b) deregulate and reform pricing and cost-recovery mechanisms (including
independent regulatory bodies) for publicly supplied goods and services; (c) implement
maintenance and rehabilitation programs; and (d) make progress on decentralization,
restructuring, and privatization.  The project is fully consistent with the Country
Assistance Strategy and will contribute to the achievement of these strategic objectives
and efforts.
1.27. The Bank's last strategy for the WS&S sector, elaborated in the report Venezuela:
Strategy Paper for the Infrastructure Sectors (Report No. 11605-VE, 1993), remains
valid. The strategy focuses on supporting the Government's efforts to change the role of
the public sector and to create a regulatory framework that ensures effective competition.
A major goal for the operating agencies will be improved technical, administrative,
commercial, and financial efficiency with increased participation, where feasible, of the
private sector. The Bank's assistance to the water sector in Venezuela is envisaged
through three lending operations: (a) the proposed project, designed to help initiate the
sector decentralization in Monagas, serve as a demonstration model for other state water
companies, and assist HIDROVEN in further defining its role as the institution that will
lead policy changes at the national level and support the preparation of similar projects in
other states; (b) a project to support the rehabilitation and institutional modernization of
WS&S services for Caracas; and (c) a sector-wide operation to replicate in other states
the model developed in Monagas and address growing water pollution problems,
particularly in urban areas. These operations would be supported by previous and ongoing
sector analysis, and by experience gained under this project.
3  A new Country Assistance Strategy is under preparation, which will reflect recent agreements
between the Government and the IMEF on macroeconomic policy, as well as the continued high
priority of decentralization, improved management of public services through private sector
participation and strengthened cost-recovery, and better access to these services by the poor under
participatory management schemes.



10
Reformulation Loan (PERL) (Ln 3223-VE) supported the Government's plans to initiate
sector decentralization by abolishing INOS and creating the regional water companies.
The project had mixed results, partly due to the volatile political environment which
predominated in 1992-93, and partly because the strategy of enterprise reform rather than
sector reform left some important issues unresolved: in the case of the water sector, the
project did not put in place a strengthened regulatory framework or improved prices. This
had direct consequences for the privatization of the Caracas water system, which the
PERL had set out to support. A combination of factors, including an unfavorable
regulatory framework, a lack of local political commitment and consensus on objectives,
and excessively low existing tariffs, caused the concessioning process to abort. Although
the bid was unsuccessful, the basic preparatory work is still relevant for any future reform
and private sector involvement in the Caracas WS&S system and in the sector as a whole.
1.29. This experience, and a review of sector experience in the LAC Region and Bank-
wide (Annex B), suggest that there are essential requirements for efficient and effective
WS&S services. They include:
*  an adequate regulatory framework and, linked to it, appropriate tariff levels
and billing and collection mechanisms to generate the funds needed for
financial autonomy and to signal the real cost of water to the consumers. The
projects intends to develop these instruments through the national institutional
component (para 3.10);
*  efficient and cost-conscious management to avoid unnecessary expenditures,
particularly in excess personnel. Decentralizing the services now provided by
HIDROCARIBE and contracting the management of the services to an
experienced private operator should achieve this requirement,
* reversing the preference of decision-makers for new capital expenditure over
proper operation and maintenance. The project's physical works concentrate
on rehabilitation, the minimization of physical and commercial water losses and
the correction of deferred maintenance as a means of postponing new
investment and improving efficiency; and
*  user involvement in the setting of priorities and, where possible, the design of
subprojects, to improve ownership and cost-sharing of operation and
maintenance functions.  The project preparation has included thorough
stakeholder consultation, and taken into account demand-based, participatory
models already tested in Colombia, Ecuador, Bolivia and Indonesia.



11
II. THE WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
A. Introduction and Overview
2.1.  The state of Monagas, located in the north-east of Venezuela, covers an area of
28,900 KM2, which represents 3.17% of the national territory.  In 1994, it had a
population of approximately 540,000, about 2.6% of the country's population. It is
divided into thirteen municipalities: Acosta, Bolivar, Caripe, Cedeflo, Ezequiel Zamora,
Libertador, Maturin, Piar, Punceres, Sotillo, Aguasay, Santa Barbara, and Uracoa. The
largest municipality, Maturin, is the state's capital, has approximately 65% of the state's
population and has been growing at a high rate of over 6.7% per annum in the last ten
years due to the activity of the petroleum industry. Other municipalities (Ezequiel
Zamora, Libertador and Sotillo) are also estimated to grow at between 2 and 5 percent per
year while the rural population has been decreasing at about 1-3 percent yearly. Roughly
15% (about 80,000 people) of the Monagas population live in rural areas. The state's
economy is based on: (i) the petroleum industry, which produces 800,000 barrels of oil
and 1,200 million cubic feet of gas daily; (ii) agriculture, with the production of corn,
sorghum, soya, caraotas (black beans), pineapple, mango, bananas, oil palm, yuca,
coffee, cocoa, and sugar cane; and (iii) cattle rearing.
2.2.  There is some degree of community organization in the state of Monagas, and this
provides a favorable vehicle for community and user participation in project design,
operation and supervision. Each municipality is organized into parishes, and these in turn
generally have several neighborhood associations. The Parish Council members are
elected by popular vote in a process similar to the election of the municipal governments.
The neighborhood associations are formed spontaneously and their members are chosen
in accordance with the by-laws of the association. There are 34 parishes in Monagas, in
addition to the 13 municipalities. The Parish Council supports the Municipal Council in
the administration of the municipality. The neighborhood associations do not have
regulated functions; they seek to promote the interests of each neighborhood.
B. Organization of the Sector in Monagas
2.3.   Introduction.  Until 1995, HIDROCARIBE provided water and sanitation
services in most urban areas of the state and in 22 communities designated as rural. The
state government is responsible for service in an additional 190 rural communities.
HIDROCARIBE also served 3 other states (Nueva Esparta, Anzoategui, and Sucre)
through separate management and accounting-centers for each state. The one operating
in Monagas was known as HIDROCARIBE-Monagas, and although it had well-defined
functions and responsibilities, it had no financial or administrative autonomy.  In



12
particular, it did not manage the funds that it collected, as these were transferred to
HIDROCARIBE's head office located in Puerto La Cruz in the state of Anzoategui.
2.4.  Service Quality and Coverage. It is estimated by HIDROVEN that in 1994,
93% of the urban population of Monagas had access to piped water supply, while 59%
had access to sewerage services. Coverage varied from a high of 95% in Maturin to a low
of 60% in Caripe, for water, and a high of 80% in Temblador and Barrancas to a low of
40% in Caripe, for sewerage. Water quality is deficient, and the incidence of water-
related diseases treated in local hospitals is quite large. In addition, sewage is mainly
discharged untreated into the rivers, although there are some small, poorly maintained
oxidation lagoons and some small, privately-owned treatment plants. Managerial and
system inefficiencies have combined to produce very high unaccounted-for water levels,
estimated by HIDROVEN at roughly 70% of the water produced. Moreover, significant
rationing is normal during the dry months. The response to this situation has traditionally
consisted in drilling wells to increase water supply. Four wells, drilled by a petroleum
company and subsequently abandoned, have recently been reconditioned to add some 150
lps (liters per second). Similarly, seven additional wells are being drilled with the
assistance of CORPOVEN - one of the three government-owned oil companies - and are
expected to add some 210 lps before the dry season of 1996.
2.5.  Operating  Practices  and  Commercial and  Financial Performance.
HIDROCARIBE, as most regional water companies have done since their creation, relied
on private sector companies (operadoras) to provide the service and operate and maintain
the system, while undertaking directly all commercial and administrative functions.
Because the contracts used to hire the operadoras were of short duration and were not
performance-based, they became "force-account" contracts, whereby a private party
provides personnel and equipment at a given rate, with no responsibility for cost or
performance. To further complicate matters, the financial situation of the sector did not
allow HIDROCARIBE to pay the operadoras on time. As a result, the operadoras were
not complying fully with their contractual responsibilities.
2.6.  The contracts with the operadoras allowed HIDROCARIBE to reduce its number
of employees and as a result, in 1994 HIDROCARIBE and HIDROCARIBE-Monagas
employed only 597 and 75 persons, between administrators and technical staff,
respectively. However, if the personnel provided by the operadoras is added, the number
of employees increased to 2,019 and 250, respectively, which led to 9 and 6 employees
per 1,000 customers, a figure higher than what is common in well-run utilities'.
2.7.  HIDROCARIBE-Monagas had an unsatisfactory financial performance since it
began operating in 1991. The main reasons for this bad performance were: (i) inadequate
tariffs and lack of financial resources that resulted in excessive dependence on transfers
from HIDROCARIBE; (ii) very high unaccounted-for water levels, inadequate billing and
The number of employees per 1,000 connections is 3.6 in Bogota, 2.1 in Santiago 2.9 in average in the
United States and as low as 0.8 in Alicante (Spain).



13
collections practices and inappropriate commercial policies; (iii) inconsistent accounting
practices; (iv) lack of clearly defined sectoral and operational objectives and
responsibilities; (v) deficient planning and control; (vi) lack of corporate independence,
managerial autonomy and accountability; (vii) insufficient qualified management and
technical staff, inadequate salary levels and excessive staffing in low level echelons; (viii)
insufficient maintenance of the infrastructure in operation, that has resulted in rationing,
averaging 4.5 days per week during the dry season, and (ix) inefficient procurement of the
operadoras' services.
2.8.  In 1994, HIDROCARIBE-Monagas served an estimated 60,900 connections, of
which 41,658 figured in its roster as clients (additionally, the state government supplied
about 12,000 connections in the urban areas, mainly in Maturin). These were billed a
total of Bs 330 million and of those, Bs 230 million were effectively collected. The
company's operation and maintenance expenditure was Bs 630 million, of which Bs 245
million were not paid, mostly for electricity supply and services provided by the
operadoras, that were added to a growing figure of payable accounts. Since there was in
effect no policy for disconnecting clients for non-payment of the service, and no
incentives to introduce adequate cost management procedures, the financial situation
would have continued to be grossly inadequate in the absence of corrective measures.
2.9.  The available financial information reveals that the rate of return was -25% for
1993 and -14% for 1994. The key standard indicators (operational, cash-flow and
balance sheet) also show HIDROCARIBE-Monagas' meager performance. In this period
gross revenues were insufficient to fund cash expenses, demanding a Government
subsidy of Bs 250 million in 1993 and Bs 490 million in 1994 to finance the operating
deficit. Central Government subsidies for new investments were also needed during the
period, and they reached Bs 870 and 892 million in 1993 and 1994.
2.10. Local Reforms in the Sector. In the context of the proposed project, authorities at
the state and municipal level in Monagas, supported by HIDROVEN, initiated steps to
improve the service and further decentralize it. The Ley Orgdnica de Regimen Municipal
includes water and sanitation services as part of the municipalities' duties and
responsibilities. To this end, the Mayors of the ten municipalities in the state created on
September 21, 1992 the Mancomunidad Monaguense de Acueductos (Mancomunidad), in
essence an association of municipalities, to deal with WS&S services management and
operation in the state. On October 26, 1993, the state government and the Mancomunidad
created Aguas de Monagas (AdM), to act as the responsible operating company for the
services in substitution of HIDROCARIBE in the state of Monagas. The capital structure of
AdM is public and comprises a 51% equity share from the Mancomunidad, with the
remainder held by the state government. Under this structure, O&M as well as management
fimctions will be contracted to the private sector, while AdM staffing is limited to the core
management which is needed for support to the Board of Directors and for project
implementation. Two members of AdM's five person Board of Directors are appointed
by the Governor and represent the state and the other three represent the municipalities.
At the request of the Governor and the Mancomunidad, CORPOVEN is assisting AdM by



14
2.11. The project will assist AdM, through a regional component (para 3.9 a and b), in
facing several key challenges: (i) urgently rehabilitating physical infrastructure that has
been seriously deteriorated due to neglected maintenance, in order to provide reliable
service and improve water quality; and (ii) strengthening its institutional and comnercial
capacity, introducing metering and involving the private sector in service management
through a management contract that will be structured with the appropriate incentives for
improved service delivery and cost-recovery. For the latter objective, AdM, with the help
of the private operator, should tap its potential revenue stream and increase revenue from
the very modest US$ 1.1 million in 1994 (about US$ 15 per connection per year), through
better billing and collection procedures, reduction of the present high unaccounted-for-
water level and adequate tariff adjustments.
2.12. Metering, Billing and Collection. AdM's main problems at this point appear to
be the high unaccounted-for-water levels, billing deficiencies and accounts receivable
inherited from HLDROCARIBE. In 1994, it was estimated that unaccounted-for water
reached 70% and was due primarily to: (i) the accumulated effects of inadequate
maintenance practices; (ii) unmetered customers and damaged meters (of a total of about
41,000 clients, only 1,200 (1.5%) have meters working properly), and (iii) the large
number of users, estimated to be about 30,000, who do not appear in the client roster and
therefore are never billed. Information provided by HIDROCARIBE-Monagas indicates
that in 1993 and 1994 collections were 34% and 56% of the amounts billed, respectively.
Although this shows some improvement, much remains to be done in this area. This
positive trend is continuing, as AdM has been able to double the amount collected since it
took over commercial operations from HIDROCARIBE in July 1995, in the context of
project preparation.
2.13. Tariff Levels. In 1994, HlDROCARIBE-Monagas' residential reference tariff
was Bs 17.5 per cubic meter of water for residential consumers (Bs 7.5/m3 for residencial
social), an extremely low figure by any standard. Since there are only a few metered
connections, consumption levels are estimated and the average revenue is therefore
unreliable and must be even lower. In practice, HIDROCARIBE-Monagas chooses which
tariffs to apply from the six tariff levels included in the current Ministerial Resolution on
tariffs, normally after they have been negotiated with the Mayors. Tariff policy is erratic at
best, and non-existent at worst. It is expected that AdM will require financial support
from the municipal and state governments through 1998, until unaccounted-for water
levels, billing and accounts receivable and tariffs are brought in line with sound utility
practice. During project preparation, tariffs have already been raised from 17.5 Bs/m3 to
40 Bs/m3 to reflect the cost of providing the service, and they will be further increased to
60 Bs/m3 before project effectiveness (para 5.1 a and 5.3 b). Additional adjustments due
to the recent devaluation and continuing high inflation are incorporated in the company's
financial plan, developed during project preparation. For this analysis, a cost-structure
similar to that of HIDROCARIBE has been assumed, with increments in the cost of labor,
energy, chemicals and operation and maintenance contracts determined by the year's
internal inflation. These increments have been used for the calculation of the reference



15
incorporated in the company's financial plan, developed during project preparation. For
this analysis, a cost-structure similar to that of HIDROCARIBE has been assumed, with
increments in the cost of labor, energy, chemicals and operation and maintenance
contracts determined by the year's internal inflation. These increments have been used
for the calculation of the reference residential tariff adjustments, in application of the
current ministerial resolution that regulates this matter. It is expected, however, that
improved management practices provided through the private operator would result in
more efficient Operation and Maintenance contracts.
2.14. Rural Systems. Rural WS&S systems in the state of Monagas are managed
either by the state government (generally the smaller systems, usually run by individually
contracted operators who are poorly trained), or HIDROCARIBE (larger systems, serving
about 800 households on average each), which contracts out the operation of the rural
systems to five operating companies, each covering a separate region of the state. Rural
water supply coverage is high, but the reliability of service is deficient (generally
intermittent and with prolonged periods of interruption). Moreover, most systems do not
treat water for potability and water quality varies widely among rural communities,
leading to adverse health impacts. These conditions stem from several critical problems:
i) inadequate supervision of operators, due to the isolation of rural systems from the
central management unit in Maturin; ii) little or no water treatment or water quality
monitoring; iii) poorly designed wells and/or water systems; iv) insufficient water
supplies;  vii) insufficient funding and lack of timely payment for repairs and
maintenance; and viii) lack of involvement of rural communities with the system's O&M
and planning. However, discussions held with rural communities representatives during
project preparation have indicated that communities would be interested in a scheme
involving them more in the management of their WS&S services.
2.15. The project will assist AdM and the municipalities to address the current problems
affecting the rural WS&S systems and channel the interest of the communities to get
involved in the management of their WS&S services. To this end, the project's regional
component includes a sub-component (para 3.9 c) to develop a pilot Rural Rehabilitation
and Participatory Management Program (Programa Participativo de Mejoramiento de
Acueductos Rurales - PROPAMAR), to:  (i) provide financial support for the
improvement of these systems; (ii) create management capacity at the community level;
and (iii) pilot decentralized and participatory management of rural water systems in the
state of Monagas and test policies and procedures that could be replicated in the rest of
the country.
2.16. Water Resources Management. In Monagas, several important problems of
water quantity and quality are being compounded by the combination of a growing
pressure on water resources and the divided responsibilities among managing institutions,
limited regulatory structure, lack of adequate institutional capacity and weak financial
incentives to implement water resources planning and management. Although the
creation of AdM is a step towards providing a more reliable and efficient municipal water
service, it does not addresses the allocation and protection of water resources in the state,



16
that is highly centralized in the MARNR. To tackle this problem and develop policies
and experience that could be replicated in other parts of the country, the project's national
institutional component will support the development and introduction of integrated water
resource management in the state of Monagas and the creation of a decentralized
watershed management unit for the Amana and Guarapiche rivers, with significant
involvement of the basin's stakeholders (para 3.10 a).
2.17. Legal and Regulatory Issues. During project preparation, the Government, with
the assistance of the Bank, developed a series of contract and agreements to address the
shortcomings of the current regulatory, legal and institutional framework of the sector and
solve legal issues that could represent constraints for project implementation and for the
proper operation of AdM, mainly those related to the decentralization of the service to the
municipalities. These contracts and agreements (para 3.14) will complement the current
regulatory and institutional framework in an interim basis, until a new, more developed,
framework is in place. The legal issues included: (i) AdM's legal status; (ii) the
assignment of the responsibility for the provision of WS&S services and the production
of water - the latter a national government responsibility;  (iii) the authority and
procedures for setting and adjusting tariffs; and (iv) the mechanisms to channel funds for
project implementation.
2.18. These contracts and agreements define and regulate the relationships among the
Government of Venezuela, MARNR, HIDROCARIBE, the State of Monagas
(Gobernaci6n), the Municipalities and AdM. At the same time, these arrangements will
serve as a demonstration and test exercise to help define requirements of the new
regulatory framework under preparation. Annex C describes in detail each of these issues
and the resulting legal agreements that will enable the Govermment to move forward with
this demonstration decentralization project.



17
III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Origin
3.1.   At the national level, the project originates from the WS&S decentralization and
restructuring policy expressed in the Government's overall sector strategy. This is based
on the concept of separation of the ownership, operation and regulatory functions and on
the involvement of stakeholders in the planning and decision activities. It is also based on
fostering the efficient participation of private operators in the sector by providing a
transparent and equitable regulatory framework and developing experiences and policies
that could facilitate this participation. These principles have been fully embraced by
HIDROVEN and by the regional and local authorities of the state of Monagas.
3.2.  At the level of the state of Monagas, the project origin can be traced to the demand
of state and local authorities for the decentralization of sector management and for the
transfer of WS&S services to the municipalities, with the creation of AdM and the transfer
of responsibilities from HIDROCARIBE to AdM. This arrangement has been very
effective and has helped to create a decisive consensus among involved stakeholders, users
and institutions.
3.3.   The state of Monagas was selected to put in place the first effective
decentralization of the WS&S services and to introduce efficiency reforms aimed at
achieving improved service delivery and financial self sufficiency, under a demonstration
project. Monagas was chosen because: (i) there is a high degree of political commnitment
by the state government and municipalities to decentralization; (ii) WS&S services in the
state are in practice contained within the states borders; (iii) these services have a large
potential for improvement and the demand and willingness to pay for them among the
population seemed sufficient to warrant support for the process; and (iv) most important
rivers for WS&S start and discharge into the sea within the state, making feasible and
coherent with the decentralization approach of the project, the solution of problems
related to the management and allocation of water resources. Project identification was
initiated in January 1993 but significant project preparation activities only began in January
1995 after the approval of the Japanese Policy and Human Resources Development grant
and additional support from the Austrian and Spanish governments was secured.
3.4.   Participatory approach. During project preparation, two formal workshops and
several other informal meetings were held with project stakeholders, including
representatives from the Governor's office, the Municipalities, HIDROVEN, AdM, the
Juntas Parroquiales, industry and commerce associations, and local consulting firms
involved in the project. In those workshops, stakeholders endorsed the project concept
and its design. In particular, they supported the need for a Contract Plan among AdM and
its stockholders to spell out targets and responsibilities and for a performance-based
management contract for AdM  with a private operator.  They also endorsed the



18
conditionality to be included in the operation to ensure its success and showed their
understanding for the need to improve commercial practices and undertake tariff
adjustments.
3.5.   Two willingness to pay studies were also carried out, one among households in
Maturin and another among rural communities. These were preceded by meetings with
focus groups, each with ten to twenty participants from different neighborhoods
corresponding to different socioeconomic backgrounds. These meetings were aimed at
collecting preliminary information on users' opinions and level of satisfaction with the
service quality, alternative water supply sources (e.g. private back-up arrangements),
billing and collection procedures and amounts spent by household on water and other
public services. Participants were also given the opportunity to react to the proposed
institutional and service changes planned under the project and to trial tariffs which could
be charged under the improved scheme. These studies and the meetings with the focus
groups have proven essential to: (i) measure and promote demand for the project among
the population, (ii) identify the main elements used to define the project design; (iii)
establish support among local policy-makers for a more rigorous approach to WS&S
services management that relies on user's contribution;  (iv) ratify tariff increase
recommendations resulting from the financial analyses carried out during project
preparation; and (v) determine rural communities' valuation of and willingness to share
investment and O&M costs for improved water systems. These studies also added much
needed light on customer concerns over current service and on preferences and priorities
for the future improvements to the system, thereby providing policy-makers and the
project preparation team with a clear sense of the priorities to be addressed.
3.6.   Additionally, local and national NGOs have been consulted about the project and,
as a result of these consultations, NGOs will be incorporated in the project execution, both
as facilitators of the actions and investments in rural areas and as part of the independent
evaluators of the project success and of the degree of achievement of its objectives.
B. Project Objectives
3.7.   The project's principal objective is to increase the efficiency and quality of WS&S
services in Venezuela through the development of a model for decentralization,
institutional strengthening, and expanded private sector participation. The project has
three specific objectives: (a) assist in the strengthening of AdM, develop its financial self-
sufficiency, and involve the private sector in the management and operation of the
company; (b) rehabilitate and improve the quality of WS&S services in Monagas; and (c)
support sector development and decentralization at the national level, including water
resources management. As a demonstration operation, the project will implement sector
reforms in the state of Monagas that could be replicated in other states and will strengthen
HIDROVEN's capacity to provide assistance to that effect. The project was selected over
other alternatives which would have attempted reform of the sector more rapidly and
broadly, because the Government wants to test innovative approaches on a small scale,



19
and because state and local authorities in Monagas are highly motivated to improve water
utility performance.
C. Project Description
3.8.   The project includes two components to finance:  (I) regional institutional
strengthening and investment in rehabilitating the existing WS&S systems in the State of
Monagas to improve quality and efficiency of service delivery;  and (2) national
institutional strengthening. A summarized description of each of these components is
presented below. A more detailed description is included in Annexes E through I.
The Regional Component
3.9.   This component seeks to strengthen AdM  and rehabilitate the existing WS&S
infrastructure. It would include the following:
(a)   Institutional strengthening and support to AdM. This would finance: (i)
contracting of management consultant services with a private operator (see annex
E);  (ii) equipment, consultant services and systems to improve commercial
practices, supervise the management contract, promote private sector participation
(including assistance in the bidding process for a concession or lease contract), and
execution of a public information campaign, a survey of customer satisfaction and
the use of a local NGO for the evaluation of the project success;  and (iii)
engineering consultant services to prepare an improvement and expansion plan for
the water and sewerage systems, including the final design of the rehabilitation
works to be financed under this project and the design of a preventive maintenance
program.
(b)   Rehabilitation in urban areas. This would finance the rehabilitation of the
WS&S services in the urban areas and some expansion in marginal areas. The
selection of these investments has been based on least-cost criteria and on a set of
priorities and objectives agreed with AdM and its stakeholders during project
preparation (see Annex F for further details). Agreements were confirmed during
negotiations on the selection and grouping of the investments in urban areas into
three rehabilitation packages consisting of. (i) emergency works; (ii) priority
rehabilitation works for the first two years, to be conditioned on the signing of the
management contract with the private operator;  and (iii) works for the
improvement of service quality, to be conditioned on the achievement of Contract
Plan targets (para 5.2 b):
* The first rehabilitation package will finance emergency works required to
avoid the physical collapse of the urban water supply systems, correct the
principal water losses and water quality problems and help lessen acute water
shortages that have had an important negative impact on customers



20
satisfaction. To this end, the emergency investments will be concentrated in
the improvement and rehabilitation of the pumping systems, in the
interconnection of the networks, in the correction of the most important water
losses and-in the provision of basic and reliable disinfection. These investments
will be contracted and carried out before project effectiveness, following
procurement procedures acceptable to the Bank to be eligible for retroactive
financing. Engineering designs and bidding documents for these investments
have been prepared with funds provided by a grant from the Austrian
government and by the state government.
* The second rehabilitation package will finance priority rehabilitation works,
mainly focused on the reduction of unaccounted-for-water and improvements
of the system efficiency that are expected to have a great incidence in
customer's satisfaction, in the reliability of the service and in the financial
situation of AdM. This second package is expected to be completed within the
first two years of project execution, before the mid-term review.
* The third rehabilitation package will finance works for the improvement of
service quality and some expansion of coverage in marginal areas. It will
include the rehabilitation and improvement of potable water production
infrastructure, the rehabilitation and expansion of water distribution and
sewerage networks and the rehabilitation and improvement of existing
wastewater management infrastructure.
(c)   Institutional development and rehabilitation of rural water systems. To
support the development of the PROPAMAR, this subcomponent would finance
investments and technical assistance to improve rural WS&S services and create
management capacity at the community level. The investments will include civil
works, equipment and materials for the improvement and some expansion of these
systems to provide a basic level of service defined as an in-house connection for
every household, 150 liters per capita per day availability; and potable water.
Rural WS&S systems in approximately 85 communities covering about 45,000
inhabitants (about 50 % of the state's rural population) are expected to be financed
over four years under this subcomponent. The technical assistance will finance the
coordination and administration of the PROPAMAR by AdM, through the services
of a non-governmental organization (NGO)' which will: (i) carry out a campaign
to promote the proposed program; (ii) assist communities in organizing and
identifying their needs, preparing a proposal in the form of a Community Water
Action Plan (COWAP) and building up their capacity to take over the management
of their rural water system using commercial practices; and (iii) help Municipalities
build capacity to take over technical assistance to communities once the NGO
withdraws (see annex G for further details).
This includes the possibility of contracting the services of a consulting firm, since the contract will be
awarded through a competitive bidding process. All further references to "the NGO" in this
document should be understood to include a consulting firm as well.



21
The National Institutional Component
3.10.  The national institutional component will finance consultant services, equipment
and training to assist and promote the modernization of the Venezuelan water supply and
sanitation sector.  It will support:  (i) implementation of the legal and regulatory
framework proposal under preparation for the sector; (ii) creation and/or strengthening of
national sector institutions; (iii) increasing technical, managerial and monitoring capacity
at the national, as well as local level to regulate, plan and operate these services; (iv)
meetings, workshops and a public awareness campaign to stimulate a broad social support
for the modernization of WS&S and the rational use of the water resources; (v) preparing
training and dissemination materials; and (vi) facilitating the preparation of at least four
projects to duplicate the model developed in Monagas.  The national institutional
component will consist of four subcomponents, three to support the Technical Assistance
Program, National Training Program and Sector Decentralization Program, respectively,
and one to finance consultant support to HIDROVEN to manage the component and
overall project coordination and evaluation activities (see Annex H for further details):
(a)    The Technical Assistance Program  subcomponent will assist in the
implementation of the legal and regulatory framework which is currently under
design with support from a PHRD Grant from the Government of Japan and from
the Bank. It will finance consultant services and equipment for:
*  institutional strengthening of national sector institutions (HIDROVEN
and the regulatory agency or agencies to be defined), including: (i) a
study to define the organizational structure of the sector according to
the proposed Regulatory Framework; (ii) technical assistance; and (iii)
implementation of a management information system in HIDROVEN
and preparation of an inventory of basic sectoral information needed to
plan, regulate and provide technical assistance to sector institutions;
*  preparation of sectoral norms and standards; and
*  development and implementation of policies for: (i) management of
shared water resources and related infrastructure; (ii) management of
rural water systems and (iii) institutional organization and promotion of
private sector participation.
Additionally, this subcomponent will finance studies, equipment and related
consulting services to support the design, creation and functioning of a Users'
Hydrographic Confederation (CHU) for the management of water resources in the
Amana and Guarapiche river basins, in the state of Monagas, as a demonstration
case.  The design will be based on the idea that effective water resources
management is best achieved through an organization that:  (i) is given the
authority to establish and implement a water resources plan; (ii) meets the goal of
financial sustainability;  and (iii) is locally based, represents the users and



22
stakeholders in a river basin and responds to and is financially dependent on them.
Since the main role of the CHU will be to develop and implement management
criteria and principles to plan for the allocation and protection of water resources
in this area, this sub-component will also provide support to the CHU to develop a
basic information systems for these river basins, prepare a management plan and
set up the necessary institutional capacity to implement it (see Annex I for further
details). Preliminary work to design the CHU has been carried out with the help of
a grant from the Spanish government. Assurances have been obtained that a
reform proposal and implementation plan. satisfactory to the Bank, for the creation
of the Users' Hydrographic Confederation will be submitted to the Bank by July
31. 1997 and that such plan would be implemented in accordance with the agreed
terms (para 5.2 d).
(b)   The National Training Program  subcomponent will finance courses,
seminars and a public information campaign to: (i) increase technical capacity at
the national and local government level in order to promote the implementation of
a modern regulatory and institutional framework,  (ii) improve technical and
managerial skills of professionals and decision makers of the WS&S sector; and
(iii) stimulate appreciation and understanding among society of the importance of
the rational use and conservation of water resources and the proper commercial
management of WS&S services.
(c)   The Sector Decentralization Program  subcomponent will finance the
preparation of at least four new rehabilitation projects, replicating the model
developed in Monagas in other states or regional water companies. The Program
will include: (i) preparation of investment plans for the physical rehabilitation of
the systems; and (ii) technical, legal, institutional and financial studies necessary to
define the decentralization projects according to the model developed in Monagas.
(d)   The fourth subcomponent will finance consultant services and equipment to
assist HIDROVEN in legal and institutional development issues related to the
management of the national institutional component and overall project
coordination.  This will be provided through specialized consultants and a
consultant services contract that will include professional and support services and
related equipment needs. Submission of terms of reference for these consultant
services was condition of negotiations (para 5.1 h). Agreements were reached
during negotiations on the project management and monitoring capacity to be
retained in HIDROVEN and AdM (para 5.2 k). As a condition of disbursement
for the national institutional component. HIDROVEN will contract and retain as
part of its project implementation unit, consultants in the areas of financial analysis.
procurement. sanitary engineering, social communications and legal (para 5.4 e).

those regulate the implementation of the project. To regulate the operation of AdM six
contracts and agreements will be or have been signed by AdM (see Figure 3):

Agreement is a condition of loan effectiveness (para 5.3 a).
The Contract Plan
3.16. A Contract Plan will be signed between AdM, as one of the parties, and the state
government and the Mancomunidad as the other party. The Contract Plan will spell out
the responsibilities of each of the parties and will thus become the main reference tool for
both the state government and the Mancomunidad to evaluate management performance,
and for the Bank to monitor and supervise the project. The key performance indicators of
the Contract Plan, which will evolve with time, reflecting the expected improvements in
efficiency, will be used for the project's monitoring of AdM. These are summarized in
table 3.3. Agreements were reached during negotiations on the content of the Contract
Plan, particularly on the financial, physical and investment monitoring indicators and
targets (para 5.2 a).
3.17. For their part, the state government and the Mancomunidad would agree to the
following:
*  Timely provision of the counterpart funds needed for project implementation.;
*  Provision of funds to meet AdM's obligations until AdM  reaches financial self-
sufficiency;
*  A single tariff structure for the State of Monagas, and a tariff level consistent with
AdM's financial-self sufficiency goals;
*  Managerial autonomy, including personnel administration (hiring, firing, promotions
and salary levels);



27
. Full support of AdM's metering and billing goals and full support for AdM's
disconnection policies for non-payment of water service.
