3.18. The Contract Plan will have a duration of four years. To ensure compliance with
the Contract Plan, a supervisory committee will be appointed with three representatives:
one from the Governor's office, one from the Mancomunidad and one from AdM. The
Committee shall meet at least twice a year, and prior to the mid-term review, to propose
the changes that might be necessary to reach the original goals. Submission to the Bank of
a draft Contract Plan was condition of negotiations (para 5.1 e). As a condition of loan
effectiveness, the Contract Plan shall have been signed by AdM. the state of Monagas
government and all municipalities in the state of Monagas which have authorized to
operate under a signed service contract (this includes all municipalities, except Cedefio)



28
(para 5.3 a). As condition of disbursements for urban rehabilitation investments in Cedefio
or any new municipality, such municipality shall sign the Contract Plan (para 5.4 a).
Management Contract
3.19. AdM will contract a private sector operator to manage AdM for a period of four
years. The management contract is intended to provide AdM with a private operator's
management approach and with the experience and working methodology that are
required to confront the important transformation process initiated with the transfer of
responsibilities from H1DROCARIBE. It is also intended to complement and improve the
chances of success of the financial support that will be provided through the project for
the company's strengthening and the rehabilitation of the existing infrastructure. The
management contract would be a first step in the process of involving the private sector in
the delivery of the service, which is expected to result in a concession or lease contract at
the end of the project.
3.20. Under the design that has been adopted for the management contract, the operator
will be directly responsible for the management of the services and the contracts with
providers, for controlling the commercial activities of AdM and for the implementation
and monitoring of the works and investments to be financed by the project.  The
responsibility of managing the services also includes the obligatory achievement of the
performance targets of the Contract Plan agreed upon by AdM and its stakeholders. Key
tasks to be carried out by the operator include the management of:  (i) setting up
macrometering of production; (ii) development and implementation of the commercial and
information system; (iii) public relations with customers; and (iv) review of present
contracts with local operators for the provision of labor and equipment services for the
operation and maintenance of the systems. Investments required for these activities,
particularly for installing macrometering of production, computer hardware and software
for the commercial system and development of a public awareness campaign will be
financed under the project.
3.21. Consistent with its objectives, the management contract will be incentive-based to
encourage the private operator to reduce unaccounted-for-water and improve collection of
bills above the targets set in the Contract Plan. It has also been designed so that the direct
risk taken by the private operator is minimized by fixed monthly payments, financed by the
project as part of the regional component. These payments will only be conditioned to the
successful achievement of performance targets equal to those adopted as part of the
Contract Plan (penalties will apply when these performance targets are not reached). This
approach has been adopted in order to overcome the doubts that could arise in potential
operators because of the past experience with the failed concession of Caracas, the present
macroeconomic situation of Venezuela and the conditions of AdM.  Submission of
evidence that AdM has issued the letter of invitation for the Management Contract bid was
a condition of negotiations (para 5.1 f). Signature and effectiveness of the Management
Contract is a condition of disbursement for the second rehabilitation package of
investments (para 5.4 b).



29
F. Project Implementation
3.22. Executing Agencies. The project will be implemented over a four-year period.
The borrower will be the Republic of Venezuela. AdM will execute the regional
component and HIDROVEN will execute the national institutional component. Present
experience of co-execution of Bank project among national and local-regional institutions
is very limited in Venezuela. Since this project intends to support decentralization, to
ensure that this concept is met and to simplify the management by AdM of the loan
proceeds for the subcomponents it will execute, confirmation of the request to the Finance
Commission of the National Congress to include AdM as co-executor of the project,
together with HIDROVEN, in the Umbrella Law (Ley Paraguas) 1996 was condition of
negotiations (para 5.1 c).
3.23.  Flow of Funds. The loan proceeds for the reimbursement of expenses incurred
under the regional component will be transferred into a trust fund managed by AdM, with
two accounts: (i) the first account will be used for the expenses related to the institutional
support to AdM and the three rehabilitation packages in urban areas and will receive the
funds as reimbursable transfers made according to a subsidiary loan agreement, between
the national government and AdM, with the same maturity and grace period as the Bank
loan; (ii) the second account will be used for the expenses related to the PROPAMAR,
which will receive the funds as non-reimbursable transfers from the national government as
part of a direct subsidy to poor rural users. The interest rate for the reimbursable transfers
of funds will be variable and pegged to the active rate for the six largest commercial banks
as reported by the Central Bank. Funds for the national institutional component will be
provided to HIDROVEN as a non-reimbursable transfer from the Government. Project
Management Units will be established in AdM and HIDROVEN to implement the
components that each will execute. HIDROVEN will coordinate overall monitoring and
reporting activities to the Bank. A detailed Implementation Plan is presented in Arnnex J.
Agreements were reached during negotiations on the onlending conditions for the
subsidiary loan to AdM, the conditions for the grant transfer of loan proceeds to AdM for
the PROPAMAR, and to HIDROVEN for the national institutional component (para 5.2
e) and the content and conditions of the Trust Fund Operating Agreement (para 5.2 f).
3.24. Disbursement Conditionality. Disbursement of funds for the first rehabilitation
package of emergency rehabilitation works, as well as funds for the institutional support to
AdM, would be contingent upon loan effectiveness. Disbursement for rehabilitation works
in Cedenio or any new municipality will be conditioned on the signature of the Contract
Plan by such municipality (para 5.4 a). Disbursement for the second rehabilitation package
for priority rehabilitation works will be conditioned on the signature and effectiveness of
the Management Contract with a private operator (para 5.4 b). Disbursement for the third
rehabilitation package will be conditioned on compliance with the first year targets
incorporated in the Contract Plan and a determination by the Bank. based on satisfactory
projections. that the targets for the current year will be reached by AdM (para 5.4 c).



30
3.25. Ceilings and eligibility criteria of the PROPAMAR will apply to investments in
rural areas under the rural rehabilitation subcomponent. Two grant ceilings would be set:
the first, amounting to approximately $56 per capita, would apply to those communities
which have a water system that needs to be improved (about 90% of the state's rural
communities); the second, amounting to approximately $180 per capita, would apply to
communities where no water supply infrastructure currently exists (about 10% of the rural
communities of the state). In order to become eligible for financing these communities
will have to: (i) set up or use an existing a community organization to plan, manage and
finance the operation and maintenance of their system; (ii) prepare, with support from
AdM (through the contract with an NGO), a proposal detailing the intended rehabilitation
investments, the nature and cost of the works, the anticipated improvements to be
obtained and the contribution to be raised in cash or in kind by the community for
investment cost-sharing and (iii) agree to take over the management and financing of the
system once it is rehabilitated, detailing the planned scheme for its operation, maintenance
and financing. Submission of a draft operating manual for the PROPAMAR was condition
of negotiations (para 5.1 g). Disbursement for the rural rehabilitation subcomponent will
be conditioned on:  (i) the signature of the contract with an NGO to manage the
PROPAMAR:  (ii) approval of the final operating manual for the PROPAMAR
satisfactory to the Bank: and (iii) initial capitalization of the trust fund in an amount of at
least US$ 150.000 (para 5.4 d and annex G para 11).
3.26.  Project Supervision.  Supervision requirements are expected to be high,
especially during the first year of project implementation. It is expected that at least three
supervision missions per year (four in the first year), staffed by an environmental engineer,
a financial analyst and an specialist in human resources and community participation, plus
one technical specialist from the resident mission, with a duration of about two weeks per
mission, will be required to supervise the project during its implementation.  Bank
missions should plan to visit the municipalities where rehabilitation works are under way,
at least once during construction and once during the operation of the rehabilitated
infrastructure, as well as HIDROVEN' offices in Caracas in relation with the national
component. Other states integrated in the Sector Decentralization Program will be visited
as time and resources permit. For the first year, including the project launch workshop,
the supervision requirements are estimated at about 45 to 50 staff weeks (about 15 staff
weeks from the resident mission, mainly for day to day supervision).  Supervision
requirements will fall to about 35 to 40 staff weeks per year thereafter (about 10 to 15
staff weeks per year from the resident mission).
3.27. Monitoring and Reporting Requirements. Overall coordination and report
preparation would be the responsibility of the Implementation Units in HIDROVEN and
AdM, which would submit semi-annual reports to all the financing agencies, the
Mancomunidad, the state's government office and HIDROVEN. Agreements were
reached during negotiations on the content and timetable for preparing and submitting to
the Bank audited project accounts and financial statements (para 5.2 h). Reporting



31
requirements and their content are spelled out in the project's implementation plan (Annex
J).
3.28. Both HIDROVEN and AdM have had little experience with managing a project
under Bank guidelines. Their capacity will be strengthened by the adoption of accounting
and financial monitoring procedures satisfactory to the Bank and by the hiring of qualified
consultants and the contracting of the private operator in AdM under the management
contract (see annex J). Recognizing these institutions' unfamiliarity with Bank procedures
and guidelines, it is also proposed that within two months after loan approval, a launch
workshop will be held in Venezuela. The purpose of this workshop will be to acquaint the
Implementation Units with Bank's procedures and therefore ensure that the project will get
off to an expeditious and good start.  The workshop will concentrate on fulfilling
conditions for effectiveness and disbursement, on procurement and sample bid documents,
on withdrawal requests, on reporting (including monitoring of key project performance
indicators), on accounting of expenses procedures (as part of project preparation,
guidelines and support is being provided to HIDROVEN and AdM on this matter,
particularly for the eligible expenses for retroactive financing) and in general on all key
project events.  Adoption by AdM  and HIDROVEN  of accounting and financial
monitoring procedures satisfactory to the Bank is a condition of effectiveness (para 5.3 c).
3.29. Mid-Term Review. The mid-term review will be carried out not be later than 2
years from the date that the loan becomes effective.  During this review particular
attention will be paid to the status of: (a) compliance with the performance indices
included in the Contract Plan (see table 3.3.); (b) creation and proper functioning of the
CHU; (c) degree of community participation in the project in general, and in the rural
systems in particular; (d) compliance with covenants, particularly those dealing with the
restructuring, finances and auditing of the sector and tariff level and structure;  (e)
progress in procurement; and (f) project success as indicated by user's satisfaction and the
report of a local NGO.  The mid-term  review will be carried out jointly by the
Government, the Mancomunidad, the state government, HIDROVEN, the IDB and the
Bank. As a result of this review corrective steps will be proposed. These might include
modifications in project scope and/or components, changes in covenants, etc. The project
has also been structured so that, depending on the results of the mid-term Review,
contracting of certain project components will proceed or be postponed or canceled. A
detailed implementation plan for the project is also included in Annex J. Agreements were
reached during negotiations on the project's implementation plan and the timing and
content of the project's annual and mid-term review mission and semiannual reports (para
5.2 c and i).
G. Procurement, Disbursement and Auditing
3.30.  Procurement.  HIDROVEN  and AdM  will be responsible for procurement
implementation. They will manage the multiple purchases required for project implementation
with the help of the consultant services hired under the national institutional component (in the



32
case of H1DROVEN) and the private operator hired under the management contract (in the
case of AdM). Their capacity and skills to this end will be enhanced during the planned
project's launch workshop. Article 73 of Venezuela's Procurement Law [Ley de Licitacionesy
su Reglamento, Gazeta Oficial No. 34.528 (1990) y No. 34.628 (1991)] states that contracts
which are financed by international financial institutions will be procured following the
procurement guidelines of the respective institutions. Procurement of goods and civil works
under the project would be done in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement
under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits dated January 1995, as revised in January 1996, using
Bank-issued standard bidding documents for international competitive bidding (ICB), and
standard bidding documents agreed to with the Bank for national competitive bidding (NCB).
The project procurement arrangements are summarized in table 3.4.
3.31. It is estimated that 8 percent of the loan will be used to procure goods and services
under ICB, 33 percent under NCB and 10 percent under fixed price contracts awarded on
the basis of quotations obtained from, at least, three qualified domestic contractors in
response to a written invitation. Of the remainder, 38 percent will be devoted to procure
consultants' services under Bank guidelines.  Specific procurement arrangements for
consultant services, civil works and goods and equipment purchases are detailed below:
(a)    Procurement of Consultants. The contracting of US$ 14.6 million of
consulting firms and of individual consultants to be Bank financed would be carried
out in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines on the Use of Consultants, dated
August 1981, which require prior review by the Bank of budgets, short lists,
selection procedures, letters of invitation, proposals, evaluation reports and
contract. For complex consulting assignments, Bank-issued standard forms of
contract would be used. Consultant services worth US$ 5.4 million would be
financed by the IDB and would be contracted according to IDB's guidelines.
(b)   Procurement of Civil Works. Given the nature of the goods and services
to be procured under the project, participation is expected from local contractors.
Civil works includes repairs to the existing water and sewerage systems, most of which
is relatively small in size and geographically dispersed. As the largest civil works
contract will not exceed US$ 2 million, it is unlikely that foreign contractors would be
interested. To the extent practicable, civil works would be grouped into packages
estimated to cost the equivalent of US$ 250,000 or more, and procured on the basis of
NCB. Very small works which cannot be grouped into packages estimated to cost
the equivalent of less than US$ 250,000 would be procured under lump sum, fixed
price contracts awarded on the basis of quotations obtained from at least three
eligible contractors in response to a written invitation, up to an approximate
aggregate of US$ 5.1 million.

3.32. Prior Review Arrangements. Requirements for Bank's prior review of procurement
documentation will be as follows:



34
(a)    Goods and Civil Works. All bid packages of US$ 100,000 or more for goods
and US$ 250,000 or more for works, and the first two contracts for works after project
effectiveness, irrespective of their amount, would be subject to the Bank's prior review
of procurement documentation.
(b)    Consultant Services. All consultant contracts estimated at the equivalent of
US$ 100,000 or more for firms and US$ 50,000 or more for individuals would be
subject to the Bank's prior review. The requirement of Bank prior review will not
apply to contracts valued at less than the aforementioned amounts. However, this
exception to prior Bank review will not apply to: (i) the terms of reference for said
contracts; (ii) single-source selection of consulting firms; (iv) assignments of a critical
nature, as reasonably deterrnined by the Bank; and (v) amendments of contracts raising
the contract value over US$ 100,000 in the case of firms and US$ 50,000 in the case of
individual consultants.
3.33. The prior review thresholds described above will result in prior review by the Bank
of approximately 60 % of the total value of all Bank-financed contracts. This relatively
limited prior review is justified by the fact that most contracts for both goods and works
will be small and widely dispersed in terms of location and timing and by the control
provided through the physical audits to be carried out by the private operator managing
AdM on the urban rehabilitation investments and by the NGO that will manage the
PROPAMAR on the rural rehabilitation investments.
3.34. Disbursements. Disbursements will be made over a four year period, which is
shorter than the average disbursement period for water projects in the LAC Region.
However, given the simple nature of the investments to be financed under the project, and
the management capacity of the private contractor which will facilitate project execution,
the proposed disbursement period is reasonable. In order to lower the risk of insufficient
counterpart funds from the central government due to current fiscal constraints,
disbursements would be made on a declining percentages basis, as shown in tables 3.5 and
3.6 below. These tables also present expected disbursement amounts by category and
year. The project is expected to be completed by June 30, 2000. Therefore, the closing
date for the proposed loan would be December 31, 2000.
3.35. Expenditures for individual contracts for goods and services for which prior review
by the Bank is not required will be disbursed by means of Statements of Expenditures
(SOEs).  Detailed supporting documentation for expenditures will be kept by the
executing agencies and provided to the Bank's supervision missions and project auditors
when requested. Full documentation shall be provided for all contracts requiring Bank's
prior review.

receipt of properly documented withdrawal applications. The initial deposit will be limited
to US$ 1,000,000. The balance of the authorized allocation would be available for
withdrawal once aggregated disbursements reach a total of US$ 4,000,000. Subject to
approval by the Central Bank, the Special Account would have two sub-accounts, one for
each executing agency, to facilitate the operation and transparency of the Special Account.
The initial date for retroactive financing will be one year before loan signing. Payments made

3.37. Accounting and Auditing. The financial management capacity of both AdM and
HIDROVEN was reviewed during project appraisal. Although it is expected that most
project management activities will be outsourced, it will still be necessary to improve
capacity in each of these institutions. The project's institutional development components
will address these needs. Before project effectiveness, a financial reporting and accounting
system in both executing agencies will be agreed upon, in order to ensure accountability
and correct management of project funds and to facilitate auditing during project
implementation. The accounting system will be developed following an in-depth review of
budgeting, accounting, internal control and reporting procedures and planned flow of
funds for the project, and it will describe: (i) the sources and uses of funds provided under
the project; and (ii) the amount of expenditures reimbursed through the Statement of
Expenditure system, the Special Account and through direct payment by the Bank,
respectively. The adoption of financial reporting and accounting systems in both agencies
satisfactory to the Bank will also have the objective of guaranteeing the existence of
adequate monitoring and reporting capacity in these executing agencies.
3.38. Bank staff have also reviewed with the authorities the set-up of the necessary
project accounts, the Bank's auditing requirements, and the use and operation of the
Special Account.  The two implementing agencies would manage separate project
accounts. The Bank will review the external auditor's terms of reference for a multi-year
audit contract. The Bank will continue to provide specialized support to the implementing
agencies during the early stages of project implementation. Agreements were confirmed
during negotiations that project accounts, financial statements, the Special Account and
the SOEs would be audited annually by independent auditors satisfactory to the Bank and
that their reports will be prepared following acceptable auditing standards (para 5.2 g).
The audited accounts and the auditor's report would be submitted to the Bank within six
months following the end of each fiscal year. SOEs will be reviewed by the Bank on a
regular basis.
H. Environmental Impacts
3.39. The project has an environmental category rating of "B". It will concentrate
primarily on the rehabilitation of the existing infrastructure, the development of the legal



37
and institutional framework for the appropriate management of water resources and the
improvement of service quality. Environmental conditions in the urban and rural areas that
now receive inadequate services will improve as a result. The project will also have a
positive impact on the water quality conditions of the state due to the adoption of better
wastewater management practices and the rehabilitation of existing treatment plants.
Specifically, water use conflicts and wastewater management options will be identified,
water quality objectives for selected river basins will be established, pollution loads and
their relative weight in overall water quality of selected river basins will be determined and
watershed protection measures will be identified. These activities will be undertaken
under the subcomponent of the national component that will support the creation and
functioning of the Users' Hydrographic Confederation, to be implemented by
HIDROVEN, with the involvement of the area stakeholders (see annex I for details and
budget line item).
3.40.  Given that the project will finance the rehabilitation of existing infrastructure, and
some minor expansion of the distribution and sewerage networks (mainly in marginal and
rural areas), no siting issues, adverse environmental effects or social problems are
expected to be encountered. Therefore, no explicit environmental criteria or review
procedures are required for the relevant subprojects.  Final engineering designs and
consultant services for the preparation of an improvement and expansion plan for the
water and sewerage systems will give adequate attention to environmental issues. Terms
of Reference to be prepared during project implementation for these activities will be
subject to prior Bank review to guarantee this end.
1. Social Impact
3.41. Given the existing relatively high levels of WS&S service coverage in Venezuela,
the impact of the project will mainly be felt through the positive health, social and
environmental consequences of avoiding the deterioration of these coverage levels and
improving the quality and reliability of these services. The project's rural subcomponent
will expand these services, improving and augmenting the present coverage levels among
the poor population in those communities, and it will help strengthen the communities'
involvement and self-reliance in public services planning and provision.
J. Project Sustainability
3.42.  The project has been prepared through a strong participatory process involving
consultation and a series of workshops with all relevant stakeholders (elected officials,
representatives of commerce and industry, user groups and NGOs) and two extensive
willingness to pay studies in the urban and rural areas. There is a broadly shared view in
Venezuela, and particularly in Monagas, that significant institutional reforms are possible
when accompanied by concrete improvements in service reliability. The project will help
obtain these. The project will also help establish an adequate regulatory framework at the



38
national level and an efficient water utility in the state of Monagas to administer WS&S
services in a commercial manner, eliminating national government subsidies over the
implementation period. A financial plan was agreed to achieve, in the early years of
project implementation, the coverage of at least operating costs, and permit, by the end of
the implementation period, debt service and contribution to future expansion. Targets for
financial and physical performance and investments were agreed and incorporated into the
draft Contract Plan to be signed among the state, the Mancomunidad of municipalities and
AdM. In addition, to achieve these targets, AdM will contract, through a competitive
process, the management of the service to a private operator with international experience.



39
IV. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
A. Project Benefits
4.1.   The major benefits of this project will be:  (a) improved water supply and
sewerage service to the population of the state of Monagas; (b) a well-operated and
financially viable water supply and sewerage system in the state; (c) a clearer delineation
of responsibilities between institutions; and (d) effective sector policies at the national
level. Another important benefit will be the mobilization of additional resources for the
sector through improved billing and collection and appropriate pricing, thereby shifting the
financial burden of the services from the national government to the users.  The
management of AdM by a private operator will be a major impetus to greater involvement
of the private sector in other systems in Venezuela. The project will directly benefit the
majority of the state population, estimated at about 540,000 inhabitants. Improving the
collection and final disposal of wastewater and adopting better wastewater management
practices will also cause environmental benefits.
4.2.  In addition to these benefits, the project is consistent with the Government's
strategy for the sector (para 1.8) and the last Bank Country Assistance Strategy, builds on
lessons learnt from other Bank operations in LAC, and has secured the national and local
government political support (para 1.25 through 1.29). The work carried out during
project preparation has also confirmed its justification through: (i) the economic analysis
of the project as a whole and, particularly, of the rehabilitation investments; (ii) the
financial analysis of AdM; (iii) two willingness-to-pay studies in Maturin and in the state's
rural communities; and (iv) the assessment and mitigation of the project risks. The results
of this preparatory work are presented below, and detailed in Annexes D, K and L.
B. Economic Justification
4.3.   The proposed project will improve water distribution and quality of service and reduce
water losses in Monagas. The evaluation of project costs and benefits of the reallocation of
water among users and reduction of losses, based on a conservative marginal social value for
water of Bs 60 per cubic meter (the current tariff), results in an economic rate of return of
15% for the rehabilitation investment component, or about 65% of project costs. If consumer
surpluses, based on a marginal social value of Bs 100/i3, are also taken into account in the
calculation, the rate of return rises to 27%. The details of this economic analysis are presented
in Annex L. Additional justification for the project can be provided by the expected impacts:
(a) at the macroeconomic level, where the reduction of public transfers will positively impact
the fiscal deficit without reducing the level of service, as AdM assumes service responsibility
and water tariffs are rationalized. Savings could eventually reach more than US$ 200 million
annually in EIIDROVENs budget if this approach is expanded nationwide; (b) at the sector
level, where the proposed project will have an important impact not only on promoting a



40
rational structure to administer water supply systems throughout the country, but it will also
contribute to the performance of other sectors such as public health. In the state of Monagas,
deficient water and sewerage service is regarded as a "public health problem", where local
health officials estimated that 42,000 cases of water-related illnesses (with a pubLic health cost
calculated at US$ 2.4 million annually) can be prevented by improved water and sanitation
services. Concomitant losses in productivity as a result of lbsences from work due to illness
have been estimated to amount to 56,000 work days per year, at an additional cost of about
US$ 0.6 million per year.
4.4.   Quantification of Economic Benefits.  Without the proposed project, it can be
expected that the distribution network in Monagas will continue to deteriorate, current
shortages will become more severe and losses will continue to increase due to lack of
maintenance. With the project, a gradual irnprovement is expected, generating benefits through
the rehabilitation of the distribution network, better operational and financial management and
reduction of losses from waste, excessive consumption and the system's disrepair. The
economic benefits of the project will be derived from (i) the reallocation of water among users
and (ii) the reduction of physical losses. In order to measure the impact of reallocation, it is
assumed that a gradual shift from areas with excessive consumption to shortage areas will take
place in the next 5 years as a result of the proposed project. This shift will reallocate as much
as 10% (5.5 Mm3 per year) of maximum demand, from a "market" which consumes excessive
amounts at almost zero price, toward a "market" which currently has permanent shortages due
to low pressures or distribution problems (deteriorated pipes, non-working valves, in-house
leaks, etc.) and where water has a higher economic value (as detected by the high willingness-
to-pay of the population). This reallocation will result in net gains with a Net Present Value of
US$ 10.2 million'. Physical losses are estimated now at about 20 Mm3 per year or nearly one-
third of production. The impact of system repairs and operation improvements on losses
reduction was estimated assuming that in the next five years, these loses are reduced by 500/o,
that is, 10 Mm3 are recovered yearly by the year 2000 and that such volume will be available to
areas with current deficits and to new consumers. The Net Present Value of the recovery of
physical losses is calculated at US$ 20.5 million. The benefits of (i) recapturing physical losses
and (ii) reallocating water use among consumers due to better operation and management, will
result in a total Net Present Value of about US$ 30.7 million.
4.5.   Sensitivity analysis. The most critical parameters in the calculation of the project's net
benefits are the marginal value of water, the volume of water which can be recovered and the
amount that can be redistributed. The above estimates may be considered a conservative
scenario, since the value of water (Bs 60 per m3) is the minimum which can be assumed (see
annex D where results from the willingness-to-pay studies show that much higher values are
attributed by consumers). Also, the 5.5 Mm3 per year redistributed is only 10% of most
probable consumption, and the 10 Mm3 of physical losses to be reduced in 5 years is only 10%
of production and 50% of the estimated physical losses - a feasible goal.
In this case, there is a zero net transfer in terms of water volumes. Gains are discounted at 12% over a
20 year. As there are no estimates of the marginal cost of capital to use as the discount rate, 12%
is used as point of reference.



41
C. Financial Analysis of AdM
4.6.   Financial Model and Projections.  According to the financial model and
projections prepared using the most reliable information available, under the project
assumptions AdM's future financial performance is expected to be satisfactory. AdM is
expected to generate operating and net incomes on the order of 22% and 13%,
respectively, of revenues by the end of project execution, cover about 33% of investments
by 1999 and 2000 and 100% thereafter, and maintain a debt service coverage ratio above
1.5 after 2000 and over 2 after 2002, and a quick ratio over 6 during project execution
and over 1 .5 after project execution. Rates of return on invested capital are projected by
the model to meet or exceed 5% on revalued assets on 2006. A study to identify and
reevaluate the service's assets and liabilities and the assets transferred under Comodatum
has been carried out during project preparation. These assets (presently under
HIDROCARIBE, MARNR and the municipalities) have been transferred to AdM, upon
the transfer of the service from HIDROCARIBE to AdM, and properly accounted for (the
model takes into consideration assets valued about 2,350 million Bs, that are registered in
HIDROCARIBE's accounts for 1995). Annex K shows the financial forecast for the
period 1996-2005, including a description of the methodology and main assumptions used.
The agreed financial performance covenants for AdM, as confirmed during negotiations,
are shown in table 4.1. (para 5.2 j)
Table 4.1. Financial Targets for AdM
Indicators             1996    1997    1998   1999    2000

Contribution to         n/a     12%     17%    33%      33%
Investments (%)
4.7.   Sensitivity Analysis. The analysis of AdM's financial projections showed that the
financial situation of the company is very sensitive to lower-than-expected revenues
because of: (i) delays or lack of adoption of tariff level adjustments; (ii) collection/billing
efficiency is not improved according to projected targets; or (iii) the reduction targets
established for the unaccounted-for-water can not be achieved (the associated reduced
revenues and increased operation costs can not be transferred to the consumers through
tariff adjustments). Small changes in the assumptions for these parameters would impact
on AdM's finances and would easily make the company's investment program not viable



42
(a mere 5% reduction in the tariff adjustment projected for 1997 would result in a
reduction of about 29 and 47% of the net income and cash flow aggregated until 2001 and
a required increase in the counterpart contribution support of about 23%, while a 10%
failure in the target for the reduction of unaccounted-for-water would result in
respectively -12%, -29% and +9% changes in these same indicators). The risk that this
represents will be controlled through intensive and close supervision and the expected
continued political commitment and support for the sustainability of the company and the
project objectives, and through these to the adoption of tariff increases when these are
necessary to counteract exceptional circumstances that are external to AdM's management
capacity. They will additionally be minimized through the contracting of the company's
management to a private operator which has proved experience in this field and the
adoption of an investment program that will concentrate on the rehabilitation of the system
and on metering.
4.8.   On the other hand, the sensitivity of AdM's financial situation to worse than
projected exchange rate and domestic inflation or higher internal interest rates is small.
The exposure of AdM to changes in these parameters can be summarized as follows:
a) AdM's principal exchange rate exposure is linked to the impact on the cost of
imported goods and services to be financed under the project, but not on the debt
service of the bolivar-denominated subloan (exchange rate risks for loan repayment
will remain with the national government). Therefore, the impact of increases over the
expected official exchange rate at the end of 1997 is small, mainly due to the automatic
adjustment of tariffs (an increment of 20% over the projected exchange rate would
only require an increment of the counterpart support of 1%), and this mnight be
mitigated by reductions or changes in the proposed investment plan.
b) Similarly, if tariffs are adjusted according to the present adjustment formula,
AdM's exposure to domestic inflation is mainly linked to the impact it might have on
the exchange rate or the cost of the investments, but only marginally through the
increments of the operation and maintenance costs (the impact of an increment of the
domestic inflation of about 20% over the projection would even have a 3% positive
impact on the net income figures in current bolivares, but would require an increment
in the counterpart support of 7%).
c) The interest rate for the subsidiary loan to AdM is assumed to respond to changes
in the inflation rate, but-if interest rate increases are above these projections, AdM's
main risk will be associate with its difficulty in transferring the incremental cost to
consumers without a tariff increase, since this element is not included as part of the
updating formula. However, since the interest will be capitalized during the
construction period, the impact of increases in the interest rate will be mainly felt on
the cash flow and income after project completion and on the ability of AdM to repay
the loan to the national government.



43
4.9.   Working capital. Initial working capital for AdM will be provided through the
combination of three mechanisms: (i) AdM will collect all accounts receivable as of July
1995, when it took over the commercial management of the service, and keep a
percentage of these payments equivalent to the excess over the historic collection rate of
HIDROCARIBE; (ii) for the period between July and December 1995, HIDROCARIBE
has covered the operating deficit of AdM using the reallocated funds received from the
national government for the operation of the service in the state of Monagas in 1995; and
(iii) a revolving fund will be created in AdM for the financing of the project investments
through the reimbursement by the Bank of the expenses eligible for retroactive financing
executed by HIDROCARIBE, that will be fully covered by grant contributions from the
state and national governments budgeted investment funds.  With the conservative
assumptions used in the financial projections, AdM is expected to maintain a positive cash
balance of about 89 million Bs in 1999 (increasing to 158 and 3,400 million Bs in 2000
and 2001) and to generate increasingly positive balances from this year onwards. Given
the importance of securing sufficient working capital for AdM's proper functioning and
for the adequate project implementation, submission of a plan satisfactory to the Bank for
the provision of working capital for AdM was condition of negotiations (para 5.1 b).
4.10. Financial Risks. As a new company with a low level of permanent personnel and
tariff levels which have been adjusted in line with projected financial needs prior to project
execution, AdM starts off on a financial footing stronger than many similar companies
supported by Bank projects. Nevertheless, it faces financial risks primarily related to
operating in a high inflationary environment for the next several years, including further
expected devaluation of the currency and variable high interest rates under the proposed
onlending arrangements. This environment will only make the movement from a culture
of non-payment for water supply and sanitation services to achievement of financial
autonomy for AdM more difficult. Therefore, the principal risk to the financial viability of
the project relates to the political willingness to make future adjustments to tariffs in the
context of high domestic inflation and adjustments to the exchange rate, lower-than-
expected revenues and slower than expected reductions in unaccounted-for-water levels.
4.11.  Also important is the risk associated to the availability of the funds that are
expected from the national government, both for the investments that are planned to be
carried out before project effectiveness (which are linked to the initial provision of
working capital to AdM) and for counterpart contribution. A possible worsening of the
macroeconomic situation might aggravate this risk.
4.12. The project has been designed to lessen these risks through: (i) a commitment for
a capital subsidy from the national government during the early years of project execution,
justified by the serious state of neglect and disrepair that characterizes the system being
turned over to the local authorities; (ii) getting clarity and support for the project's
financial needs by holding discussions with a wide circle of stakeholders in the executive
and legislative branches of the national government and local governments and broad
consultation with the affected communities and agreeing on mutual obligations, formalized
in the Contract Plan;  (iii) utilizing the services of a private operator to introduce



44
efficiencies and provide tangible evidence of improved services to make the tariff
adjustments, when required, more supportable; and (iv) linking the third package of
rehabilitation investments with compliance with the agreed financial, physical and
investment targets (including the provision of sufficient counterpart funds) of the Contract
Plan (para 5.4 c).  Given the importance of adequate tariff levels, during project
preparation the residential reference tariff was increased from Bs 17.5 to Bs 40 per cubic
meter as a condition of negotiations (para 5.1 a) and submission of evidence that a
residential reference tariff increase from Bs 40 to Bs 60 per cubic meter has been
approved and implemented is a condition of effectiveness (para 5.3 b). Additionally, to
lessen the risk of a possible shortfall in counterpart funds from the national government,
the project design allows for a reduction in the investment plan for AdM, since works have
been packaged into three subcomponents that can be independently executed.
D. Willingness to Pay Studies
4.13. Two willingness to pay studies conducted during project preparation2 yielded the
following main results, which support the project justification:
*  The Maturin Study. Results from the Maturin study indicate that among the urban
population there is a high demand and willingness to pay for a water supply system
with the characteristics the project will support. Against a present average cost of the
service of US$ 1.25 per month (US$ 15 per year) per connection, the study found that
85% of respondents would use an improved water system (without improvements in
sanitation) at an average monthly cost of 1,000 Bs (US$ 5.88), 63% would use it at
4,000 Bs (US$ 23.5), and even 35% would use it at 7,000 Bs (US$ 41.2)3. The study
also found that household demand is mainly for improved water quality and
reliability; hence the project has given priority to rehabilitating the water systems
over other actions (see Annex D for further details).
*  The Rural Areas Study. Results indicate that rural residents are primarily concerned
with water quality and its impact on health.  The study predicts that 60%  of
respondents -would be willing to pay Bs 2,000 (US$ 11.76) per month and 50% would
pay 3,000 (US$ 17.6) per month for improved service providing safely potable water.
These results are significant given that rural users are currently not billed at all for their
water service.  Moreover, Fifty-six percent of respondents believe that their
community would want to run its own water system, 73% are somewhat or very
interested in serving in a water committee, and 58% think that the community would
do a better job than government in managing the water system. These results provide a
2     The studies were conducted by CVM, Inc. (USA), in association with DOXA Instituto de
Opini6n Piiblica (Venezuela), in June, July and August 1995.
The planned tariff increase from the current 40 to 60 Bs/m3 will amount to an average monthly
price of 1,800 Bs (US$ 10.60) for residential users and about half that figure for social
consumers, for an average household consumption level of 30 m3/month.



45
very favorable basis for the rural pilot program proposed under the project. (See
Annex G for further details).
E. Project Risks
4.14. The major risks for the proposed project are: (i) inability to overcome the present
weak institutional capacity; (ii) absence of payment culture which could hinder sound
commercial practices and the achievement of agreed financial targets;  (iii) possible
shortfall in counterpart funds and delays in tariff adjustments, which could be aggravated
by a worsening of the macroeconomic situation; and (iv) decreased political support. The
first two risks will be reduced by the implementation of the institutional strengthening
components of the project which will build up normative, regulatory and technical capacity
and take advantage of private sector involvement through a management contract. The
third risk has been greatly reduced by the recent agreement with the IMF and is mitigated
by the strong commitment of national and local government authorities to the
decentralization process and to the concept of financial sustainability in general and to the
project in particular, as evidenced by budgetary allocations made for this project under the
Ley Paraguas (despite stiff public expenditure cuts), the agreed obligations of the state
government and the municipalities under the Contract Plan, including the tariff increase
adopted during project preparation and the tariff increase to be adopted before loan
effectiveness, and a financing plan that seeks to reduce dependency from national budget
support early in project implementation. The fourth risk is being minimized and managed
through: (i) the Bank's ongoing discussions with a wide circle of stakeholders in the
executive and legislative branches of central government and at the local government
level; and (ii) broad consultation with the affected communities and NGO involvement in
project preparation.  The third and fourth risks will also be reduced by expected
constituent demands on the authorities to address the problem of the physical supply
infrastructure, which is near a state of collapse, and by the grouping of the works to be
financed under the project into three rehabilitation packages that can be independently
carried out, linked to the achievement of financial and operation conditions. The project
assumptions, risk ratings and minimization measures are summarized in the following risk
matrix.

5.1.  The following conditions were met before negotiations:
(a)   submission of evidence that the residential reference tariff increase from Bs
17.5 to Bs 40 per cubic meter has been approved and implemented (para
2.13 and 4.12);
(b)   submission of a plan satisfactory to the Bank for the provision of working
capital for AdM (para 4.9);
(c)   confirmation of the request to the Finance Commission of the National
Congress to include AdM as co-executor of the project, together with
HIDROVEN, in the Umbrella Law (Ley Paraguas) 1996 (para 3.22);
(d)   submission of signed copies satisfactory to the Bank of the agreements and
contracts that confer and regulate AdM's right to operate the services
(Ratification Agreements by municipalities, Comodatum Agreement among
AdM and the MARNR, Service Contracts between the municipalities and
AdM, and Service Transfer Agreement between AdM and
HIDROCARIBE) (para 3.15);
(e)   submission of draft copies of the Contract Plan, Subsidiary Agreement
among the MF, AdM and HIDROVEN and of the Trust Fund Operating
Agreement (para 3.15 and 3.18);
(f)   submission of evidence that AdM has issued the letter of invitation for the
Management Contract bid (para 3.21);
(g)   submission of a draft operating manual for the Rural Rehabilitation and
Participatory Management Program (PROPAMAR) (para. 3.25); and
(h)   submission of terms of reference for consultants to assist HIDROVEN in
the execution of the national institutional component and the overall
project coordination (para 3.10 d).

monitoring indicators and targets (para 3.16);

rehabilitation packages consisting of: (i) emergency works; (ii) priority

5.5.  With the above conditions, the proposed project is suitable for a Bank loan of US$
39 million to the Government of Venezuela. The loan would be a Single Currency Loan,
with repayment over 17 years, including five years of grace at the standard variable
interest rate for LIBOR-based US dollar.  After negotiations the Government of
Venezuela selected this type of loan, with five years of grace, as part of its strategy to
reduce the risk in the debt portfolio and to limit the interest rate differential between this
loan and the subsidiary loan between the national government and AdM for the regional
component.

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Lessons Learned from Past Bank Experiences in the Water Sector
Introduction
1.    Until now, Bank involvement in the Water Supply and Sanitation Sector in
Venezuela has been limited, although Bank lending for the sector in the LAC region
reached $439 million in FY 1993. Those commitments have been heavily concentrated:
Brazil accounts for 47% of commitments, Mexico for 18% and Colombia for 15 percent.
Until recently, WS&S was viewed as a "social sector," with primary emphasis placed on
management from the central government and investments in expanding coverage rather
than assuring cost-recovery. Now many Latin American countries are moving towards
decentralization of services to regional and local authorities and increased attention to
rehabilitation and improved maintenance. This is a response to excessive drains on central
government resources, and a recognition that the quality and efficiency of services can
improve as management is brought closer to consumer demands at the local levels and
maintenance is properly done. Such decentralization efforts have also provided propitious
grounds for participation of the private sector in service operation and maintenance
(management contracts), and in some cases, investment (through long term concession
arrangements). The Venezuela Water and Sewerage Decentralization project for the state
of Monagas fully embodies the Bank's new policies for the WS&S sector, as well as the
Latin American movement towards decentralization and innovative approaches to water
resources management. Lessons learned from past WS&S operations in Argentina, Chile,
Brazil and other countries have helped to refine the design of this project, and are
presented below.
Fostering Financial Autonomy in a Regional/Local Water Agency: The Chile
Example
2.    Several experiences show that decentralization to a local or regional water agency
offers few advantages if the dependence on central govermment budgets for a large share
of investment needs and/or operation and maintenance costs is maintained. Such financial
dependence offers a disincentive for regional/local agencies to operate efficiently and
reduce costs. Political pressures that prevent the application of a tariff policy also hurt the
utility's ability to provide reliable service and appropriate coverage, often at the expense
of the poor who are left unconnected. They in tum must resort to altemative strategies
that often prove much costlier than would a simple connection to the network with an
enforced monthly tariff



Annex B
Page 2 of 6
3.    In Chile, the water supply sector benefits from a strong adherence to the principles
of cost-recovery with full marginal cost pricing. Financially independent regional water
companies receive no central government transfers, and must generate the resources
necessary to finance new investments. The government acts as a regulator through the
Superintendencia de Servicios Sanitarios, which approves tariff levels proposed by
agencies. This regulatory framework is neutral, and public and private water companies
must meet the same quality and efficiency requirements. As a result, both quality and
coverage of water services are high in that country, while central government resources
are not drained. The proposed project for Monagas places strong emphasis on helping the
new decentralized water agency, Aguas de Monagas, achieve financial self-sufficiency
through improved cost-recovery policies and an appropriate regulatory framework for
tariff-setting and efficiency monitoring.
Private Sector Participation: The Buenos Aires and Caracas Experiences
4.    Private sector participation in the management of water and sanitation services is
increasingly seen as a desirable alternative to weighty and inefficient public management.
The latter typically depends on central government transfers to finance its operations as it
is unable to gather the political will to enforce water tariffs. Publicly managed utilities are
often overstaffed, and cash-flow problems translate into poor service and deteriorating
infrastructure. Contracts with efficient private enterprises appropriately regulated by the
public sector provide a solution to the problems of deficient and deficitary service and
deterioration of assets.
5.    Recently, the Bank has supported efforts to transfer the operation, maintenance,
and investment responsibilities for WS&S services in Caracas and Buenos Aires to private
enterprises.  In a 25-30 years concession arrangements, the concessionaire enterprise
would assume all commercial and most financial risks, while fixed assets would remain
owned by the government.
6.    The two experiences have widely differed. In Caracas, the bid failed due to several
factors: i) a lack of political commitment and of good working relationships among the
municipalities of the Caracas metropolitan area impeded the successful regulation of the
contract, and implementation of the concession; ii) existing tariffs were well beneath the
level necessary to cover operating costs, and yet strong public demonstrations taking place
at the time against gasoline price increases made it difficult for the authorities to raise
water tariffs; iii) the negotiations process allowed little flexibility once the contract would
be awarded; iv) information on the system's commercial performance, the condition of
the assets and requirements for rehabilitation and investment was scant, and no significant
effort was made to upgrade it before the bid; and v) under the Venezuelan law, investors
are not protected against exchange rate changes.
7.    By contrast, the Buenos Aires concessioning experience has been relatively
successful, although it is too early to judge its operational and financial results. Argentina



Annex B
Page 3 of 6
had several advantages over Venezuela: i) the average tariff in that city already covered
operational and maintenance costs, which enabled bidders to offer even lower rates rather
than have to confront the political difficulties of raising water rates; and ii) it benefits from
foreign exchange risk law that protects investors. Moreover, the Argentinean government
had mobilized strong political will for the project, and with World Bank support, spent
resources on preparation work to diagnose the state of the system and service, and to
identify potential investors in advance.
8.    The proposed project will involve the private sector in the management of the
service through a management contract rather than a full concession, in order to achieve a
smoother transition towards private delivery of the service. In the context of project
preparation, one of the most significant achievements of local authorities - and
demonstration of their commitment - has been the increase in tariffs from 17 Bs./m3 to 40
Bs./m3, and the planning of an additional increase to 60 Bs. before the end of the year,
which makes the management of the service much more attractive to a prospective private
operator.
Institutional Readiness for Increased Responsibilities at the Regional/local level
9.    The process of decentralization depends for its success on the adequate
preparation of the local and/or regional authorities to whom responsiblities are delegated.
In several countries, such as Argentina, Brazil and Columbia, the implementation of
decentralization plans overlooked the lack of preparation of the local entities, causing
additional difficulties in the transfer process. The above-cited case of Caracas, where local
authorities could not reach consensus on the aims to work towards, illustrates this as well.
In many cases, this can be circumvented by relying at the outset on private sector
participation rather than on local public agencies, although the Caracas example shows
that agreement on this between local leaders is crucial. Transferring responsibilities to a
weak local entity can jeopardize service quality and efficiency from the beginning, which in
turn undermines the public's trust and reduces the entity's ability to justify tariff increases
and/or firmer cost-recovery policies.  The proposed project will finance technical
assistance to the local authorities for the implementation of sector reforms and the
contracting and supervision of a private operator.
Rural Water Supply: The Colombia Example
10.   Successful projects in Colombia and Bolivia have demonstrated the usefulness of
linking community participation with project design to ensure better user responsibility for
operation and maintenance, and better satisfaction of user needs at appropriate prices.
Colombia now has the reputation of having the best rural water supply program in Latin
America, as a result of a successful program sponsored by the National Institute of Public
Health which fostered community-managed water systems. Colombia's National Institute
of Public Health provides technical assistance to communities for project design as well as
community organization for water system management, while the community raises funds



Annex B
Page 4 of 6
and provides materials, labor, transport and cash for construction, and subsequently
operates, maintains and regulates the system through its water administrative committee.
This provides a useful model for the Rural Rehabilitation and Participatory Management
Program in the Monagas Water project, which aims to involve communities in the
preparation and implementation of community-based rehabilitation and improvement
plans, and to transfer the operation and maintenance of rural water systems to community-
elected water committees.
Water Resources Management: Chile, Venezuela and Brazil Experiences
I.   The proposed project will include in its national institutional component a program
to organize the management of the region's resources at the river basin level through an
Umbrella Association of Users (Confederaci6n de Usuarios) that would convene all water
users (farmers, municipalities, private users, the state water company AdM, industrialists,
etc.), to make decisions on water allocation and pollution control in the state of Monagas.
This model has been pioneered in Germany and Spain at the beginning of the century and
in the financial aspects in France in the late 1960s. It is now being replicated with
adjustments in several Latin American countries. Management institutions of this type
have been created in Venezuela (Lago de Valencia and Rio Tuy "Autoridades Unicas de
Area") and in Brazil (Sao Paulo and Ceara states). In Chile, the allocation of the
resources is now carried out through a water market.
12.   With the exception of the water market in Chile, it is too early to assess the
experience of these new institutions in Latin America, but initial developments suggest
that they benefit from better management and increased participation of user groups in
selecting priorities and planning investments. In the case of Venezuela, the Agencias
Ulnicas de Area have improved the management of water resources in the areas under
their jurisdiction.   However, they have had difficulties in implementing a truly
decentralized management system and remain fully dependent from central government
transfers for their functioning. The Agencias represent an enduring financial burden for
the central Government, and this has highlighted the need to develop their financial self-
sufficiency through more reliable cost-recovery mechanisms.  In the context of the
proposed project, the new scheme will place emphasis on applying appropriate water
charges that will enable the Confederaci6n to achieve financial autonomy from the central
Government in the medium term.
Major Lessons Learned from Past Experiences in Bank Lending in the Sector
13.   The above examples illustrate some of the experiences that have been useful in
formulating the project concept and design. Combined with additional analysis of past
Bank operations from the OED and the 1993 World Bank Policy Paper on Water



Annex B
Page 5 of 6
Resources Management, they yield the following major lessons for this and future projects
in the sector:'
*  WS&S agencies that rely less on government involvement and more on a clear
regulatory framework to guide private/user participation in service delivery
function better. The proposed project will help in clarifying the regulatory and
involving the private sector and user groups in the management of water
services;
*  Borrowers' executing  agencies greatly  benefit from  additional training
(intersectoral management, fee structure administration) and information
dissemination to improve their ability to implement the project and monitor its
performance. The proposed project will assist in training and information
dissemination through the national institutional strengthening component;
*  Emphasis should be placed on strengthening O&M  capacities before new
investments are financed. The proposed project places strong emphasis on the
improvement in management of WS&S services and infrastructure
rehabilitation rather than expansion;
*  Emphasis on efficiency and adequate water pricing will help to address inequity
issues related to the poor's access to WS&S services. Water pricing has been
efficiently addressed during project preparation and will continue to be a
priority for the new water agency in Monagas,
*  Where possible, emphasis should be given to private pumping and small scale
systems managed by users. The proposed project will achieve this through the
Rural Rehabilitation and Participatory Management Program, which will - on
the basis of community demand - finance community-based sub-projects and
transfer the O&M and cost-recovery responsibilities to communities,
*  An enabling institutional envirornment with consensus among main political
entities is key to the successful development and implementation of a project;
sustained Bank supervision and assistance to executing agencies in complying
with Bank directives and requirements is essential to ensure the integrity of the
project concept and its satisfactory execution and. The project includes close
'References for this annex included:
1. Water Supply and Sanitation Projects: The Bank's Experience, 1967-1989, OED, June 1992.
2. Annual Review of Portfolio Performance (ARPP) FY 93, Water and Sanitation Sector, TWURD, April
1994.
3. Venezuela Strategy Paperfor the Infrastructure Sectors, LAIIN, June 1993.
4. Managing Urban Water Supply and Sanitation: Operation and Maintenance, Lessons and Practices,
