VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Regulatory Framework Arrangements
Background
1.    Although the structural adjustment reforms initiated in 1989 in Venezuela included
the restructuring of Water Supply and Sanitation (WS&S) services by abolishing INOS -
the government-owned national WS&S agency - to create regional water companies, these
services remain de facto highly centralized with a complex organizational structure.
Moreover, the regulatory framework is undeveloped and ill-defined with overlapping
statutory responsibilities among the many water-related agencies. As part of the above
reforms, in 1991, a new national institution was created for the implementation of policy
and the coordination of the sector, HIDROVEN. At the same time, ten regional water
companies (HIDROS), wholly owned subsidiaries of HIDROVEN, were established to
provide this service, as a transitory decentralized arrangement until municipal or regional
companies could be set up. However, both HIDROVEN and the HIDROS have in reality
acted as regional branches of the Central Government. Although the Municipalities hold
constitutional responsibility for water supply and sewerage, the HIDROS have operated
the services under informal concession agreements (see Figure 1). This process has
resulted in a situation where responsibilities are undertaken in an informal manner, without
legal or administrative backing, by many of the institutions involved in the sector.
2     In 1992, authorities at the state and municipal level in Monagas initiated a local
reform process to increase the efficiency of water supply and sanitation services through
further decentralization. The state's ten municipalities first formed the Mancomunidad
Monaguense de Acueductos, in an effort to better coordinate water-related initiatives.
The Mancomunidad then associated with the state government to create Aguas de
Monagas (AdM), a new company that would gradually take over the service
responsibilities from HIDROCARIBE-Monagas, the local HIDRO. Fifty-one percent of
the company's capital is owned by the Municipalities, and the remaining forty-nine is
owned by the State. Until recently, AdM was only a legally created shell with no formal
operational responsibilities. In the context of project preparation, however, the process of
transferring functions to AdM has accelerated, highlighting some legal issues that are key
to the sector's decentralization activities.
Key Legal Issues
3.    The legal issues that could represent the main constraints for project
implementation and AdM's proper functioning have been analyzed by the Government



Annex C
Page 2 of 9
with the assistance of the Bank. They include: (i) AdM's legal status; (ii) the assignment
of the responsibility for the provision of WS&S services and the production of water; (iii)
the authority and procedures for setting and adjusting tariffs; and (iv) the mechanisms to
channel funds for project implementation.
1975  to  1991                1991  to  1994                      1996  -
Central Govemment             Central Govemment                Central Goverment
c1J 
cIII~HIDROS
FIGURE 1: INSTITUTIONS IN THE WATER SECTOR
4.    The resolution of these issues has formed the basis for several contracts and
agreements between the involved institutions in order to:-  i) improve the current
undeveloped regulatory framework and clarify institutional roles; ii) allow the proper
operation of AdM;- and iii) facilitate project implementation.  The contracts and
agreements have been prepared during project preparation to define and regulate the
relationships among the Government of Venezuela, the Ministry of Environment and
Natural Renewable Resources (MARNR), HIDROCARIBE (the regional HIDRO
responsible for service provision in Monagas), the state of Monagas (Gobernacion), the
Municipalities and AdM. This set of contracts and agreements is intended to complement,
in an inter-im way, the existing regulatory framework until the more developed regulatory
framework that will result from the the project's national institutional component is in
place. At the same time, it will serve as a demonstration exercise, to test the viability and



Annex C
Page 3 of 9
requirements that should satisfy the regulatory framework under preparation. This annex
presents each of the above-mentioned issues and the resulting legal agreements that will
enable the Government to move forward with this demonstration decentralization project.
5.    AdM's Legal Status.   Neither AdM's bylaws, nor the Venezuelan legal
framework, clearly establish the type of company AdM is (private company, public
company, municipal company) and which legal code it should follow in its operation. This
is critical with respect to accounting obligations, to the possibility of receiving transfer of
funds from Central Government under the project, to the obligations it might acquire as
part of the project legal terms and to its contractual relations either with the users or
among the share-holders of the company, particularly regarding the Mancomunidad and its
role as representative of the municipalities.
6.    As a result of the legal studies carried out, it seems that the statutes of AdM and
the Mancomunidad provide that: (i) AdM could be considered a municipal company that
operates under normal commercial practices;  and (ii) the Mancomunidad could
collectively represent the municipalities as shareholders of this company and take in their
name decisions on the normal functioning. The signing of this ratification was a condition
of negotiations.
7.    Responsibility for providing the WS&S service. Although Municipalities hold
constitutional responsibility to provide WS&S services, several other institutions had been
in charge of the delivery of this service and had to be involved in the transfer of
responsibility to AdM. In order to have AdM taking over the service in an orderly and
regulated way, the current practice and the legal opinion consulted has indicated that it is
necessary that, regulated under specific contracts and agreements, provisions are
established for AdM receiving (see Figure 2):
(a)   the legal title to provide the servicefrom the Municipalities,
(b)   the right to produce potable water and use the infrastructure that belongs
to the national governmentfrom MARNR; and
(c)   the infrastructure and  responsibility to  provide the  service firom
HIDROCARIBE.
8.    A Service Contract between AdM and each municipality is the legal agreement
which will authorize AdM to provide the WS&S services. Under this service contract the
municipalities delegate to AdM in all their rights and assignments regarding the provision
of the service. This contract: (i) expressly entitles AdM to contract totally or partially the
provision of the service, with the obligation to inform the municipality; (ii) establishes that
the tariff revenue must cover operation and maintenance costs, depreciation, investment
and improvement costs, and debt service;  (iii) entitles AdM  to propose to the
municipalities the regulation of the service and establishes that the municipalities must



Annex C
Page 4 of 9
issue, according to this proposal, the needed regulation regarding the obligations and
rights of the users and those of AdM. Eight municipalities have to date signed the Service
Contract, including among them Maturin, and the two other largest ones. Submission of
signed copies of the Service Contracts satisfactory to the Bank was condition of
negotiations.
9.    In addition, a Comodatum Agreement has been reached between AdM  and
MARNR, that cedes to AdM the right to use the infrastructure for the production of
drinking water in the state of Monagas. Indeed, in order to obtain an integrated
framework where water-related responsibilities are consolidated under one institution,
both water production and service provision will be taken over by AdM. The agreement
defines the obligations and rights of both AdM and MARNR. In particular, it establishes
that: (i) MARNR agrees not to grant new concessions for the use of water in the state of
Monagas without the technical advice of AdM; (ii) that MARNR retains general control
and supervision powers on water resources management, on a transitional basis, until the
Users' Confederation is established (see Annex I, which provides a detailed description of
the proposed institutional framework for water resources management); and (iii) AdM has
the capacity to contract with third parties the operation or maintenance of the
infrastructure for the production of potable water. Submission of a signed copy of the
Comodatum Agreement satisfactory to the Bank was condition of negotiations.
10.   Finally, a Service Transfer Agreement has been signed between HIDROCARIBE
and AdM  for the transfer of responsibilities, assets and personnel.   Although
Municipalities hold constitutional responsibility for the provision of this service, it has
been de facto provided  by HIDROCARIBE; therefore this agreement regulates the
transfer of responsibility to AdM and cedes the right to use the infrastructure. This
agreement will also regulate different aspects of this transfer regarding obligations,
pending contracts, debts and assets.  Submission of the signed Service Transfer
Agreement satisfactory to the Bank was condition of negotiations.
11.   Aside from the above agreements, which give AdM the legal rights to provide
water and sanitation services and to produce water, two additional legal instruments will
be used to ensure the proper operation and delivery of service by AdM. First, to formalize
the definition of performance expectations for AdM, a Contract Plan will be signed
between AdM and its stockholders, which defines the responsibilities of each party (AdM,
state of Monagas, and the Mancomunidad) and the performance targets for AdM. The
main elements of the Contract Plan are: (i) the preservation of AdM management
autonomy; (ii) the obligation of AdM to achieve specific performance targets; and (iii) the
authorization for AdM to interrupt the provision of service in the case of non-payment.
Submission of a draft Contract Plan was condition of negotiations. As a condition of loan
effectiveness. the Contract Plan shall have been signed by AdM. the state of Monagas
government and all municipalities in the state of Monagas which have authorized to
operate under a signed service contract (this includes all municipalities, except Cedenio).



Annex C
Page 5 of 9
As condition of disbursements for urban rehabilitation investments in Cedenio or any new
municipality, such municipality shall sign the Contract Plan
12.   Second, because the main problems of HIDROCARIBE are related to poor
management practices and as a result of policy discussions on the optimal manner of
involving the private sector, it has ben decided that a Management Contract would be
established between AdM and a private operator to manage the services over a period of
four years (described in detail in Annex E). Until now billing, collections and supervision
of service contracts has been deficient. Moreover, little management capacity exists in
public sector agencies. These factors have contributed to the decision to hire a private
operator for the management of water supply and sanitation services in the state of
Monagas. The Management Contract will be awarded through an international
competition in accordance with Bank guidelines for the hiring of Consultants, and financed
under the project. The main characteristics of this contract are that: (i) it is a contract
based on management incentives, which gives the operator full responsibility for the
commercial system of AdM and for the management of the investment plan financed under
the project, (ii) it uses the performance targets corresponding to those established in the
Contract Plan between AdM and its stockholders, and (iii) it defines the compensation
system, which will consist of a fixed retainer payment, to be financed by the project, and a
variable payment and two bonuses contingent on substantial improvements superior to the
performance targets and the achievement of key management targets. These variable
payments will be financed and limited by the income those improvements generate for
AdM. As a condition of negotiations AdM has issued the letter of invitation for the
management contract bid. Signing of the management contract will be a condition of
disbursement for the second rehabilitation investment package.
13.   Tariffs Setting and Adjustment Authority.  The legal basis for water tariff
setting like many other regulatory aspects of the sector is not totally clear. According to
the Law for the Protection of Consumers, the Ministry of Development (MF) and the
MARNR seem to have the authority and responsibility to regulate WS&S tariffs. Based
on this Law, tariff levels and structures were established in 1993 by a joint decree of both
ministries, that included a procedure for the automatic adjustment of tariffs according to
changes in the basic costs of the service. Also, HIDROVEN's approval of tariff changes
have been until now necessary in the name of the MF and the MARNR before they could
be implemented by the HIDROS.
14.   To overcome the rigidity of this procedure, which could imply a risk for AdM's
financial viability, the average residential tariff reference rate has been raised to 40 Bs/m3
before project appraisal and will be raised to 60 Bs/m3 before loan effectiveness.
HIDROVEN approved these adjustments and the application of the procedure for
automatic adjustment of tariffs according to changes in the costs of the service.
Additionally, the results of the legal studies carried out during project preparation on this
subject indicate that, once the service has been taken over by AdM in the name of the
municipalities, even though tariffs must still comply with the regulations of the Ministry of



Annex C
Page 6 of 9
Finance and the- MARNR, the authority to set tariffs resides in the municipalities,
represented by the Mancomunidad because: (i) WS&S services are a constitutionally
established municipal responsibility; and (ii) WS&S tariffs are a public price and not a
"tasa"'. Submission of evidence that tariffs adjustments have been aproved and
implemented will be a condition of loan effectiveness. Satisfactory compliance with the
physical. operating and finncial targets established under the Contract Plan will be a
condition of disbursement of funds for the third package of rehabilitation investments.

15.    Mechanisms for channeling loan funds. The Venezuelan authorities and the
project preparation team, with the assistance of a legal consultant, have defined
mechanisms to ensure an efficient, decentralized flow of funds from the Central
Government to AdM for the execution of the regional components of the project. Until
now any funds from the Central Government were channeled through HIDROVEN to the
HIDROS. Under the new institutional framework, and to effect decentralization, AdM
' Charge imposed by a public entity for the delivery of a service that is compulsorily given and taken by
the individuals



Annex C
Page 7 of 9
will receive loan funds directly from the Central Bank. It was agreed that starting in 1996,
the Ministry of Environment will give payment orders to channel funds directly from the
Central Bank to AdM for the components it will execute. HIDROVEN, as executor of
the national institutional component, will also receive funds from the Central Bank upon
order of the Ministry of Environment.
16.   As a result, two specific agreements between AdM, the Ministry of Finance and
HIDROVEN for defining responsibilities among involved institutions and regulating the
transfer of funds will be signed: (i) the Subsidiary Agreement between the national
government (through the Ministry of Finance and MARNR), HIDROVEN and AdM to
(a) transfer loan proceeds, on a reimbursable basis, to AdM for the institutional
development of AdM and the three rehabilitation packages for urban areas under the
regional component, (b) transfer loan proceeds, on a grant basis, to AdM for the rural
rehabilitation subcomponent, and (c) transfer loan proceeds, on a grant basis, to
HIDROVEN for the national institutional component; and (ii) the Trust Fund Operating
Agreement among AdM and a Fiduciary Bank, for delineating the terms and conditions
under which the Fiduciary Bank shall administer all funds to be provided for the financing
of the regional component. The Trust Fund Operating Agreement will clearly delineate
the responsibilities of AdM, the state government, the Municipalities, and the Ministries of
Finance and MARNR, use of funds, financial contributions, corresponding schedule and
terms and conditions under which the Fiduciary Bank shall administer such financial
contributions.  Submission of a draft Trust Fund Operating Agreement and draft
Subsidiary Loan and Grant Agreements satisfactory to the Bank were condition of
negotiations. Submission to the Bank of signed copies of these Agreements will be a
condition of effectiveness.
Summary and conclusion
17.   The contracts and agreements that have resulted from the resolution of the legal
issues derived from the decentralization and project preparation process can hence be
grouped in two types: (i) those that regulate the operation of AdM, and (ii) those regulate
the implementation of the project.
18.   To regulate the operation of AdM five contracts and agreements will be or have
been signed by AdM (see Figure 3):
(a)   The Service Transfer Agreement between HIDROCARIBE and AdM for
the transfer of responsibilities, assets and personnel;
(b)   the Comodatum Agreement between AdM  and MARNR that cedes to
AdM the right to use the infrastructure for the production of drinking
water in the State of Monagas;



Annex C
Page 8 of 9
(c)   the Service Contract between AdM  and the Municipalities authorizing
AdM to provide the WS&S services;
(d)   the Contract Plan between AdM and its stockholders (state of Monagas
and the Mancomunidad) to define the responsibilities of each party and the
performance targets for AdM,
(e)   the Management Contract between AdM and a private operator to manage
the services over a period of four years, and
(f)    a Ratification Agreement of the municipalities to harmonize these
documents and correct some inconsistencies and lack of definition detected
in the Mancomunidad and AdM's statutes.

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Willingness to pay for improved Water and Sanitation Services
Introduction: Rationale for a Willingness-to-pay Study
I.    The provision of water supply and sanitation (WS&S) services in Venezuela is largely
subsidized by the central government, which until now has born the increasing weight of the
investment and operating costs of the W&S service without making any major attempts at cost-
recovery. Under the decentralization approach to be tested in Monagas, a local authority will take
over the responsibility for service provision, setting tariffs and carrying out billing and collection.
The major challenge for the newly formed water company, Aguas de Monagas (AdM), will be to
align historically low tariffs with the trus cost of service and to become a financially self-sufficient
utility.
2.    In order to meet this challenge, and to define acceptable service tariffs, it is important to
predict user willingness-to-pay and its determinants.  This annex describes the household
willingness to pay (WTP) study that was performed in Maturin, the capital city of the State of
Monagas, during the period of June 2-30, 1995, in preparation for the transfer of facilities to AdM
and the improvement of Maturin's water and sewer systems. This study has proven essential to i)
establish support among local policy-makers for a more rigorous approach to WS&S services
management that relies on user contribution; ii) confirm the emphasis of project design on water
supply infrastructure improvement and rehabillitation; and iii) ratify tariff increase
recommendations resulting from the financial analyses carried out during project preparation. The
study also sheds much needed light on customer concerns over current service, and preferences
and priorities for the future improvements to the system, thereby providing policy-makers and the
project preparation team with a clear sense of priorities to be addressed.
Summary and Conclusions'
3.    In summary, results indicate that 85% of respondents would use an improved water supply
system (without improvements in sanitation) at an average monthly price of 1,000 Bs (US$ 5.88),
63% would use it at an average monthly price of 4,000 Bs. (US$23.5), and only 35% would use it
at an average monthly price of 7,000 Bs. (US$41). The study found that the variables which are
most statistically significant to explain willingness-to-pay results are price, income, and education.
The level of satisfaction with current level of service and the gender of the respondent were not
statistically significant variables. Given the demand probabilities derived from the study to project
revenues from water billing, an average monthly price of 5,000 Bs. constitutes a threshold beyond
which expected revenues would decrease.
' This and subsequent sections are adapted from the preliminary and draft final reports submitted by Donald T.
Lauria, Jennifer Davis and Elizabeth Mclelland from CVM, Inc. a U.S. firm which carried out the study herein
described with DOXA, a Venezuelan firm specializing in public opinion surveys.



Annex D
Page 2 of 13
4.     Three observations are in order. The first is that demand for improved water supply in
Maturin is quite high; for example, about 70% of the households are willing to pay about US$200
per year. In the face of such demand, a strong case cannot be made for external subsidies. The
second observation is that this high demand is for the type of improved system described to
respondents in the questionnaire, i.e., a system similar to those in Europe and North America. If
such a system cannot be created, it is unlikely that the demand reported herein would exist for an
alternative system with lower quality.
5.     Finally, household demand is mainly for improved water quality and reliability; hence, if
the investment criterion is to satisfy existing consumer preferences, then upgrading the water
system should take priority over wastewater removal and treatment. The fact that water quality is
viewed as a greater priority than is the quantity of water provided should not be interpreted to
mean that construction of water treatment facilities should take priority over rehabilitating the
water distribution network. The single most important step toward improving water quality may
be to ensure the reliable distribution of water 24 hours per day under positive network pressure.
6.     To conclude, the study achieved three important objectives: (i) it provided input on the
views and preferences of service users to policy-makers at the local and national level in
Venezuela as well as to the Bank, and this input proved essential in the context of stakeholder
workshops held during project preparation; (ii) by showing user preferences and levels of
satisfaction with the existing service, the study confirmed the appropriateness of the project
emphasis on water supply infrastructure rehabilitation to improve the quality of water and service;
and (iii) by yielding high WTP figures for average monthly prices of 2,000 Bs. and above, the
study showed the financial viability of the project, indeed, the planned tariff increase from the
current 40 to 60 Bs./m3 will amount to an average monthly price of 1,800 Bs. (US$ 10.60)2.
Methodology
7.     Focus Groups and Questionnaire Design. As a first step for the questionnaire design,
three focus group meetings' were held, each with ten to twenty participants from different
neighborhoods corresponding to different socioeconomic backgrounds in the city. Discussions
aimed to collect preliminary information from water and sanitation users on service quality and
level of satisfaction with the service, alternative water supply sources (e.g. private back-up
arrangements), billing and collection procedures, and amounts spent by households on water and
other public services. Participants were also given an opportunity to react to the proposed
institutional and service changes planned under the project, and to trial prices for tariffs to be
charged under the improved scheme. The information resulting from focus group discussion was
used to develop a first draft of the household questionnaire.  In addition, the focus group
discussions comprised a valuable participatory tool to elicit preliminary information on user
preferences, level of satisfaction and willingness-to-pay, which was essential for the discussions
with stakeholders and policy-makers held during project preparation.
2 Using an average household consumption level of 30m3/month.
3 The focus group discussions were facilitated by Donald Lauria (CVM), Isabelle Fauconnier (the World Bank),
and Isbelia de Malave (Doxa).



Annex D
Page 3 of 13
8.    The questionnaire includes five sections: i) general questions about the household's
characteristics;  ii) priorities and preferences of the respondent (list includes transportation,
schooling, water supply and sanitation, marginal areas); iii) current water supply and sanitation
service and practices, iv) willingness to pay questions; and v) socio-economic information about
the respondent's household (e.g., levels of education, occupation, characteristics of the dwelling,
income and assets, expenditures and utility bills).
9.     The WTP questions presented institutional and service improvement scenarios as a basis
for contingent valuation questions inquiring aboutfavoring the scenarios if average monthly cost
to the household was X, and using the improved system at that cost. As a checking mechanism,
the questionnaire also included an open-ended question asking households what would be the
maximuim they would be willing to pay for the improved system. The questionnaire versions
included two different willingness-to-pay scenarios, one that describes improvements only to the
water supply system ("Water Only") and the other that describes improvements to both the water
and sanitation systems ("Water and Sanitation"). The scenario versions, along with the average
monthly bill amounts of 2,000 Bs. (US$11.7), 4,000 Bs. (US$23.4), 7,000 Bs. (US$41.1), or
10,000 Bs. (US$58.8)4, were randomly assigned to respondents. The willingness-to-pay section
is described in detail in paras. 18 to 31.
10.   Sample Design.  Aguas de Monagas and HIDROCARIBE provided their recently
updated city consumer data-base and cadaster, including rough information on socioeconomic
class. This provided a sound basis from which to draw a random sample of 600 households, for a
target figure of usable surveys of approximately 420 after data entry and quality control, a figure
considered robust given the number of questionnaire versions (8).  The sample included
households ranging from upper-class, fully serviced neighborhoods to unserviced marginal areas
and all levels of service in between.
11.   Enumerator training and Pre-testing.  Enumerators hired locally by DOXA were
trained in Contingent Valuation Household Survey methods during three days in Maturin.
Training included mock interviews with the study directors and field training during the
questionnaire pretesting.  Training sessions also were used to improve the organization and
language of the questionnaire. The pretest included approximately 150 households. Only a single
version of the questionnaire with two different prices (2,000 Bs. and 5,000 Bs.) was used. The
pretest provided the final range of average monthly bill amounts to be used in the survey: 2,000
Bs. to 10,000 Bs.
t US$ 1=170 Bolivars



Annex D
Page 4 of 13
Findings
12.   Household Characteristics. Households have an average size of 5.4 persons. Most
households have 2 adults, one of whom works outside the home for pay. The median level of
education is the same for both the head of the house and the spouse: completion of primary
school and some secondary schooling.
All the houses surveyed have electricity and all purchase bottled gas; about half have telephones.
The median monthly payments for light, gas, and telephone are 2,000, 340, and 3,500 Bs.,
respectively. Forty percent of the households have a car, and more than 90% own both a TV and
refrigerator. Average monthly income per household is 62,500 Bs. (US$ 370), and the median
income is 50,000 Bs. (US$ 300). Self-reported incomes spanned a wide range from 6,000 to
400,000 Bs. Income distribution is detailed in table I below.
Table 1: Income categories of sample households
Category              Monthly income (Bs.)          % of households
Low                      < 25,000                       16
Lower middle              26,000 - 50,000                  40
Upper middle               51,000-95,000                   27
High                     > 95,000                       16
13.   Water Supply. In the dry (summer) season, the average number of hours that water
arrives at homes each day is 16; the median is 18 hours. In summer, about half of the households
have water 24 hours per day, and 80% have it for at least 6 hours per day. In the rainy (winter)
season, houses have water from the piped system 22 hours per day on average; 86% have it 24
hours per day; and 95% have it at least 6 hours per day. These data therefore indicate a relatively
high level of water supply from the piped network in comparison with other developing countries.
However, water quality is generally deemed low. The percentages of households rating water
quality as Good, Fair, or Poor during the rainy and dry seasons are as follows:
Table 2: Respondents' perception of water quality
Quality    Rainy Season     Dry Season
Good          16%             22%
Fair         55%             64%
Poor         29%              15%
In the dry season, 30% of households drink water directly from the tap; in the rainy season this
proportion drops to 25%. Hence, water quantity is better in the rainy season than in the dry, but
water quality is better in the dry than in the rainy season. Of the households that do not drink
water directly from the tap, two thirds purify it using a filter, 24% boil it, and the remainder use
both filters and boiling.
14.   Eighty-three percent of the households obtain most of their water from the piped system in
the dry season, and 92% get most of their water from the piped system in the rainy season. Some



Annex D
Page 5 of 13
households get water from tanker trucks and a few obtain it from neighbors. About 30% of
households buy bottled water at least occasionally; about 60% of these use it only for drinking
and the rest for both drinking and cooking. About 40% of households periodically give water to
their neighbors, but do not charge for the water. Virtually no one in the sample reported using a
public standpost or a private well.
15.   Sixty-three percent of the households with a water connection reported receiving a bill
from Hidrocaribe (25% every month, 55% every two months, and 19% once in a while). Most
(58%) said they check their bill for accuracy. Sixty percent of Households reported that they
always paid their water bill, 14% said almost always, 16% said sometimes, and 9% said never. If
true, these responses imply that slightly over one-third of all households in Maturin pay a water
bill. The median payment for the past six months was reported to be 3,000 Bs. (i.e., 500 Bs. per
month), and the average payment was 3,600 Bs. (i.e., 600 Bs. per month). The top ten percent of
households paying the largest bills contributed slightly more revenue than the 50% of households
that paid the median amount or less.
16.   Sanitation. Ninety-three percent of the respondents have a toilet and nearly half have
more than one. Sixty-eight percent of the households are connected to a piped sewer. In the dry
season, 96% of these said that their sewer almost always works well; in the rainy season, this
proportion is 93%. No households are charged for sewerage.
17.   Satisfaction, Priorities and Attitudes. Respondents were asked about satisfaction with
both their water and sanitation systems.  The percentages of households that were "Very
satisfied," "Somewhat satisfied," and "Not satisfied" were as follows:
Table 3: Respondents' satisfaction with current water supply and sanitation services
Satisfaction level         Water supply          Sanitation service
Very satisfied            20%                     53%
Somewhat satisfied           59%                     29%
Not satisfied             21%                     18%
18.   Clearly, households are much more satisfied with their sanitation service than with their
water supply. When given a list of six problems facing Maturin (3 environmental and 3 non-
environmental), improving the water supply was selected by 46% of respondents as the most
important issue to resolve. Twenty-three percent selected sanitation as the top priority, and 11%
selected improvement of solid waste collection. Hence, twice as many households selected
improvements in water supply as compared to sanitation service, which is consistent with the
results shown in Table 3. The three remaining problems (public transportation, education, and
upgrading squatter settlements) were each selected as the top priority by about 7% of
respondents. The list of problems was then reduced to only environmental issues (water supply,
sanitation, and solid wastes), and respondents were asked to select the top priority among them
(Table 4).



Annex D
Page 6 of 13
Table 4: Respondents' perceptions of environmental priorities
Environmental Problem         Percent of Respondents
ranking as first priority
Water supply                      55
Sanitation                       24
Solid Waste Collection                21
19.   Half the respondents think Maturin does not have a wastewater treatment plant. In fact
the city has no plant, indicating that half the residents are not well informed.  Nearly all
respondents think that Maturin sewage is contaminating the environment, is causing problems for
the city and their families, and should be treated. Two-thirds feel that household sewage and
industrial wastes are equally to blame for water pollution.
20.   Willingness to Pay. The willingness-to-pay section of each questionnaire included two
questions. The first, which followed a description of the improved service, posed a hypothetical
vote to the respondent. He or she was asked whether, as a citizen of Maturin, he/she would vote
in favor of or against the proposed improvements. The first question was:
"Suppose that a vote were taken to determine if the people of Maturin are in favor or
not in favor of this project.for improving the ("water" or "water and sanitation")
service in the city. Suppose the average cost that houses in Maturin would have to
pay were X bolivars per month (where X = 2,000, 4,000, 7, 000, or 10, 000). Would
you be in favor of this project or not?"
In the preamble to this question, respondents were told that water meters would be installed so
that payments would be based on the amount of water that households actually use. Hence, the
reference to "average cost" in the above question implies that some households would pay more
than the average and others would pay less. Because the majority of focus group participants did
not know how much water they were using, it was decided that posing willingness-to-pay
questions in terms of average monthly cost would produce more useful information than asking
about the volumetric price they would be willing to pay.
21.   A second question asked the respondent whether his or her household would use the
improved system if it cost them the same monthly price stated in the first question. Specifically, it
asked:
"Now I would like to know what you think the possibility is that you would use the
new ("water" or "water and sanitation ") service I have just described You would not
be obliged to use the new system if you do not want to. However, if you decide not to
use it, your house could not be connected to the ("water" or "water and sewer")
network, and you would have to buy water from your neighbors, or construct your
own well (or build your own septic tank).
"Now I would like to know, if this new system were installed in Maturin and the
average amount your household had to pay were approximately X bolivars per



Annex D
Page 7 ofl3
month, would you use the new system or would you arrange to use some other
system? "
The first question is referred to as "Favor," and the second is referred to as "Use." The
proportion of households that said they favored and would use the Water Only and the Water and
Sanitation improvements are shown in Table 5; a graph of the data is provided in Figure 1.
Table 5: Percent of households that would favor, use improved system
Price                     Water Only               Water and Sanitation
(Bs./month)             Favor           Use          Favor           Use
2,000                  8 1           79             8 1            70

10,000                 31             13            46             20
22.   These results give a general sense of community demand for the proposed improvements.
A comparison of the Water Only with the Water and Sanitation results reveals that the proportion
of respondents who would favor or use the Water and Sanitation program equal or exceed the
corresponding proportion who would favor or use the Water Only program at every price except
one. For example, at a fee of 4,000 Bs. per month, 68% favored the Water Only plan whereas
78% favored the Water and Sanitation plan. These data are logical since for a given price, a
higher percentage of respondents would be expected to want the more complete system of Water
and Sanitation than the Water Only system. In addition, the data in the "Favor" and "Use"
columns of Table 5 show that the percentages decrease as the fees increase, which is as expected.
23.   Several  regression analyses were conducted to fit probit and logit models to the
wvillingness-to-pay data. These models were of the form Y = Bo + B I X I + ... + BnXn, where the
Xs are explanatory (independent) variables, Bo is a constant, and Y is the dependent variable, a
measure of the probability that a household will favor or use the improved system. The first
analysis pooled all the responses to the Favor question for both types of systems, Water Only and
Water and Sanitation. A full model with the following nine explanatory variables was fitted to the
data:
XI = price (i.e., fee to use the system)
X2 = log of income
X3 = education of respondent
X4 = gender of respondent
Xs = number of persons in household
X6 = I if household pays its water bill; 0 otherwise
X7 = I if household is very satisfied with its water system, 2 if somewhat
satisfied; 3 if not satisfied
X8 = I if the household chose either water or sanitation as the first or
second priority problem in Maturin; 0 otherwise
Xg = I if the questionnaire included the Water Only scenario; 0 if it included the
Water and Sanitation scenario

Page 8 of 13
In the probit analysis, monthly fee (XI), the logarithm of respondent's income (X2), respondent's
education (X3), whether the respondent paid his/her water bill (X6), and version of the
questionnaire (X9) were all found to be statistically significant, but the other variables were not.
To examine the robustness of the coefficients of the statistically significant variables to an
alternative model formulation, a reduced model with only these five variables and a constant was
refitted to the data. All five variables were statistically significant, as before, but the constant was
not. The model correctly predicted 68% of the discrete household responses. The regression
result is as follows:

24.   Education and questionnaire version are significant only at the 10% level. The negative
sign of the price coefficient indicates that as price increases, the probability that a household
favors an improved system decreases, as expected. This variable is highly significant (1% level).
The posilive sign of the income coefficient indicates that households with higher income are
more likely to favor an improved system, which is a logical result. The positive sign of the
education coefficient indicates that households whose heads have more education have higher
probability of favoring an improved system, which is as expected The negative sign of the
dummy variable for questionnaire version indicates that households are more likely to favor the
Water and Sanitation scheme than the Water Only project, other things being equal.  The
significance of this variable indicates that households were able to distinguish between the two
different systems that were described to them. It is important to keep in mind that each household
was asked about only one project, either Water Only or Water and Sanitation. Hence, the
households that were asked about the Water and Sanitation project were more favorably disposed
toward it than the households that were asked about the Water Only project.

27.   Questionnaire version was statistically significant in the "Favor" model but not in this
model for "Use." This implies that other things being equal, the probability that a household
would connect to an improved Water and Sanitation system is no greater than the probability that
it would connect to a Water Only system.  This is probably explained by the fact that the
households could distinguish between the two systems, but they did not perceive the sanitation
component as being very important.  This is consistent with the observation that households in
Maturin are much more concerned about water supply than sanitation.
28.   The reduced model for using an improved system was refitted to the pooled Water Only
and Water and Sanitation data using logit analysis. The fitted logit model is:
y = -8.233 - 0.000403*Price + 0.953*ln(Income) + 0.137* Education
- 0.770*Pay Bill - 0.159* Version
The probability (p) that a household will use an improved system is calculated using the equation
p=1/[1 + exp(-y)]. To calculate this probability using the above logit model, Education and Pay
Bill were held at their mean values (3.964 and 0.570, respectively) and Version was held equal to
zero to represent the Water and Sanitation system. The results for incomes in the range 20,000-

Application of findings

29.   Results of the probit and logit analyses are plausible and constitute the heart of this
application. Predicting household use of an improved water and sanitation system produces the
curves in Figure 2. Monthly incomes of 20,000, 50,000, and 140,000 Bs.-- the median incomes
of the high-, middle- and low-income groups, respectively--are used. Assuming the sample is
representative of Maturin households, the proportion of households in each group is one-sixth,
two-thirds, and one-sixth, respectively. With about 46,000 households altogether in Maturin, the
household distribution by income category is as follows: 7,400 are Low Income (median=20,000
Bs./mo), 31,200 are Middle Income (median=50,000Bs./mo), and 7,400 are High Income
(median=140,000). Using the 20,000, 50,000 and 140,000 Bs. curves in Figure 2 based on the
logit model, the probability that households with these incomes will use the improved Water and
Sanitation system at different prices can be calculated (Table 8).

30.   If the expected number of user households from Table 13 is multiplied by a monthly price
for the service, the result represents expected revenue. However, this calculation is based on the
assumption that the monthly water bill of all households in Maturin is the same-- even those in
different income classes. In reality low-income households would have lower water bills because



Annex D
Page 12 of 13
(1) they use less water than middle and high income households, and (2) there is a social tariff
currently in place in Maturin so that low income households pay less per unit of water than
middle- and high-income households. The calculations that follow are thus presented simply for
illustrative purposes as rough approximations.
31.   If the price were set at 1,000 Bs. per month, the expected number of low-income
households that would use the improved system is 5,300, and the amount they would pay is
5,300,000 Bs. per month or 63.6 million Bs. per year (US$374,000/year). At this price, total
revenues would equal 39.0 million Bs. per month, which is equivalent to US$ 2.76 million per
year. If this amount is divided by the total number of households in Maturin (46,000)--rather than
the predicted number of subscribing households--the resulting contribution per household in the
city is about US$ 60 per year. Similar results for all three income groups at different prices are
presented in Table 10.

The bottom three rows of Table 10 show that revenue increases as the price is increased, reaching
a maximum at a price of 5,000 Bs. per month. As the price is further increased, revenue
decreases. The important implication is thus that 5,000 Bs. is the maximum monthly price for
improved water and sanitation service that should be considered for Maturin. At prices below
5,000 Bs., revenue increases with higher prices but coverage decreases. As price is increased
beyond 5,000 Bs., however, both revenue and coverage decrease.
32.   Another significant result from Table 10 concerns households' annual expenditures for
improved water and sanitation. For example, if the price were set at 3,000 Bs. per month, the
average contribution of revenue per household in Maturin would be about US$151.  The
contribution per household served would be about US$212 per year. The pertinent planning
question is whether these amounts would be sufficient to cover debt service and operation and
maintenance costs in order to produce a financially self-sufficient and sustainable system. When
combined with engineering cost estimates, Table 9 includes the data for predicting whether a
suitable project exists for Maturin.

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Management Contract with a Private Operator
I.    Previous Bank involvement with the water supply and sanitation (WS&S) sector in
Venezuela has been very limrited. There is nonetheless a specific experience related to the
preparation of a privatization attempt of the Caracas water system. Although this attempt
was unsuccessful, it provided useful informnation and insight for future private sector
participation initiatives in the WS&S sector. The Caracas experience highlights the three
main factors that influence the delivery of efficient and effective WS&S services: (i) the
need for an adequate regulatory framework that establishes clear roles and responsibilities
among sector agencies; (ii) the existence of appropriate tariff levels and billing/collection
mechanisms that can generate the funds necessary for financial autonomy and signal the
real cost of water to the consumers; and (iii) the need for an efficient and cost-conscious
management that can rationalize expenditures and plan for fluctuations in the stream of
revenues.
2.    HIDROCARIBE, the regional water company that provided WS&S services in the
state of Monagas, had problems in those three areas, which led to a serious deterioration of
service quality in the state. In the context of the proposed project, authorities at the state
and municipal level in Monagas, supported by HIDROVEN, have initiated steps to
decentralize and improve the service. The state's municipalities have formed an association
(Mancomunidad), which together with the state government has created the company Aguas
de Monagas (AdM), a commercial company, that has taken over from HIDROCARIBE the
responsibility for providing WS&S services in the state. AdM is now responsible for the
WS&S services for the entire state of Monagas, with a staff limited to core management in
support of its board of directors and a reduced commercial department.
3.    AdM faces several key challenges related to commercial, financial and technical
efficiency in the management of the service which are critical for its sustainability and for
the achievement of the project objectives. They are: (i) urgently rehabilitating physical
infrastructure seriously deteriorated due to neglected maintenance; (ii) increasing its
revenue stream from a very modest US$1.1 million in 1994 (about US$15 per connection
per year) through better billing and collection procedures; (iii) reducing unaccounted-for-
water levels (currently at 70%); and (iv) improving the operation and maintenance of the
system, which is being inefficiently carried out by local "operators" that supply labor and
equipment under short contracts without any performance requirements.
The Management Contract
4.    In order to address these challenges, it has been agreed that AdM will contract the
management of the services with a qualified and experienced private operator. The



Annex E
Page 2 of 6
management contract would be a first step in the process of involving the private sector in
the delivery of the service, which is expected to result in a concession or lease contract at
the end of the project. This management contract is intended to provide AdM the
flexibility that comes with working outside the public sector, as well as the experience
and working methodology that are required to confront the important transformation
process initiated with the transfer of responsibilities from  HIDROCARIBE.  The
management contract is also intended to improve the impact of the financial support that
will be provided through the project for the rehabilitation of the existing infrastructure.
5.    The management contract constitutes a key element in the set of agreements,
contracts and obligations that have been designed to foster the institutional modernization
and improvement of water and sanitation services in the state of Monagas. Two of these
agreements are directly related to it: the Service Contract and the Contract Plan. The
service contract between the Municipalities and AdM represents the core of this
framework, delegating to AdM the obligation of the municipalities to provide water and
sanitation services, and allowing AdM to deliver this service either directly or through a
private operator. The Contract Plan defines the obligations and responsibilities of AdM
and its shareholders and establishes the operational and efficiency targets that AdM
should achieve. These arrangement are explained in more detail in Annex C.
6.    Under the design that has been adopted for the management contract, the private
operator will be directly responsible for the management of the services and the contracts
with providers, for controlling the commercial activities of AdM and for the
implementation and monitoring of the works and investments to be financed by the
World Bank project. Key tasks to be carried out by the operator include: (i) setting up
macrometering; (ii) development of the commercial and information system; (iii) public
relations with customers; and (iv) review of present contracts with local operators for the
provision of labor and equipment services for the operation and maintenance of the
systems (investments required for the installation of production macrometers, computer
hardware and software for the commercial system, and the development of a public
awareness campaign will be financed under the project). The responsibility of managing
the services also includes the obligatory achievement of the efficiency goals which will
be part of the performance targets of the Contract Plan agreed upon by AdM and its
stakeholders.
7.    Consistent with its objectives, the management contract will incorporate both
economic incentives to reward the private operator for reductions in unaccounted-for-
water, achievement of cost reductions and improvement of collection above the targets
set in the Contract Plan, and financial penalties when these and other performance targets
included in the Contract Plan are not reached. However, the contract has been designed
so that the direct risk taken by the private operator is moderated by fixed monthly
payments, financed by the project as part of the regional institutional component. The
fixed payments will -be conditioned to the successful achievement of the performance
targets adopted as part of the Contract Plan.



Annex E
Page 3 of 6
8.    The performance targets included in the Contract Plan are presented in the
Appendix 1 to this annex. They have been established during project preparation through
consultation with municipal authorities, users and AdM's and HIDROVEN's technical
staff, supported by consultants with international experience in contracts involving
private operators in the management of water supply and sanitation services. The targets
were based on an evaluation of AdM's present conditions and an estimate of realistic
potential for improvement based on the experience of the consultants. The performance
targets will be reviewed after the first two years.
Contract Payment
9.    As mentioned above, the compensation system consists of two components. The
first component will be a fixed monthly payment ("retainer") which will be financed from
the World Bank loan, and will be roughly equivalent to the firm's institution building and
management consulting role. The second component will be a variable incentive
payment, contingent on improvements on the performance targets for unaccounted-for
water and collection efficiency, reflecting the entrepreneurial impact of the operator's
work. This will be financed by the revenue generated for AdM by the improvements
made, with a maximum value limited to fifty percent of the marginal benefit generated.
The incentive part of the payment system is based on the idea of short-run sharing of
benefits obtained from the independent activities of the operator. It will provide
incentives for augmented efficiency, introduce a quasi-risk-effect to the participation of
the operator, and create the possibility of additional benefits in the form of payments for
performance above pre-established targets. Both incentives and penalties will be levied
on an annual basis.
10.   Since the management contract is the first step in the process of involving the
private sector in the delivery of the services in Monagas, and that process will likely lead
to a concession/lease contract at the end of the project, two additional conditions have
been incorporated in the management contract: (i) the winner will not be excluded from
the bidding process for the concession/lease contract; and (ii) strict and transparent
conditions for reporting and accessibility by other firms to the technical and commercial
information of the operation and management of AdM have been included as part of the
Terms of Reference of this contract. Both conditions have been incorporated in order to
avoid market capture by the operator and exclude potential bidders that would be
interested in a longer term type of contract.
Bidding Process
11.   The management contract will be awarded through Competitive Bidding among a
short list of international operators of WS&S services, according to the "mission
statement" of the proponent, the qualifications of the proposed staff (which will be given
significant weight in the bidding process) and the economic terms of the proposal, that



Annex E
Page 4 of 6
should differentiate between the "retainer" and the "incentives" for each of the two
performance targets (unaccounted-for water and collection efficiency). It is expected that
bidders will compensate for any under or over estimation of the performance targets
through their proposed combination of a retainer and the performance incentives.
Weighting for each of the components of the bid (mission statement, team, retainer and
incentives) has been selected with the intention of indicating potential bidders that a
highly qualified management team and some risk taking is expected from the operator to
be selected.
Short-list Preparation
12.   Given the fact that the management contract has been designed to reduce the
direct risk to the operator, the potential of the bidders to achieve high levels of efficiency,
generating rapid benefits to the community and improving the economic-financial aspects
of the services has been one of the key elements considered in the preparation of the
short-list.
13.   A public call for declarations of interest was issued in order to create a short list of
bidders which could adequately address all of the aspects of the private sector
participation model proposed for AdM. From the eleven consortia that expressed their
interest in this contract-, six were selected to be included in the short-list (two British, one
American, one Spanish, one French and one German) according to three sets of criteria:
commercial capacity, technical capacity and technical quality. The main aspects of these
sets of criteria are:
(a)   Commercial Capacity.  All bidders must demonstrate the capacity to
operate a service with a business volume comparable to that of the AdM. As part of this
requirement, annual sales in potable water and sanitation services operated by the bidder
must be over US$ 20 million, and in addition, the company must exhibit a minimum
audited net worth of US$ 20 million as of December 31, 1994.
(b)   Technical Capacity. Operators that only manage potable water services
were excluded from consideration for the management contract and potential bidders
were selected among those that presented experience with the production phase, water
distribution, collection of effluents, and/or treatment plants in urban systems. Business
experience also constituted an important component of technical capacity, and to that end
a series of minimum standards representing the base levels of experience and
management capacity that was considered necessary to manage AdM were requested.
Those minimums standards are:
* minimum total population served of 900,000 inhabitants
* operation of an urban system serving at least 450,000 inhabitants



Annex E
Page 5 of 6
* minimum annual sales of US$ 20 million (referred to water and sanitation
services, including all of the services in which the bidder exhibits
responsibility for overall management)
(c)   Technical Quality. The technical-operative capacity and efficiency with
which the bidders currently manage services under their control were also considered key
conditions. Three parameters were selected to measure the operational aspects of
management and define a minimum level of efficiency:
*  The minimum  collections index should exceed eighty percent, and was
defined as the percentage of total billing for the service that is actually
received by the provider;
* The maximum unaccounted-for-water index should not exceed thirty percent
of production, and was defined as the percentage of water produced that is not
billed to users; and
*  The ability to manage a unit responsible for analyzing potable water quality to
fulfill the requirements of the Venezuelan regulations on this subject.
Loan Conditionality
14.   Terms of Reference and bidding criteria have been prepared by AdM  and
Hidroven with the assistance of consultants. As a condition for negotiations, a call for
bids to the satisfaction of the World Bank was issued by AdM. The signing of the
contract will be a condition of disbursement for the second package of rehabilitatio
investments (see Annex F).

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Rehabilitation Plan for Aguas de Monagas
Background
1.    The quality of water supply and sanitation (WS&S) services in Venezuela, and
particularly in Monagas, is poor, even though more than eighty percent of urban population
has piped water supply and slightly more than sixty percent has sewerage. Water supply is
characterized by widespread rationing, low pressures, questionable water quality, extremely
high unaccounted-for-water (up to 70% according to some estimates) and inadequate
customer service. Waste waters are not treated and raw sewage is discharged into the
receiving waters, polluting surface and groundwater sources.  Despite heavy capital
investment in infrastructure, the system has largely failed to provide reliable and efficient
service.  In general, the financial performance of the sector operating company
(HIDROCARIBE) has been dismal and the quality of service has deteriorated continuously.
Physical losses are very high, billing often does not cover more than a third of the consumers
and collection typically is less than half of the billing. In order to improve these inadequacies
of the water supply and sanitation system a rehabilitation plan has been designed.
Plan Objectives
2.    In light of these water supply and sanitation problems, the two main objectives
contemplated in the Rehabilitation Plan for Aguas de Monagas are: (i) resolving the
problems associated with water services quality; and (ii) increasing the reliability of the
service.  An additional complementary objective to be achieved is improving the
customer's satisfaction and incentives for payment by avoiding disruptions in service, an
event frequently used by users as an excuse for non-payment.
Rehabilitation Investments. Selection of Priorities
3.    A Rehabilitation Plan has been designed to achieve these goals. The selection of the
works has been done according to the following order of prioritiesi:
(a) improve water quality;
(b) provide sufficient water storage capacity for a reliable service provision,
_ _ (c) improve distribution networks and
(d) improve sewerage networks and existing wastewater treatment infrastructure.
'These priorities were not followed only in a few cases where inmmediate attention to wastewater
was necessary for health and aesthetic reasons.



Annex F
Page 2 of 12
4.    The works selected to be financed under this project in conformity with these
priorities are itemized in the tables to this annex, and grouped into three rehabilitation
packages that can be independently completed in their entirety, consisting of: a) emergency
investments; b) an initial package of investments for the first two years; and c) investments
for the final two years of the project, which will be conditioned on the achievement of
contract-plan targets and in particular the agreed financial rate of return and capital
availability. Main characteristics of these three rehabilitation packages and total
investments costs to be financed under this project are summarized in the table I below:
(a) The first rehabilitation package will finance emergency investments required
to avoid the physical collapse of the urban water supply systems, correct the
principal water losses and water quality problem and help lessen acute water
shortages next summer season.  They will also concentrated in the
improvement and rehabilitation of the pumping system, in the interconnection
of the networks and in the provision of basic and reliable disinfection. These
investments will be carried out before project effectiveness and will be
eligible for retroactive financing.   Engineering designs and bidding
documents for these investments have been prepared with the help of a grant
from the Austrian government and funds provided by the state government
and are expected to be ready before negotiations (see table 2).
(b)  The second rehabilitation package will finance priority rehabilitation
investments in the urban area, mainly focused on the reduction of
unaccounted-for-water and the improvement of the system efficiency, that are
expected to have a great incidence in customer's satisfaction and in the
reliability of the service. This second sub-component is expected to be
completed within the first two years of project execution, before the mid-term
review (see table 3).
(c)  The third rehabilitation package will finance the improvement of service
quality in the urban areas and some expansion of coverage in marginal areas.
It will include the rehabilitation and improvement of potable water production
infrastructure, the rehabilitation and expansion of water distribution and
sewerage networks and the rehabilitation and improvement of wastewater
management infrastructure. This third sub-component is expected to be
carried out in the last two years of the project. Its execution will be dependent
on the achievement of the physical, financial and efficiency targets
established for mid-term review (see table 4).

Additional Considerations
5.    Together with the priorities presented above, two main problems that affect
Maturin, the largest consumption center, have been taken into account for the selection of
the investments to be included in the rehabilitation plan: (i) the present supply relays
heavily on the Amana-Maturin system, a complex infrastructure, largely overdimensioned,
that takes water from the Amana river more than 100 km from Maturin and transports it
after treatment through a gravely corroded steel pipe; and (ii) the lack of reliability in the
service is the main cause of customers dissatisfaction and one of the main incentives for
non-payment, apart from having an important impact on water quality.
6.    Which water sources should be used to supply Maturin?   As part of the
analysis made of the supply options for Maturin during project preparation, it has been
determined that the current system that relays heavily on the Amana-Maturin system
should be progressively abandoned in order to reduce dependence on the flow from the
Amana River, which has been greatly reduced in recent years and is subjected to increasing
competing demands (it hardly reaches 600 lls in the summer dry season, while the nominal
capacity of the Amana system is 4 m3/s). The proposed solution is based on the following
criteria:
(a)   The Amana system will be repaired only and where it is strictly necessary to
ensure the continuation of the supply in the short term. Latter, it will be
maintained as is necessary for supplying the town located along the Amana-
Maturin pipe.
(b)   Water drawn from two points on the Guarapiche River will become the
principal source used for the supply of Maturin. The first proposed intake is



Annex F
Page 4 of 12
at the present abstraction point in the Lower Guarapiche, which will be
improved and expanded to produce 1,200 I/sec. The second proposed intake
is located upstream, near Cerro Colorado and as close as possible to the
Alto Guri tank, so that both intakes could be connected to the Alto Guri
tank, which currently receives water from the Amana treatment plant.
Thanks to the additional supply provided by the two intakes on the
Guarapiche, the dependence on the Amana River will be diminished and
eventually eliminated and the present Amana pipeline will be used only to
supply towns upstream from Maturin. The result of this plan will be a
system based- in the State of Monagas with principal installations close to
the capital. Operation will be facilitated and personnel demands for housing
and convenient access to work will be easily satisfied.
(c)   The alternative of a second diversion from the Amana River has been
considered and deemed inadvisable. Technical studies concluded that both
diversions in the Guarapiche River are recommended because the river
waters are regulated and the installations are located nearer to Maturin, the
principal consumption area. Any possible advantage of diversifying the
sources (Guarapiche and Amana instead of two sources from the
Guarapiche) is limited by the fact that the catchment areas of the two rivers
are similar and near one another. Therefore, when negative conditions
(drought, high turbidity) exist in one, the same will hold true in the other
and the benefits associated with diversification become less significant.
7.    How to get better reliability for the system?  Adequate reliability  on the
distribution network requires a greater water storage capacity and the possibility to transfer
water among the different sectors and water supply sources. The Alto Guri tank (30,000
in3) represents the only significant reserve storage capacity of the supply system at the
present time and a minimum of another 30,000 m3 is suggested for the system. Under these
conditions, a new tank located near the Rojo Tank is recommended (La Manga Tank).
Distribution would so come from two sources: (i) the Alto Guri tank, which connects to the
Cerro Colorado treatment plant (as well as the Amana pipeline in the short and medium
term); and (ii) the La Manga tank, which connects to the Lower Guarapiche treatment
plant. With this additional tank, the distribution acquires a very favorable symmetry,
which in the future could be complemented by two additional underground reserve tanks of
30,000 mi3 each.
8.    The optimal operation of the system also requires a connection between the two,
now partially independent, distribution sectors of Maturin, to allow sufficient water
transfers when one of the sources experiences adverse supply conditions. Even without the
La Manga tank at ground level, the Lower Guarapiche treatment plant should be able to
pump into the Rojo tank instead of pumping directly to the network. This connection is
also necessary since it will be necessary to transfer water from Lower Guarapiche to Alto
Guri, because of the expected short-term deficiencies in the Amana supply, so that the
burden of water shortages could be shared across the entire population in the most sensible
way.



Annex F
Page 5 of 12
9.    Due to these conditions, the rehabilitation of the Rojo tank and the main pipeline
from the Rojo tank to the Alto Guri tank has been included as part of the maximum priority
emergency works, substituting the existing small-diameter pipeline and inadequate
reinforcement with the proposed connection pipe between these two tanks.
