VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Rural Rehabilitation and Participatory Management Program
(Programa Participativo de Mejoramiento de Acueductos Rurales - PROPAMAR)
Background'
1.    Unlike many other countries in the region, Venezuela has high rates of coverage for
WS&S services in rural areas. However, the quality of services is poor. It is estimated that 80%
of the rural population has piped water supply through individual house connections and 40% is
connected to a sewerage system. In the state of Monagas, rural water supply coverage is also
high. Sewerage service is uncommon, however, in Monagas' low population density rural areas,
and the majority of residents rely on pit-latrines and septic tank systems for the disposal of
household wastes. Rural water supply systems in the state of Monagas usually consist of a well, a
storage tank and a small distribution system.  They are subject to intermittent service with
prolonged periods of interruption. Moreover, most systems do not treat water for potability and
water quality varies widely among rural communities, leading to adverse health impacts in some
cases. These conditions stem from several critical problems: i) inadequate supervision of well
operators, due to the isolation of rural systems from the central management unit in Maturin; ii)
little or no water treatment or water quality monitoring; iii) poorly designed wells and/or water
systems; iv) insufficient water supplies; and vii) insufficient finding and lack of timely payment
for repairs and maintenance.
2.    All of these problems are rooted in highly centralized and deficitary public management
systems with few, if any, built-in incentives for delivering a reliable and efficient service and no
attempt at cost-recovery.  Rural water and sanitation systems are the responsibility of
municipalities. Historically, however, municipalities have not had the budget resources or the
institutional capacity to assume this responsibility. To fill the void, the Ministry of Sanitation first
and the state government later have provided informal yet systematic technical and financial
support to rural water systems in recent years. Today, ninety percent of the 206 rural water
systems in the state of Monagas are managed by the State Public Works Department (Obras
Publicas del Estado - OPE), and the remaining ten percent are under the responsibility of
HIDROCARIBE, the regional water company. The OPE systems are generally small, each
covering about 100 households. They are usually run by individually contracted operators who
are poorly trained and have disincentives to travel to the capital to report on problems, since they
must pay travel costs out of their own salary.  HIDROCARIBE - Monagas contracts out the
operation of the rural systems to five operating companies, each covering a separate region of the
This background section is based on a report on institutional arrangements for water supply and sanitation in
rural and peri-urban areas in the state of Monagas Monagas, prepared by Gabrielle Watson, from Vanasse
Hangen Brustlin, Inc. (VHB), in February 1995.



Annex G
Page 2 of 15
state (about 800 households on average each). In many cases, paradoxically, the state's poor
performance has led municipalities to re-assume some of their initial service responsibility and
give ad-hoc support by providing emergency water supply or funding to conduct repairs.
3.    The sustainability of rural water systems is all the more precarious that in the present
organizational framework, communities are virtually not involved in the operation and
maintenance of water and sanitation services.  Although communities participated in water
services management in the 1960s, generous Government subsidy programs beginning in the
1970s for basic services such as housing, health, water, sanitation and even electric power have
encouraged communities to take a more passive role in defining and meeting their service needs.
Recent government budget cuts and the resulting deterioration of basic services has increased
communities' dissatisfaction with service levels while raising their awareness of government
funding limits. Moreover, discussions with rural community representatives have indicated that
communities would be interested in a scheme involving them more in the management of their
WS&S services. A relatively high level of organization at the community level provides a
favorable basis for a community based service management scheme: communities in rural areas
are currently organized in functional groupings such as neighborhood associations (juntas de
vecino.s), farmer associations (sindicatos agrarios), and women's groups (amas de casa). In
addition, since 1989, groups of communities are represented by Parish Committees (juntas
parroquiales), which are elected in parallel with the municipal government2. In order to develop
a community-based management system for rural water supply, the proposed loan will include a
rural subcomponent which will support the Rural Rehabilitation and Participatory Management
Program (Programa Participativo de Mejoramiento de Acueductos Rurales - PROPAMAR) to
assist in the rehabilitation of rural water supply infrastructure, and to pilot decentralized and
participatory management of rural water systems in the state of Monagas.
Objectives and Strategy
4.    Objectives. The objectives of the rural subcomponent are to i) improve the quality of
water supply service in rural areas; ii) improve the sustainability of rural water systems through
better operation and maintenance and cost-recovery practices by the communities themselves; and
iii) pilot a new participatory management model that could be replicated in the rest of the country.
5.    Strategy.  Growing fiscal demands on the central and regional governments have
prompted a search for alternatives to the heavily subsidized and centralized water supply and
sanitation management scheme for rural areas in Venezuela. In the state of Monagas, state and
municipal authorities have decided to test a new participatory approach to increase the rural
population's access to good quality water services through community-generated and managed
water supply projects, with support form the central Government. The proposed approach also
2 The Venezuelan Organic Law for Municipal Administration states that "Parishes will be auxiliary entities of the
municipal government and of local participation, through which residents will collaborate in the management
of community matters" (Chapter V, Article 34). To date, Monagas Parish Committees have acted primarily as
conduits of demands from rural and peri-urban communities, though carrying out projects or monitoring
senrice conditions are clearly functions within their constitutional scope.



Annex G
Page 3 of 15
seeks to control investment and maintenance expenditures through improved cost-recovery, and
increase the efficiency of investment.
6.    The strategy of the state and municipal authorities relies on the following key principles:
*  the government will provide low per capita investment grants to ensure a basic level of
service for all rural residents; the basic level of service is defined as potable water
supply through individual house connections that will meet the daily human
consumption need, set at 150 liters/capita/day by the Venezuelan authorities using
guidelines from the World Health Organization.
*  communities will finance the cost difference between a higher level of service they may
choose and the basic level of service;
* investment decisions will be based on community demand;
* communities will take over the management of water service operations and
maintenance, and the responsibility for cost-recovery.
7.     Preparation Work3. During project preparation, the following studies were carried out
with support from the Japanese Policy and Human Resources Development Grant: i) an analysis
of the institutional framework and the distribution of responsibilities for rural water supply4; ii) a
diagnosis of the current infrastructure and rehabilitation needs, including detailed cost estimates5;
and iii) a survey of approximately 800 rural households to assess rural residents' willingness to
pay and willingness to participate in the management of rural water supply services6. In addition,
the project preparation team held consultative meetings with leaders of several rural communities
and mayors of municipalities to discuss current problems and the communities' interest in taking
over the operation of and cost-recovery for their WS&S systems. Results from the willingness-
to-pay study indicate that rural residents are primarily concerned with water quality and its impact
on health. The study predicts that 60% of respondents would be willing to pay Bs 2,000
(US$11.77) per month and over 50% would pay 3,000 (US$17.6) per month for improved service
providing safely potable water. These results are significant given that rural users are currently
not billed at all for their water service.  Moreover, 56%  of respondents believe that their
community would want to run its own water system, 77% of respondents were somewhat or very
interested in serving in a water committee, and 58% thought that the community would do a
better job than government in managing the water system. A summary of the results of the study
is presented in Appendix 1.
Description of the Program

8.    The subcomponent, also called PROPAMAR, would finance investment and technical
assistance for the improvement of water supply service and management capacity at the
community level. The investments will include civil works, equipment, goods and materials for
the rehabilitation, improvement and some expansion of water systems in rural areas to provide a
basic level of service which is defined as: (i) a service connection for every household; (ii) water
supply service to ensure the availability of 150 liters per capita per day, and (iii) supply of potable
water (i.e., disinfected). Rural WS&S systems in approximately 85 communities covering about
45,000 rural inhabitants (about 50 % of the state's rural population) are expected to be financed
over four years under this subcomponent. Two subsidy ceilings would be set: the first, amounting
to approximately $56 per capita, would apply to about 90% of communities, which have a water
system that needs to be improved; the second, amounting to approximately $180 per capita,
would apply to about 10% of communities where no water supply infrastructure currently exists.
A detailed estimate of investment needs is presented in Appendix 2.
9.    The technical assistance will finance the coordination and administration of PROPAMAR
by AdM, and it will enable AdM to assist communities in organizing, designing and executing
investments, and building management capacity. Specifically, the subcomponent would finance
the services of a non-governmental organization (NGO)8 to assist communities in: (i) setting up a
water committee; (ii) examining technical options to meet different service levels, and the cost-
sharing implications of investment and operation and maintenance for these options; (iii)
organizing and managing their water systems, (iv) preparing a financing plan and financial
management scheme for proposed investments and subsequent 0 & M of the system; and (v)
raising awareness on water resource issues. The NGO would also carry out: (i) training through
workshops on management and accounting and cost recovery for water committee
representatives; (ii) a certification program for water system operators; and (iii) an information,
dissemination and awareness campaign using local media sources (radio, newspapers) as well as
existing community fora to promote the Government's strategy for managing rural water systems
and the proposed program; and (iv) capacity building for municipalities to take over technical
assistance to communities once the NGO withdraws.
Cost and Implementation Plan

and taxes (US$.24 million for equipment purchases). Costs for works and equipment are based on
an average per capita investment of about US$70 ($12,000 Bolivares) for a variety of technology
options resulting from the rural infrastructure diagnostic study prepared as part of project
preparation. The estimated cost of the proposed technical assistance activities is based on the
scope of work indicated in the activity's terms of reference and on consultant and management
fees for similar work carried out in Venezuela.

presentation of its proposed investment project and cost-sharing plan (see Community Water

13.   Institutional arrangements. PROPAMAR will be implemented over a four-year period.
In the first year, a small number of communities will be targeted for program promotion on the
basis of the demand analysis yielded by the willingness-to-pay study for the rural areas, and of the
interest in the program expressed by the corresponding communities and mayors. In the following
three years, continued demand assessment and outreach to communities will enable communities
and mayors to come forward and express their intention to participate in the program.
14.   AdM will be the executing agency for PROPAMAR. To channel investment and technical
assistance financing, AdM will create a trust fund and act as its trustee. AdM will contract a trust
fund coordinator and necessary support staff to supervise the program on a full time basis. The
World Bank will approve the operating procedures of the trust fund.
15.   To manage the day-to-day operation of the program and provide technical assistance to
communities, a well-qualified Non Governmental Organization (NGO) or consultant will be hired
under Bank financing. The contract will be for a period of 2 years. At the end of two years it will
be either extended or terminated, according to the NGO's performance to be evaluated by AdM
after 18 months. In the case where the contract is terminated, another NGO would be contracted
for the remaining two years of the project. The Venezuelan authorities have prepared terms of
reference for the NGO's work that are satisfactory to the World Bank. They have also presented
to the Bank a draft Operating Manual - as a condition of negotiations - which must be revised and
completed as a condition of disbursements. Other conditions of disbursement for this
subcomponent include the signing of a contract with an NGO for the execution of PROPAMAR.
and the initial capitalization of the Trust Fund.
16.   Implementation Responsibilities. AdM will be responsible for:
*  Supervising the work of the NGO, and preparing quarterly reports on the program's
performance for the Board of Directors in AdM;
*  Approving Community Water Action Plans (COWAPS) at the level of the Board of
Directors, on the recommendation of the NGO,
*  Aministering procurement and disbursements from the trust fund.
17.   The NGO will be responsible for:
* Preparing and executing the information campaign to promote PROPAMAR and
explain eligibility and participation rules;
* Identifying communities and municipalities that are willing to participate in the
program;

diagnostic of problems and identification of technical solution options with
community; and iii) preparation of COWAPs,
* Evaluation of COWAPs to be funded under project guidelines, and recommendation to
AdM for approval;
* Preparation of Terms of Reference for works corresponding to each COWAP, in
consultation with communities;
* Once works are initiated, continued assistance to communities in supervising works,

communities during program implementation, and to take over technical assistance

years.

* supervising the works;

cost-recovery functions.

Summary of Steps in Implementation for PROPAMAR Sub-projects
Eight steps will be followed for the implementation of a community water project under the proposed program:
1. The NGO promotes the program and raises awareness on water issues through direct media, thtough
mayors and juntas parroquiales in the municipalities, and through community leaders. Duration: I month.
2. The interested community forms a water committee, either as a new association or within an existing
community organization structure. The water cormmittee receives assistance from the NGO to promote the
program wNithin the community. Duration: 1.5 months.
3. The water committee, with assistance from the NGO, collects a commitment fee (500 Bs., equivalent to
US$3) from each household, to be used as part of the community's contribution to investment costs. The fee
ensures commitment and motivation from the community to pursue the program. The water committee
deposits the commitment funds directly into the rural program trust fund. Duration: I month.
4. Once the commitment fee is secured in the trust fund, the NGO formally begins technical assistance to:
the water committee to: i) strengthen its planning and management capacity; and ii) prepare the COWAP.
Duration: 3 months.
5. The Water Committee presents its COWAP for the NGO's clearance and recommendation for approval
to AdM. Along with the COWAP, the community presents the remainder of its contribution to investMent
costs (cash, tools. and/or letter of commitment from water committee to mobilize a certain quantity of
community labor). For the COWAP to be formally considered, the municipality must deposit its share of
investment costs into the trust fimd. Duration: 0.5 month.
6. If necessary, the COWAP is modified or improved to reach complete compliance with program conditions..
Once AdM approves the COWAP, it can begin the contracting of works. The community water comrnmittee
gives the no-objection for the signing of contracts. Duration: 1.5 months.
7. The water committee is actively involved in the execution and supervision of works. Throughout, it is
receiving T.A. to take over the management and operation of the water system. Duration: 3 months.
8. The water committee begins managing its water system, and it receives continued T.A. from the NGO
and the municipality. An evaluation of the community water project is carried out after 6 months, to assess: i)
the effectiveness of the rehabilitation works; and ii) the functioning of the water committee as provider of
service.
Throughout steps I to 7, municipalities are building up capacity to take over the NGO's T.A. role once the NGO]>
withdraws, at the end of its contract.



Annex G
Page 9 of 15
22.   Eligibility criteria.  Communities will learn about the program, its conditions and
eligibility criteria through outreach and promotion activities coordinated by the NGO. Once a
community decides to participate, its newly formed water committee, with support from the
NGO, will collect a flat commitment fee from community households, for a total amount
equivalent to 500 Bs. (approximately US$3) per household for at least 80% of the households in
that community. The commitment funds collected will be deposited directly into the trust fund, to
indicate the community's willingness to participate in the program and receive technical assistance
in the preparation of a COWAP. The funds will be used as part of the community's contribution
towards investment cost-sharing.
23.   To be eligible for investment funding through the program, communities should fulfill the
following criteria:
i) The community will have formed a water committee to manage a) the preparation of
the Community Water Action Plan (COWAP), the contents of which are detailed below
and b) the water system itself, once improved, including cost-recovery activities,
ii) The water comittee should agree to cover all routine operations and maintenance costs
for the system, once it is improved;
iii) The water committee will have prepared a COWAP; in order to ensure complete
community commitment, the COWAP will be signed by at least 80 % of households
before it is acceptable for submission to the selection committee.
24.   Content of Community Water Action Plan (COWAP). The COWAPs will detail:
a) the nature and cost of investments planned, including engineeering designs and the
anticipated improvements yielded by those investments;
b) the amount raised by the municipality and the community for 15% investment cost-
sharing; in addition, the amount raised by the community for investment above the subsidy
level, if any.
c) the planned scheme for operation and maintenance of the system, whether by the
community itself (through a community-selected operator), or through a hired contractor,
d) the planned financial plan and management scheme to cover routine operation and
maintenance costs and an increasing part of major maintenance and future capital costs.
This would include the proposed mechanism for tariff collection at the community level;
e) the community association that will hold responsibility for WS&S management
activities. This section will describe: i) how and when the association was established; ii)
its composition (number and gender of members, division of responsibilities); and iii) what



Annex G
Page 10 of 15
its training needs are, if any; the existence of a body to manage the sub-project at the
community level will be a condition for funding.
Participating communities will receive technical assistance from the NGO or Consultant in
coordination with AdM and the municipalities (through the juntas parroquiales and the appointed
staff member) to improve their management and decision-making capacity and to prepare
COWAPs, including the technical/design work.
25.   COWAP Selection and approval. The NGO/Consultant will evaluate the COWAPs on
a first-come, first-served basis and make recommendations for approval or modification to AdM,
which, with involved municipalities, will select a number of proposals corresponding to the funds
available for that year. Complete procedures will be detailed in the operational manual for the
program.
The approval of proposals will be made on the basis of the following criteria:
- technical soundness, cost-feasibility and cost-effectiveness of proposed works and
improvements;
- evidence of compliance with the financial conditions described above, i.e. i) deposit of
community commitment fee in the trust fund account to allow the onset of technical
assistance activities; and ii) deposit of community and municipality cost-sharing funds
(1 5% of total basic investment cost plus amount above basic investment cost) in the trust
fund account;
26.   In principle, once the community has reached the COWAP presentation stage, its chances
of receiving funding will be high. Adjustments and revisions of the COWAP may be necessary,
and will be carried out with assistance from the NGO or Consultant. The Bank would review the
first five COWAPs before final approval, and subsequent reviews would be done on an ex-post
basis.
27.   Technical Assistance to Communities during and after COWAP Implementation.
During construction the community, through its water committee, and the NGO with the
municipality will supervise the works.  The water committee will also set up the financial
management scheme and tariff collection mechanism to be implemented once the improved system
is functioning. It will select an operator (or may keep the one already in place, if his/her service
has been satisfactory) to operate the system, on a competitive basis. The operator will undergo a
short training and certification program organized by the NGO or Consultant and AdM for all
new and continuing water system operators in the communities involved in the program. After
the works are completed, the community will be fully responsible for implementing its water
systems management scheme, including cost-recovery activities.
28.   Support to the communities would include a yearly inspection of the infrastructure, a
review of the tariffs charged in the community and of management practices, and assistance in
large maintenance works. In the first year after works are completed the NGO/Consultant would



Annex G
Page 11 of 15
provide this yearly technical consultancy at a nominal fee, which will be defined in the operational
manual to be presented to the bank as a condition of effectiveness.  It will also train the
municipality to take over this task thereafter. The municipality would continue to charge a
nominal fee for the yearly consultancy to cover part of its costs.
Procurement and Disbursement
29.   Procurement will follow Bank procedures. It is anticipated that contracts for works and
goods will be below US$250,000 and US$50,000, respectively, allowing for local shopping
procedures with at least three quotes. AdM will be responsible for procurement and will operate
in consultation with the water committees, who will give their no-objection to the signing of
contracts and who will instruct AdM to make payments when works/tasks are completed to the
water committees' satisfaction. Where possible and convenient, purchases may be grouped to
allow for local competitive bidding. The Bank will disburse loan funds upon review of all
procurement documentation for community investment projects, presented in groupings to
diminish transaction costs. Bank review of procurement documentation will be done ex-ante for
the first ten community projects, and ex-post thereafter.

3 1.   The performance of the program will additionally be measured through annual audits of

i) whether the improved water systems supply potable water at the set objective of 150

stemming from the performance audit. The report will detail the problems as well as the
achievements of the program to allow for a constructive learning process and program
adjustment, if necessary. The report will be submitted to the Bank for review, comment, and
action, if needed, to improve the program's implementation mechanisms. In addition, a special
report on this subcomponent will be prepared for the mid-term review of the project.
33.   Financial Audits. During project preparation, the accounts to be set up and auditing

separate accounts for all program expenditures involving the use of Bank loan proceeds. These
accounts, as well as the trust fund account for PROPAMAR, will be audited annually by external
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank. The audits will be carried out in conjunction with
the annual financial audit for the overall project, procedures for which are detailed in the main text
of the Staff Appraisal Report. All audit costs will be financed by AdM.

Appendix 1
Summary of Preliminary Results of the
Willingness-to-Pay study in
Rural Areas of the State of Monagas
1. In July 1995, a willingness-to-pay survey was carried out in a sample of 15 rural communities in the State
of Monagas, covering approximately 900 households in three zones of economic activity: the cattle region, the
petroleum region, and the farning region. Focus group meetings were held with ten to twenty residents each,
to obtain preliminary information on current water supply conditions, satisfaction with the service, willingness
to pay for improvements in the service, and interest of users in running their own community water system.
This allowed for a first draft of the questionnaire to be prepared and subsequently tested in a pilot survey. The
final questionnaire included six different versions, corresponding to two different scenarios for water
improvement and three different average monthly prices per household for each scenario. The first scenario
(Option 1) proposed an improvement of water quantity to guarantee at least 12 hours of water each day.
Prices for this option were Bs 500, Bs 1,000, and Bs 2,000. The second scenario was presented as more
expensive than Option I and proposed an improvement of water quantity and quality to guarantee safely
potable water for 24 hours per day. Prices for this option were Bs 1,000, Bs 2,000 and Bs 4,000.
2. The survey showed that water supply is by far the highest priority for the sample households compared to
other community problems listed (education, streets, etc.). Only 16% of respondents receive water 24 hours
per day in the summer and 31% in the winter, yet the quality of water and its impact on health was of more
concern than its available quantity, which was deemed reasonably satisfactory. Ncvertheless, the majority of
respondents drink water directly from the tap (73% in the summer and 67% in the winter). The rest boil it,
filter it, or "purify" it by settling the turbidity in a storage container. Household incomes were low overall,
with 25% at a median of Bs 16,000/month, 50% at a median Bs 32,000/month, and 25% at a median of Bs
70,000/month. This explains why only 9% of the respondents buy bottled water, with an average monthly
expenditure of Bs 1,600 in the summer and Bs 1,400 in the winter.
3. The study concluded that willingness to pay in rural areas of the state of Monagas is quite high, with 80%
of the respondents willing to use the system with Option 2 improvements at an average monthly price of Bs
2,000 (US$ 140 per year). Seventy-five percent of respondents would pay Bs 3,000 per month (US$ 210 per
year). These amounts are of the same magnitude as those charged for well-run systems in industrialized
countries. Almost two-thirds of respondents chose Option 2 over Option I and over 80% of those respondents
said they would use it and pay the corresponding average monthly fee. Demand for improvement was lowest
in the agricultural zone. The econometric analysis showed that major determinants of willingness to pay for
Option I were income, education, the cost to users of Option 2, satisfaction with existing water supply service,
and the desire for operating the improved system by the community itself. All five variables had the expected
coefficient signs for Option 2 and were statistically significant at the 1% level, indicating that survey results
are highly plausible.
4. The survey also asked sample households about their current participation in water supply management
and their interest in becoming more involved. Only 11% of respondents said their community made repairs on
its own, while the remainder stated that outside assistance was usually sought. Lack of funds and knowledge
for repairs were given as reasons to explain reliance on outside sources. However, 27 % of respondents stated
that it was highly probable that their community could run its own system, and 41 % said it could maybe do so.
The majority (56%) said that the community would want to run its own system, and 58% thought the service
would be better as a result. Fifty-nine percent thought that it would not be too difficult to serve on the
community's water committee, and 77% were somewhat or very interested in doing so.

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
National Institutional Component
Background
1.    The water supply and sanitation (WS&S) sector in Venezuela has undergone a
major restructuring over the past five years.  In spite of these efforts, the results have
proven insufficient and the sector as a whole remains "de facto" centralized with a complex
organizational structure. Many of the water-related agencies suffer from ill-defined and
overlapping statutory responsibilities and an undeveloped regulatory framework, private
sector participation remains limited and federal subsidies for operating costs as well as for
capital investment are required due to low tariffs, inadequate commercial practices and
inefficient administration that commonly result in deficient metering, partial billing and low
collection. Water resources management and wastewater management are also centralized
and the few experiences of management at the river basin level have remained fully
dependent on the central government and lacked financial viability.
2.    The current administration has given high importance to the water and sanitation
sector because of the acute problems in service delivery and the heavy fiscal burden of
present centralized management. The Government's policy for the sector, articulated in a
letter to the Bank, has as its key elements: i) accelerating the decentralization process and
building local institutional capacity for management; ii) developing a modem institutional
and regulatory framework; iii) improving cost-recovery and achieving financial self-
sufficiency; iv) increasing sector investment to an average annual 4% of total public
investment, concentrating on rehabilitation, expanded sewerage and treatment coverage and
greater access by the poor to WS&S services; and v) improving efficiency and involving the
private sector in service delivery. The planned rehabilitation investment projects at the
estate and regional levels, including this project in the state of Monagas, will be
supported by the building of national and local institutional capacity and the improving of
technical and managerial skills, regulation, operation and maintenance of the services.
3.    The proposed modernization and improvement of the WS&S services in
Venezuela require: a) a sound regulatory framework and the strengthening of the national
water related agencies; b) sector development and decentralization at the regional level,
replicating the Monagas experience in other states, and c) improvement of sector wide
management and technical capacity. Similarly, the introduction of national standards to
be developed under the project and the adoption of state and municipal regulations and
new commercial rules will demand specialized support to fulfill the legal roles and a
better social understanding of the new  sectoral reality.  For these reasons, the



Annex H
Page 2 of 23
Government has requested the support of the Bank for institution building in the sector in
the context of the proposed project. The project's National Institutional Component,
which will be co-financed with the InterAmerican Development Bank (IDB), includes
financial support to assist the Government in carrying out its sectoral strategy.
Program Objectives
4.    To assist and promote the modernization of the Venezuelan water supply and
sanitation sector, the National Institutional Component will finance consultant services
and equipment to HIDROVEN, in order to:
(a)   assist in the adoption and development of a Regulatory Framework
proposal;
(b)   create and/or strengthen national sector institutions;
(c)   increase technical and managerial capacity at the national, as well as local
level to regulate, plan and operate these services;
(d)   stimulate a broad social support for the modernization of WS&S, and the
rational use of the water resources;
(e)   prepare training and dissemination material; and
(f)   facilitate the preparation of four projects, to duplicate the model developed
in Monagas.
Content of the National Institutional Component
5.    The National Institutional Component will support three different programs:
Technical Assistance Program, TAP
National Training Program, NTP
*     Sector Decentralization Program, SDP
6.    The Technical Assistance Program will assist in the implementation of the
Regulatory Framework which is currently under design with support from a PHRD Grant
from the Government of Japan and the Bank. It will finance consultant services and
equipment for:
(a)   institutional strengthening of national sector institutions (HIDROVEN and
the regulatory agency or agencies to be defined), including: (i) a study to
define the organizational structure of the sector according to the proposed
Regulatory Framework;  (ii) technical assistance for the Regulatory
Agencies and HIDROVEN and (iii) implementation of a Management
information system in HIDROVEN and the preparation of an inventory of
basic sectoral information needed to plan, regulate and provide technical
assistance to sector institutions;



Annex H
Page 3 of 23
(b)   preparation of sectoral norms and standards; and
(c)    development and implementation of general policies for: (i) management
of shared water resources and related infrastructure; (ii) management of
rural water systems and (iii) institutional organization and promotion of
private sector participation.
(d)   development and implementation of a national Management Information
System for the sector
7.    Additionally, this subcomponent will finance studies, equipment and related
consulting services to support the design, creation and functioning of a Users
Hydrographic Confederation (CH) for the management of water resources in the Amana
and Guarapiche river basins, in the state of Monagas, as a demonstration case. The design
will be based on the idea that effective water resources management is best achieved
through an organization that: (i) is given the authority to establish and implement a water
resources plan; (ii) meets the goal of financial sustainability; and (iii) is locally based,
represents the users and stakeholders in a river basin and responds and is financially
dependent from them. Since the main role of the CH will be to develop and implement
management criteria and principles to plan for the allocation and protection of water
resources in this area, this sub-component will also provide support to the CH for the
development of a basic information systems for these river basins and for the preparation
of this management plan and for setting up the necessary institutional capacity to
implement it. Preliminary work for the design of the CH has been carried out with the
help of a grant from the Spanish government.
8.    The National Training Program will:
(a)   increase technical capacity at the national and local government level in
order to promote the implementation of a modem regulatory and
institutional framework;
(b)   improve technical and managerial skills of professionals and decision
makers of the WS&S sector; and
(c)   stimulate appreciation and understanding among society of the importance
of rational use and conservation of water resources.
9.    The Sector Decentralization Program will finance the preparation of four new
full rehabilitation projects replicating the model developed in Monagas. The Program will
include:
(a)   preparation of investment plans for the physical rehabilitation of the
systems and



Annex H
Page 4 of 23
(b)   technical, legal, institutional and financial studies necessary to define the
decentralization projects according to the model developed in Monagas.
10.   These three programs are presented in detail in Appendices 1, 2 and 3 to this
annex.
Component Implementation Arrangements.
11.   HIDROVEN would be responsible for carrying out the National Institutional
Component through a sub-project management unit. This unit will be created under the
supervision of HIDROVEN's Presidency and will be responsible for:
(a)   preparing final proposals and Terms of Reference for the Program's
activities and their submission for the Bank's approval;
(b)   procurement of consultant services and equipment needed for the
execution of each Program;
(c)   supervising and evaluating the consultants and services provided;
(d)   preparing progress reports to be presented to Bank's and contracting
yearly and overall evaluation studies of this component;
(e)   administering the component's execution;
(f)   providing support and supervision for the execution of the regional
rehabilitation project in Monagas and for the sector decentralization
program in four additional regional water companies; and
(g)   overall project coordination and monitoring.
12.   The unit will be under the direction of a General Manager. It will be staffed with
specialists hired as consultants to advise the General Manager on legal and institutional
development issues.  Additional staffing for project management assistance to the
General Manager will be provided through a consultant services contract that will include
professional and support services and related equipment needs. Financing of these
consultant services will be provided through the Bank loan at an estimated cost of US$
480,000 per year, to be contracted on a bi-annual basis. Submission to the Bank of terms
of reference for the management assistance contract was a condition of negotiations. The
signing of these contracts will be a condition of effectiveness. Agreements were reached
during negotiations on: (i) the personnel and expertise that HIDROVEN should maintain
in the sub-project management unit; and (ii) the content of the support to be provided
through the hiring of specialized consultant and through the consultant service contract
for project management assistance, which should be hired and maintained by
HIDROVEN.
13.   The component's Programs will be revised on a yearly basis for the purposes of
implementing any necessary adjustments and for monitoring purposes. Yearly (after the
second year) and overall monitoring studies will be contracted by the sub-project



Annex H
Page S of 23
management unit to specialized consultants to monitor each sub-component development
and the achievement of the specific objectives specified in this annex.
Component Cost.
14.   The total estimated cost for the National Institutional Component is US$
14.000.000. US$ 4.580.000 would correspond to the implementation of the Technical
Assistance Program (of which US$ 2,050,000 correspond to the studies for the creation
and support of the Users Confederation in Monagas), US$ 3.210.000 for training
activities (courses, seminars, dissemination events, public awareness campaign and
documents production), US$ 4.200.000 for the support of sector decentralization and US$
2.010.000 for the financing of the consultant services support to the sub-project
management unit and the evaluation studies. Table I summarizes the objectives, goals,
instruments and costs of the National Institutional Component to be supported under the
Project.



Annex H
Page 6 of 23

Appendix-1
TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE PROGRAM                           --
Program objectives
1.    The Technical Assistance Program will: (i) assist in the implementation of the
Regulatory Framework which is currently under design with support from the Bank; (ii)
assist in the creation and/or strengthening of the national institutional sector organizations
and regulatory agencies defined according to the regulatory framework proposed and (iii)
implement a management information system needed to plan, coordinate, regulate and
provide technical assistance to all sector institutions and develop general policies and
sectoral norms and standards.
Program activities
2.    The specific activities to be supported under the TAP include the financing of
consultant services and equipment for:
(a)   Institutional strengthening of national sector institutions (HIDROVEN
and/or the mational regulatory agency or agencies to be defined). This
sub-program would support:
(i)    A study that will define the organizational structure of the sector
according to the proposed Regulatory Framework, designing: (1)
the Regulatory Agencies at both central and regional levels,
including organizational structure and budgetary, technical and
human resources so as to ensure their proper functioning along
with an action plan for their creation and implementation and (2)
the organizational structure of HIDROVEN in such a way that it is
able to carry out its functions of planning, financial support and
promotion of decentralization and modernization of the sector.
(ii)   Technical assistance for the Regulatory Agencies and HIDROVEN
in the design and implementation of policies and in administrative
procedures in the following matters: (1) budget formulation and
control, (2) human resources policy, and (3) accounting and
finances.
(iii)   Implementation  of a management information  system  in
HIDROVEN to strengthen its capacity to monitor technical and
economic aspects of WS&S services delivery. It will also include



Annex H
Page 8 of 23
the preparation of an inventory of basic sectoral information
needed to plan, regulate and provide technical assistance to sector
institutions.
(b)   Preparation of sectoral norms and standards. This sub-program would
support the preparation of technical standards applicable to the sector. It
would also support the preparation of model by-laws and regulations that
would be needed by states and municipalities to complement the national
regulatory framework, including those dealing with the design and
execution of investment projects, operation and maintenance of water
supply services, service quality and users rights and responsibilities.
(c)   Development and implementation of general policies for:
(i)    Management of shared resources.  This would finance:  (i)
preparation of an inventory of shared resources (available water
resources, present uses and estimated demand, infrastructure for
the production and treatment of potable water and the management
of wastewaters) and  (ii) development of a model for the
institutional framework, financing and regulations necessary for
the decentralized management of these resources.
(ii)    Management of rural water systems. This would finance: (i)
preparation of an inventory (including an identification of the
different types) of rural water systems; (ii) development and of
various models for operation and maintenance, community
participation and financing for future investments; and (iii)
execution of a national willingness-to-pay study for rural areas.
(iii)    Promotion of private sector participation. This would finance a
study of institutional arrangements possible at a regional level in
order to promote the creation of local water companies and the
development of a general policy for the promotion of private sector
participation in the management of WS&S services, including the
preparation of models contracts, regulations and bidding
documents and procedures for: (i) concessioning complete water
and sewerage systems;  (ii)  performance-based management
contracts and operation contracts; and (iii) maintenance contracts,
including appropriate supervision and control procedures.
(d)   Studies and support for the design, creation and functioning of a Users
Hydrographic Confederation for the management of water resources, as a
demonstration case in the Amana and Guarapiche river basins in the State
of Monagas.



Annex H
Page 9 of 23

3.    The total cost of the TAP is estimated at US$ 4.580.000. The financing of
individual consultants and firms would amount to US$ 2.250.000. US$ 280.000 have
been budgeted for the purchase of computer equipment and software needed for the
execution of the Program. Table 2 summarizes the specific goals, instruments and costs
of the TAP.

Alppendix 2
NATIONAL TRAINING PROGRAM
Program objective
1.    The modernization process of the WS&S service in Venezuela must overcome
two main human resources impediments. The first one is related to the lack of technical
and managerial skills of decision-makers responsible for sector's regulations, norms and
policies and the managers and technical personnel responsible for the operation and
administration of the services. The second impediment to be overcome has to do with the
citizens' awareness with regard to the necessity of rational use of water resources and
their commitment to pay in accordance to the quality of the service.
2.    The main purpose of the proposed National Training Program (NTP) would be to
overcome impediments to the modernization of Venezuela's water sector. The NTP has
three objectives:  (i) to build support for the new regulatory framework and the
Venezuelan modernization process; (ii) to train a nucleus of professionals and decision-
makers in basic technical and managerial concepts of WS&S services; and (iii) to
stimulate appreciation and understanding of the importance of rational use and
conservation of water resources.
Content of the NTP
3.    To accomplish these objectives, an integrated training program will be financed
by the project. The NTP will be organized and carried out through three sub-programs
defined according to the beneficiaries:
* Support for the new regulatory framework and sectoral modernization process
*  Managerial and technical training and
* Users awareness campaign.
Sub-program I. Support for the new regulatory framework and sectoral
modernization process
4.    This sub-program is oriented towards promoting the approval and implementation
of the new Regulatory Framework and its relates policies, norms and standards, in
accordance to the results of the study carried out in the Technical Assistance Program.
This implies: (i) the approval of legal reforms at the National Congress, and the approval
of resolutions and by-laws by the Executive branch, and (ii) the approval of municipal
laws and regulations in accordance with the proposed Normative and Regulatory



Annex H
Page 13 of 23
Framework. The support activities will include: (i) field visits and study tours; (ii)
international seminars and (iii) special workshops.
5.    Field visits and study tours. These activities will include four field visits and
study tours to countries with regulatory frameworks that could be considered as a model
for the Venezuela WS&S sector modernization process. Each visit will last ten days and
will be attended by about three members of Congress and the Municipal Councils, one
official from the MARNR and one from HIDROVEN. The participants from the
Legislative Branch and the MARNR will be selected on the basis of their influence and
leadership in the implementation of the Regulatory Framework. The representative of
HIDROVEN should be an expert in this field and will act as a mediator in the discussion
process.
6.    International Seminar.   This activity comprises the organization of an
international seminar in Venezuela for the purpose of disseminating and discussing the
tendencies and state of the art in the field of WS&S Regulatory Frameworks. About sixty
professionals and authorities from Venezuela and forty representatives from abroad will
attend the Seminar. Sixty percent of the Venezuelan participants will represent the
Legislative Branch either at the National or the Regional level and the remaining forty
percent will include authorities from the Executive Branch and WS&S companies.
Priority will be given to the attendance of Council Members and authorities from those
regions where the implementation of the Regulatoly Framework is already under
discussion and where the transfer process of the service is underway.
7.    Special Workshops. These will provide an opportunity for thorough discussion
regarding the submitted proposals and their consequence on the future development of the
sector.  Ten two-day workshops are proposed for an attendance of about fifteen
participants each: eight from the legislative branch, four from the executive branch and
three consultants who will coordinate the discussions. These workshops will be held in
different regions: two at the National Congress, three in Caracas Metropolitan Area
Municipalities and five at regional level.
Sub-program II. Managerial and Technical Training
8.    This sub-program is oriented towards a short-term training of a nucleus of
professionals and decision-makers regarding basic concepts of water supply and
sanitation services management, and towards increasing technical capacity at both state
and local government level to plan, design, and operate modern WS&S services.
9.    In order to accomplish these objectives, HIDROVEN  will implement an
integrated training program  consisting of:  (i) executive seminars;  (ii) intensive
specialization courses and (iii) introductory courses.



Annex H
Page 14of23
10.   Executive Seminars (ES). The ES are designed primarily for the top managers of
the WS&S companies. The main objective of the ES is to introduce the fundamentals of
modem management models and their applications to water supply services. The ES will
consist of four two day sessions, three of them for the presentation of theoretical
knowledge and the fourth as an in-house workshop in order to apply the instruments
delivery during prior sessions. Details of the sessions are as follows:
Session 1. General Concepts: This session will cover: (i) a brief description of
managerial techniques and their application to public services with emphasis on
the water sector, (ii) strategic planning of service delivery, including private
sector participation, (iii) analysis of the institutional arrangements, and (iv)
solving of internal and external conflicts including company municipalization,
concession and regulatory framework.
Session 2. Finance and Cost Recovery: This session will include: (i)introduction
to cost analysis, cost recovery practices and commercial principles, (ii) analysis
of financing alternatives, (iii) financial instruments and their application in WS&S
companies, (iv) budget formulation and control, and (v) tariff structure and
overcoming subsidy practices.
Session 3. Presentation of Real Problems. This session will be oriented to: (i)
case-studies dealing with managerial and financial problems in water services
companies, and (ii) analysis of altematives and problem solving strategies.
Session 4. In-house workshop.  The in-house workshop will be executed
individually to each of the selected operating companies. Priority will be given to
those companies already involved in a rehabilitation program, privatization
process and/or to those having obtained municipal concession for their operation.
This include a two day work session in the company premises . The specialized
experts will help management to identify their main restrictions as well as to
design appropriate strategies for overcoming these.
11.   Intensive Specialization Courses (ISC). These courses will be aimed at training
middle management personnel and technicians of water supply enterprises either in the
new regional companies or in the current HIDROS. Through the ISC, it will be possible
to deliver specialized training program in the most important technical issues of the
WS&S service. The main objectives of the ISC will be to increase the understanding and
technical capacity of personnel in WS&S companies and of assuring their skills to apply
new technologies, operational procedures, standards, and regulations in the delivery of
the service.
12.   The ISC will comprise specialized courses available individually, each with a
duration of about one week. During each course, several examples of local situations will



Annex H
Page 15 of 23
be presented and discussed. A provisional list of the ISC that will be offered and their
specific contents includes the following:
Preventive Maintenance Workshop including: (i) importance of preventive
maintenance in the effectiveness of the services and its costs, (ii)  design
techniques and coordination of preventive maintenance in WS&S service, (iii)
planning, implementation, follow-up, and  evaluation  of the  preventive
maintenance programs.
Inventory Control Workshop that includes: (i) inventory as a key instrument to
ensure operational effectiveness and cost control, (ii) standard inventory
techniques in WS&S companies, and (iii) frequent problems and solutions.
Laboratory Analysis Workshop including: (i) identification of main water-
polluting agents, waste water disposal, (ii) laboratory test and analysis most
frequently used in quality control of water supply and sanitation, (iii) upgrading
practices for industrial safety, (iv) case studies for the WS&S sector.
Formulation and evaluation of projects including:  (i) demand studies and
projections; (ii) techniques for identification and assessment of water projects;
(iii) methods of project evaluation and analysis of alternatives; and (iv) case
studies for the WS&S sector.
Commercialization Workshop including: (i) basic principles involved in the
commercialization of public services, (ii) customer services (iii) identification and
reduction of unaccounted-for-water, (iv) design and application of tariff
structures, and (v) measuring and billing principles.
Introductory Workshop for Managerial Techniques. This course is oriented
towards providing middle management personnel with the management
techniques and principles most commonly applied in public service companies
with emphasis on WS&S service. These include: (i) a brief description of
managerial techniques and their application, (ii) strategic planning of the service,
including private sector participation, (iii) institutional assessment, and (iv)
conflict solving and case studies.
13.   Introductory Courses (IC). These courses will be aimed at training members of
Regulatory Agencies, personnel of MARNR and other related central government
organizations and local or municipal staff responsible for the WS&S services. The IC
will yield a common basis of knowledge that will improve the decision making process
regarding the regulations and policies related to water supply services.



Annex H
Page 16 of 23
14.   These courses will last five days with a maximum of 16 participants in each
course and will comprise three parts: general concepts, operational matters, and strategic
issues. Details of the different parts are as follow:
General Concepts: This area will cover the following: (i) the situation of the
WS&S services in Venezuela and its main figures, (ii) sanitation problems, (iii)
socio-economic considerations of the WS&S sector's critical situation, (iv) the
Venezuelan Water and Sewerage Modernization Process: advances, issues and
action plan.
Operational Matters: This part will include: (i) description of the main sources of
water production and basic operational aspects of water supply systems, (ii)
unaccounted-for-water and conservation practices, (iii) waste water disposals and
environmental considerations, and (iv) operational costs and definition of tariffs.
Strategic issues: (I) cost recovery and self financing of the services,  (ii)
Regulatory Framework, norms and standards, (iii) decentralization and transfer of
responsibilities, and (iv) concepts of privatization and concessions, including
advantages and disadvantages.
Sub-program III. User Awareness Campaign (UAC)
15.   This sub-program is oriented towards promoting a broad understanding of the
main objectives of the Venezuelan WS&S sector's modernization process, and towards
building public support for the critical issues include in this process, such as rational use
and conservation of water, cost-recovery and self-financing goals and customer service.
The UAC will be oriented to replace the general understanding of natural, cheap and
plentiful water supply for a new idea of water supply as an industrial, costly, and scarce
product, to promote water resources conservation and rational consumption, to explain
the need for cost-recovery practices and to inform the population about its duties and
rights as WS&S users.
16.   To accomplish these objectives, the UAC will include:
* a multimedia national campaign to inform the public about the modernization
process of the sector and to build support for critical issues, such as water
conservation, cost recovery and environmental concerns;
* preparation of printed and audiovisual material to be distributed in elementary
and high school centers, sport contests and ecological events; and
* short term seminars and workshops for journalist and other media workers.



Annex H
Page 17 of 23
17.   The design of activities to be undertaken as well as the proposal for campaign
material and events to be promoted will be done during the first six months of the
program. Latin American experience in these type of activities will be consulted. The
detailed proposal of the User Awareness Campaign as well as the chronogram of
execution and corresponding costs will be submitted for approval to the Bank prior to the
start of the program.

Appendix 3
SECTOR DECENTRALIZATION PROGRAM
Program objectives
1.    The Sector Decentralization Program (SDP) is designed as a project preparation
facility to replicate the organizational model implemented in the Monagas Rehabilitation
Project. The main objective of the SDP is to accomplish all the studies and activities
needed for the decentralization of at least four additional integrated water systems.
2.    The SDP will be executed by HIDROVEN with the support of the corresponding
regional company. Participation in this Program will be subject to:
* the creation of a decentralized regional water supply company that would take
over service responsibilities and management related to the respective integrated water
system from the corresponding HIDRO,
. provision of start-up working capital to the new company by the regional
government and the municipalities, equivalent to no less than 10% of the proposed
Technical Assistance Program and
*  the commitment to adopt cost-recovery practices and financial sustainability, and
to promote private sector participation.
Program activities
3.    The SDP would finance the followings activities:
(a)   Institutional design and support for: (i) a study of the organizational
structure and operational modality of the service, including commercial
practices, (ii) legal assistance in the transfer process of the service, assets,
liabilities, and personnel and in the preparation of draft regulations and
municipal laws, concession contracts, by-laws or any other legal documents
(to be provided directly by the EPU and financed under its budget), and
(iii) assets and liabilities appraisal, including labor liabilities.
(b)   Support in Managerial and Commercial Areas
(c)    Engineering Designs for the definition of the necessary investment
programs for the rehabilitation of the WS&S system including: (i)
identification and evaluation of the investment required for operational



Annex H
Page 22 of 23
rehabilitation, proposing priorities and an execution chronogram; (ii)
design of a program  for the unaccounted-for water;  (iii) detailed
engineering study for emergency rehabilitation work, and (iv) environment
analysis of the ecological implications of the project.
(d)   Economic and Financial Studies: (i) population willingness to pay for the
WS&S service, (ii) financial model of the company based on an estimation
of the demand and the operational costs of the service per system, (iii)
elaboration of the contract program to be negotiated by the interested
parties , and (iv) economic evaluation of the rehabilitation project.
