VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Technical Assistance Program
for the Creation of a Users' Hydrographic Confederation
for Water Resources Management in the State of Monagas
Background
1.    The management of water resources in Venezuela is de facto centralized in the
Ministry of the Environment and Renewable Natural Resources (MARNR), which
formulates policies, sets water quality standards and controls most water-related
(particularly water supply and sanitation) investments as well as their operation and
maintenance. Several other national, state and local agencies are also involved in water
resources management, but these operate without coordination, a comprehensive
management approach or a clear policy or strategy for integration between them. As a
result of this situation, although water policy formulation is technically the responsibility
of MARNR, the Ministry of Agriculture and Breeding (MAC) separately plans,
constructs and manages irrigation, and, similarly, although water quality standards are
established at the national level by MARNR according to the type of water body, the
Ministry of Health and Social Assistance (MSAS) sets water standards whenever issues
related to the protection of public health are or might be involved. With respect to water
supply and sanitation, some of MARNR's centralized control responsibilities have been
delegated to HIDROVEN, a state-owned corporation, when sector restructuring and
decentralization begun in 1990. HIDROVEN, wholly owned b-y MARNR, oversees
water supply and sanitation services that are provided through regional water companies,
called HIDROS, which are themselves wholly owned by HIDROVEN.
2.    As part of the sector restructuring initiative, the government of Venezuela created
two river basin commissions in 1993 to decentralize and coordinate water resources
management in the Tuy River and Lake Valencia. However, these have failed to achieve
the objectives set for them, have not achieved sustainability and have remained fully
dependent from MARNR for their financial needs. The commissions were created
following a new institutional model to address problems of water and the environment
with a more decentralized approach, where public participation was presented as a key
component of the institutional framework (public meetings were held in the basins to
involve stakeholders in some planning decisions). In reality, the decision-making has
been maintained at the federal level. There is not a clear idea about the usefulness and
convenience of these commissions among officials in MARNR and the basin's
stakeholders have not been properly involved in its management and have remained un-
bound to requirements or decisions of financial significance.



Annex I
Page 2 of 7
3.    The state of Monagas is a good example of the difficult and complex institutional
framework of water management in Venezuela, since numerous agencies are operating in
the state without a comprehensive or integrated water resources management plan or
coordinating structure, either institutionally or through regulations. Indeed, water
resources management in Monagas involves: (i) local offices of MARNR; (ii) MSAS;
(iii) local offices of MAC; (iv) HIDROCARIBE-Monagas, the fully-owned subsidiary of
HIDROVEN for this state; and (v) Aguas de Monagas (AdM), the company formed by
the municipalities Mancomunidad and the state government which is being strengthened
under the proposed Bank project.
4.    Several important problems of water quantity and quality are being compounded
in Monagas by the combination of a growing pressure on water resources and divided
responsibilities among managing institutions, limited regulatory structure, lack of
adequate institutional capacity and weak financial incentives to implement water
resources planning and management. The state's urban areas have been steadily growing
in recent years (the capital and largest municipality in the state, Maturin, is experiencing
an annual rate of growth of 6.5 percent) and are already competing for increasingly scarce
resources with the main economic sectors, which are mainly based on the production of
oil and agriculture (crop and livestock, with plans for tripling of irrigated area in the next
decade). Municipal water supply systems are inadequately maintained and have lacked
the financial capacity, resulting in unreliable services, large losses, recurrent annual
periods of rationing and almost nonexistent wastewater treatment. Surface water quality
has, as a result, been degraded by industrial and domestic discharges which have
increased without corresponding investments in wastewater treatment or management.
Groundwater contamination is also thought to be widespread because of inadequate
sanitation practices, placing public health at risk, while monitoring and control measures
remain very weak and hundreds of wells are used for the supply without adequate control
of groundwater hydrology or quality.
5.    Although the creation of AdM is a step towards providing a more reliable and
efficient municipal water service, it does not addresses the allocation and protection of
water resources in the state and conflicts over water allocation and pollution will
probably grow in intensity if no action is taken in this area. To tackle this problem and
develop policies and experience that could be replicated in other parts of the country, the
project includes a technical assistance (TA) program to support the introduction of
integrated water resource management practices in the state of Monagas, through the
creation a Users' Hydrographic Confederation (CHU - Confederaci6n Hidrografica de
Usuarios) with heavy involvement of the basin's stakeholders. This action will help shift
decision-making activities from the central government to the region's stakeholders,
simplify and provide coordination in the institutional framework for the management of
water resources and will support the development of the principles, criteria and tools
necessary for tackling the physical issues mentioned above, particularly those related to
the allocation and protection of the resources. The principles and strategy used for the
implementation of this water resource management approach are described below.



Annex I
Page 3 of 7
Objectives and Strategy
6.    The inclusion of CHU in the project is based on the idea that effective water
resources management is best achieved through an organization that: (i) is given the
authority to establish and implement a water resources plan; (ii) meets the goal of
financial sustainability; and (iii) is locally based, represents the users and stakeholders in
a river basin and responds and is financially dependent from them. When these three
conditions are meet, it can be expected that there would be an inherent motivation to
efficiently allocate adequate volumes and sufficient quality for diverse uses, allocation
will be agreed upon by the users represented in CHU, and the degree of water quality
demanded by each water user and other stakeholders could be negotiated within the
organization. Because costs are covered among the stakeholders, the goals and the means
to reach these goals (in terms of water allocation and protection) can also be expected to
be cost-effective and financially sustainable.
7.    During project preparation, the Venezuelan authorities, with the support of
consultants financed by a grant from the Spanish government, evaluated the present
regulatory and institutional framework for water resources management, made a first
estimate of the possible solutions and developed and agreed to the above criteria. Based
on these criteria and on a preliminary assessment of the basic physical information
available, a decision was made that institutional development for water resource
management at the national level and for the state of Monagas should be based on a
comprehensive framework that accounts for the interdependencies among potentially
competing socioeconomic activities, while protecting aquatic ecosystems. Accordingly,
the TA to be provided will support the study, design and establishment of an effective
management framework through a strategy that relies on two key principles: (i)
management should be decentralized, with strong involvement of users and stakeholders,
and (ii) financial sustainability and self-sufficiency should be achieved through the
introduction of water use charges and pollution fees.
8.    The TA progran to be carried out through HIDROVEN would support the
establishment of CHU to manage water resources in the Amana and Guarapiche river
basins in the state of Monagas. Since the main role of CHU will be to develop and
implement management criteria and principles to plan for the allocation and protection of
water resources in this area, the TA program will also provide support to CHU for the
preparation of this management plan and for setting up the necessary institutional
capacity to implement it.
Description
9.    The TA program will be implemented in two phases: the first phase will support
the creation of the CHU; the second phase will provide technical support to help it
undertake its role. The first phase includes: (i) the development of a proposal for the
design of the institutional and legal framework necessary to create CHU and provide it



Annex I
Page 4 of 7
with the instruments to operate and achieve financial sustainability; and (ii) the
preparation of a basic information system of water resources, to be used for the
development of a water resources model for planning.  The second phase, to be
implemented following the creation of the CHU, will provide technical support to the
CHU for the selection of objectives, strategies and alternatives that will be translated in a
Water Resources Management Plan and for setting up a water resources monitoring
system.
10.   Design of Users' Hydrographic Confederation. The creation of an organization
of users and stakeholders capable of water resources management requires an enabling
institutional and legal framework. To create this type of organization in Monagas, the
project will finance a study to: (i) identify and evaluate the institutional system and key
stakeholders involved in water management, and their roles, as specified by law as well
as those actually demonstrated in practice; (ii) analyze the laws, regulations and
responsibilities distribution relevant to water resource management; (iii) evaluate the
financial and economic implications of a decentralized management approach based on
stakeholders participation; and (iv) propose a design for CHU, drawing from experiences
in similar circumstances either in Venezuela or other countries, with identification of
institutional and legal modifications necessary to create a functioning organization with
financial viability.
11.   The study will particularly explore and identify methods for the selection of
stakeholders to participate in CHU, procedures for decision-making, and use of economic
instrunents based on pricing for water quality and quantity. Potential stakeholders would
include representatives from  water companies, municipalities, industry, commerce,
agriculture, NGOs, and affected government agencies. To define the decision making
role of CHU, the study will develop proposals for the procedures it will use to coordinate
arnong and resolve disputes between stakeholders and for evaluating the roles and
responsibilities of different interest groups. The design will also address the staffing
needs for the technical unit that will be needed to support CHU, drawing as much as
possible on the present planning office which MARNR has located in the state of
Monagas, both to serve as a core unit for this and to provide counterpart role in the
execution of this study.
12.   Basic Information System. The design of CHU and the sustainable management
of water resources requires accurate knowledge of the area to be managed, the involved
stakeholders and its physical, chemical, biological, and socioeconomic conditions,
translated into a form usable for decision-makers. To this end, building on the initial
analysis made by consultants during project preparation, the project will finance the
development of a basic information system that will include the study and analysis,
among others, of the following:
(a)   Appropriate basin boundary for water management by CHU;
(b)   Hydrology of ground and surface water resources;



Annex I
Page 5 of 7
(c)   Ground and surface water quality;
(d)   Socioeconomic activities related to the water sector, including the
financial mechanisms and charges in place in the management of water.
(e)   Current and projected water uses and demands;
(f)    Land use and management;
(g)   Existing  infrastructure for water supply, sanitation, flood control,
irrigation, etc.;
(h)   Waste and pollution flows to and from the basins;
(i)    Natural ecosystem; and
(j)    Water-related public health issues, including incidence of water related
disease;
13.   The basic information system will also include the preparation of a geographic
information system (GIS) on the water resources in the area to be managed by CHU, to
facilitate the management of the information and its use by stakeholders and policy-
makers. It will include information on the allocations of water, water quality conditions
and existing and planned infrastructure for water supply, flood control, irrigation,
hydropower and wastewater treatment. Gaps in data, or poor quality data will be
identified and estimated in this first phase of the project.
14.   Technical Support. Upon its creation, the main tasks of CHU will be to: (i) set
goals for the river basins; (ii) generate and evaluate strategies to achieve those goals;
(iii) evaluate and select the alternatives that will define the Water Resource Management
Plan; and (iv) implement this plan. The project will support the development of the first
three.
15.   To support the goal identification and prioritization process, the project will also
finance the preparation of a Water Resources Model to provide a tool with which the
members of CHU may gain an understanding of the impacts of alternative strategies of
water management. The model will be based on the basic information system and on
input from stakeholders as identified in the design study for the CHU. The model will
mathematically relate the data on water quantity and quality with economic and
environmental information in a multi-objective framework to examine the tradeoffs
between objectives, such as economic growth among competing sectors, public health
protection and environmental preservation. The model is not intended as a substitution
for the negotiation and iteration with stakeholders that must form the basis of CHU role,
but as a tool to support it and to facilitate the consideration and evaluation of the costs
and inputs of the full range of management strategies and alternatives available,
including: demand management, regulatory measures, supply management, public
education, water charges, pollution fees, new institutional arrangements, and
environmental reclamation. At a minimum, the model will allow the strategies to be
assessed for their environmental, socioeconomic, political, legal, institutional, and fiscal
constraints and impacts.



Annex I
Page 6 of 7
16.   Additional technical support will be provided through the financing of the
establishment of a monitoring program of water quality and ecosystem health, including
acquisition of monitoring equipment and computer hardware and software. This technical
support will contribute additional knowledge of the resource, as well as a tool to
understand how to manage the watershed and to evaluate and review the management
decisions.
Implementation
17.   HIDROVEN will be responsible for implementation and for the development of
the policies and institutional framework necessary for the creation of CHU and for their
evaluation for possible replication in other parts of the country, as part of the National
Institutional Component. HIDROVEN will prepare the terns of reference and administer
the studies and activities of this sub-component, incorporating the Monagas' offices of
MARNR --and to the extent possible other stakeholders through it-- in this process and in
the administration and evaluation of the studies. Under this approach, the Monagas
offices of MARNR will constitute the core unit of CHU design. Following the creation
of CHU, financial responsibility for the technical unit would be progressively transferred
from MARNR to CHU, once its financial mechanisms are put in place.
Loan Condition
18.   Submission of a reform proposal and implementation plan satisfactory to the Bank
for the creation of the Users' Hydrographic Confederation by July 31, 1997, and the
implementation of this plan would be loan conditions.
TA Program Cost Summary
19.   The total estimated cost for TA program for supporting the creation of CHU is
US$ 2,050,000. Individual costs of the different tasks it will finance, its instruments and
planned execution period are outlined in Table 1.

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Implementation Plan
1.    This annex details the Implementation Plan for the above project. It presents: (i)
project monitoring and reporting arrangements; (ii) Bank supervision requirements; and
(iii) project financing plan and work program. The annex also addresses the content of the
project's annual and mid-term implementation reviews and Bank supervision
requirements.
Project Monitoring and Reporting Arrangements
2.    Monitoring Indicators. Tables I and 2 at the end of this annex present the input,
output and impact/development indicators to be monitored during the implementation of
the project. Given the difficulty of measuring some of the project objectives success and
impact just by the use of numerical indicators, customer surveys will also be carried out
after two years and annually thereafter until the project is completed to help measure the
impact of the project on WS&S services and customer satisfaction. Additionally, NGOs
will be contracted (one at the regional level and one at the national level) to evaluate the
achievement of the project's objectives at mid-term and at project completion. These
monitoring indicators and reports must be incorporated in the semi-annual reports to
provide the executing agencies and the Bank with a detailed assessment of how the
project is performing and which correcting measures should be adopted, if necessary.
They will be reconfirmed during negotiations and set forth in a letter from HIDROVEN
and AdM to the Bank that will complement the Loan Agreement.
3.    Three main groups can be identified in this whole set of monitoring tools,
according to their expected use:
(a) the first group is formed by the indicators and targets that will be monitored as
financial covenants for AdM of the project agreement (these are listed in table 4.1
of this SAR and in the Article IV of the project agreement);
(b) a second larger group is formed by the whole set of monitoring indicators and
performance targets for AdM's, which have to be substantially achieved for the
disbursement of the third package of rehabilitation investments. These are listed
in table 1 of this annex and in the schedule 1 of the project agreement with AdM
(they correspond to the targets of the Contract Plan between the state government,
the Mancomunidad, and AdM, and will be the key vehicle for monitoring
performance and compliance with agreed objectives in the regional component);
and



Annex J
Page 2ofll
(c) a third group is formed by the "key monitoring indicators" (input, output and
impact), which will be used by the Bank to measure implementation performance
and monitor the project's impact (these are listed in table 2 and 3 of this annex
and include part of the performance targets for AdM, as well as other monitoring
indicators for the national institutional component).
4.    Project Monitoring and Reporting Requirements. The executing agencies
(HIDROVEN and AdM) will be responsible for project monitoring activities and for
preparing semi-annual progress reports to be submitted by September 30 for the semester
ending June 30, and by March 31 for the semester ending December 31. The contents,
schedule and distribution of responsibilities are shown in Table 4 and 5 at the end of the
annex. The reports will also evaluate compliance with subloan conditionality by AdM,
each municipality and by the state government, and actions taken to enforce compliance.
In cases of unsatisfactory progress, the reports will detail proposals for adjustments and
corrective actions.
5.    Project monitoring capacity. Project monitoring capacity in the executing
agencies will be guaranteed by the establishment of accounting and financial reporting
systems acceptable to the Bank in both agencies before effectiveness, and by the
strengthening of their capacity through the financing of consultant services.  In
HIDROVEN, a consultant contract will be financed under the national institutional
component to support the management and monitoring of this component and the overall
coordination of the project, including major monitoring and reporting activities. In AdM,
a management contract will be entered with a private operator, that includes among its
responsibilities the monitoring and reporting of the project implementation (financial,
investment and quality performance indicators), and a consultant will be hired to provide
assistance to AdM's Board of directors to supervise project implementation and the
performance of the private operator. Both these consultant contracts and the management
contract with a private operator should be made effective for project effectiveness or
disbursement and maintained during project execution, to guarantee adequate monitoring
capacity in HIDROVEN and AdM.
6.    Additionally, as has been pointed out above, project evaluation studies with
NGOs and customer satisfaction surveys (among AdM customers) will be carried out
before the project's mid-term review and after completion, to evaluate its success and the
impact of those components that can not easily be measured by numerical indicators.
Base conditions (before project starts) are available from the willingness-to-pay studies
carried out and from a survey of environmental health to be carried out shortly with the
support of the PHRD grant from the government of Japan.
7.    Annual Reviews. Within two months of the submission of the semi-annual
report due on September 30 of each year, the Bank and the Government will conduct a
formal joint review of the progress made in implementing the strategy for the sector,
meeting the project's objectives and achieving agreed monitoring indicators. The review
would be based on the semi-annual progress reports submitted by AdM and HIDROVEN



Annex J
Page 3 of 11
according to the reporting schedule shown in tables 4 and 5, and on performance
according to the targets established in the Contract Plan. The agenda for the reviews, in
addition to evaluating the semi-annual reports, would include an assessment of the
adequacy and timeliness of the National Government budget allocation for the project for
meeting the investment needs of the following year. The discussion and analysis of these
issues will lead to the formulation of an action plan, satisfactory to the Bank, to be
prepared by AdM, HIDROVEN and the Government, the terms of which will be carried
out during the succeeding year.
8.    Mid-Term  Reviewv. The second annual review would constitute a Mid-Term
Review of project implementation, and provide an opportunity for a more comprehensive
review of the achievements of the project to date and of the prospects for successful
completion. In addition to the agenda items for annual reviews, the Mid-Term Review
will include: (a) analysis of the flow of funds and funds management; (b) performance
under the Contract Plan; (c) an evaluation of the remaining investment needs and
authorization of the third rehabilitation investment package; (d) an evaluation of project
success to date based on the evaluation made by a NGO for the national component and
another NGO for the regional component, as well as consumer satisfaction surveys for the
components executed by AdM; and (e) any proposals for restructuring or reorienting the
project in light of the reviews and assessments of the issues discussed during the Review.
Bank Supervision Requirements
9.    Given the project's comprehensive approach to sector reform and the demands of
supervising subprojects across Monagas and Venezuela, extensive and close supervision
will be required, especially during the first year of project implementation. A project
launch workslhop and yearly annual reviews will be organized, assisting the staff of
institutions participating in the project to maintain a common understanding of the
project's overall development objectives, operational procedures, coordination needs, and
potential implementation problems. The project launch workshop will take place within
30-45 days of loan effectiveness, and will focus on any necessary reviews and
adjustments in the implementation plan, project management and work prograrn,
procurement, disbursement, and auditing procedures and requirements.  The launch
workshop would last three to five days and should include the following staff: Task
Manager, Financial Analyst, Procurement Specialist, Disbursement Specialist and
Accounting Specialist.
10.   It is expected that at least three supervision missions per year (four in the first
year), staffed by an environmental engineer, a financial analyst and an specialist in human
resources and community participation, plus one technical specialist from the resident
mission, with a duration of about two weeks per mission, will be required to supervise the
project during its implementation. Bank missions should plan to visit the municipalities
where rehabilitation works are under way, at least once during construction and once
during the operation of the rehabilitated infrastructure, as well as HIDROVEN' offices in
Caracas in relation with the national component.  Other states integrated in the Sector



Annex J
Page 4 of l1
Decentralization Program will be visited as time and resources permit. For the first year,
including the project launch workshop, the supervision requirements are estimated at
about 45 to 50 staff weeks (about 15 staff weeks from the resident mission, mainly for
day to day supervision). Supervision requirements will fall to about 35 to 40 staff weeks
per year thereafter (about 10 to 15 staff weeks per year from the resident mission).

table at the end of this annex.

DEFINITIQN
Operaling Ratio. the percentage of operating income that is spent on operating expenses plus
depreciation.
Debt Service Coverage: the internal cash generation divided by the debt service.
Quick Ratio:  the percentage of current assets (cash, marketable securities, and accounts
receivable) in relation to current liabilities; this ratio is also called the acid test ratio.
Contribution to Investment Program: percentage of total investment costs contributed from
internal cash generation.
Users Billed: number of users billed divided by total number of users.



Annex J
Page 6 of 11
Collections/Billing: bills collected (in Bs) divided by total bills issued on an annual basis.
Accounts Receivable (nmonths): total uncollected bills at the end of the year divided by total
billing for the year and then multiplied by twelve.
New Meters Installed: anllual number of meters installed.
Unaccounted-for-water: volume of water produced less the volume of water billed
(micrometered plus non-metered estimated at 15 m3 per month per household for residential
consumers and 10 m3 per household for social consumers) divided by the volume of water
produced and metered.
Billing Period. number of days between reading of meters and issuing of bills.
Collection Period: number of days between issuing of bills and collection of payment.
# Days of Rationing Per Year: number of days in a year where water supply must be rationed
due to the inability to meet total demand.

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
AdM Finances
Background
1.    Aguas de Monagas (AdM) is a newly established company, created with limited
operating capital and no assets, and staffed only by the core personnel needed to manage
the transfer of operations from HIDROCARIBE, the regional water company, and
prepare this project. It took over the commercial part of the service in July 1995. In
January 1996 it became responsible for the WS&S services in the entire state in
substitution of HIDROCARIBE.  AdM  has inherited a service saddled by poor
management, weak commercial practices, bad culture of payment, low billing and
collection rates (61 and 56 percent respectively for the whole state in 1994 and 52 and 27
for 1995, the year of the transfer) and insufficient tariff levels (17.5 Bs/m3 for residential
consumers and 10 Bs/m3 for low income "social" consumers).
2.    AdM's main challenge is to overcome and correct the main financial and
commercial problems that affected HIDROCARIBE's performance, rehabilitate seriously
deteriorated physical infrastructure and improve service quality. Consequently, better
billing and collection procedures and reduction of the high unaccounted-for-water are
critical features of AdM's reform agenda and represent essential elements needed to
achieve its two key objectives in the financial area: (i) develop and maintain the financial
sustainability of the company; and (ii) guarantee the availability of sufficient working
capital for an adequate operation and for undertaking the required rehabilitation
investment plan.
Financial Model and Projections
3.    AdM's financial model and projections are included in the tables at the end of this
annex. They show that under the assumptions adopted, AdM will be sustainable and able
to reduce its dependency from national government transfers early in project execution
and cover most of the needed counterpart for the planned investment program, having a
positive net cash flow and income at the end of project execution. These projections were
prepared for this Staff Appraisal Report in June 1995 and updated on November 1995 and
April 1996, during negotiations. Main assumptions used for the preparation of AdM's
financial projections are summarized in table 1.
4.    AdM is expected to generate operating and net incomes on the order of 22% and
13%, respectively, of revenues by the end of project execution, cover about 33% of



Annex K
Page 2 of 12
investments by 1999 and 2000 and 100% thereafter, and maintain a debt service coverage
ratio above 1.5 after 2000 and over 2 after 2002, and a quick ratio over 6 during project
execution and over 1.5 after project execution. Rates of return on invested capital are
projected by the model to meet or exceed 5% on revalued assets on 2006. A study to
identify and reevaluate the service's assets and liabilities and the assets transferred under
Comodatum has been carried out during project preparation. These assets (presently
under HIDROCARIBE, MARNR and the municipalities) have been transferred to AdM,
upon the transfer of the service from HIDROCARIBE to AdM, and properly accounted
for (the model takes into consideration assets valued about 2,346 million Bs, that were
registered in HIDROCARIBE's accounts for 1995).
Table 1. Main Assumptions for the Financial Projections
i;Parameter/Actiondl                           AssumptionF   -i C 0-l;-0it f;0 0   0:00llif 00:0    t 
Inventory reserves                             15 days of consumption

Revaluation of Assets                          Automatic, with inflation
5.    HIDROCARIBE before and AdM now follow Venezuela's generally accepted
accounting and auditing principles, which are similar to those internationally recognized,
although their practices suffer from some deficiencies. Financial statements are audited
by a national firm that is appointed by the Director of the company. As part of project
preparation, the Bank is providing assistance to AdM (as well as to HIDROVEN) to
strengthen its accounting system and address deficiencies in this area before eligible
investments are initiated.
Working Capital
6.   Initial working capital for AdM will be provided through the combination of three
mechanisms: (i) AdM will collect all accounts receivable as of July 1995, when it took
over the commercial management of the service, and keep 40% of these payments; (ii)
for the period between July and December 1995, HIDROCARIBE will cover the
operating deficit of AdM using the reallocated funds received from the national
government for the operation of the service in the state of Monagas in 1995; and (iii) a
revolving fund will be created in AdM for the financing of the project investments
through the reimbursement by the Bank of the expenses eligible for retroactive financing
executed by HIDROCARIBE, that will be fully covered by grant contributions from the
state and national governments budgeted investment funds. The amounts to be received
through these three mechanisms have been estimated in: (i) 125 million Bs from pending



Annex K
Page 3 of 12
accounts receivable, (ii) 242 million Bs to cover AdM financial deficit in 1995; and (iii)
545 million Bs to finance the planned investments that will be eligible for retroactive
financing by the Bank under the proposed project (the revolving fund will be initially
constituted by the Bank disbursements, estimated at 265 million Bs).
7.    According to AdM's adopted policy, cash requirements have been projected to
maintain additional cash to cover at least two months of the operating expenses (less non-
receivable accounts) and short term investments projected for each year. Submission of a
plan satisfactory to the Bank for the provision of working capital for AdM would be
condition of negotiations.
Statement of Financial Results. Revenues
8.    Tariff Revenues. HIDROCARIBE's and now AdM's tariff structure follows the
1993 joint Ministerial Resolution of the Ministry of the Environment and Renewable
Natural Resources (MARNR) and the Ministry of Finance. Tariffs for residential, social
(low  income residential consumers), commercial, industrial and official users were
calculated by HIDROCARIBE against the residential tariff (referential residential tariff)
selected for each WS&S system, according to its characteristics (five reference levels
were established in the Resolution).
9.    The Resolution also establishes a formula to adjust the referential residential
tariff according to changes in the cost of labor, chemicals, energy and maintenance
contracts. However, this formula was never applied by HIDROCARIBE and tariff levels
have been eroded below what would be necessary to maintain the financial viability of
the company. Until 1995, tariffs remained at 17.5 Bs/m3 for residential, 10 Bs/m3 for
social and around 50 Bs/m3 for industrial, commercial and official users (level 4) in
Maturfn, and even lower in the rest of the state (normally at level 3, with 15, 7.5 and
around 40 Bs/m3 for residential, social and other users, respectively). Additionally, the
great majority of consumers were billed on a non-metered flat consumption rate (30 and
15 m3 per month for residential and social users respectively) and a disproportional large
number of consumers are classified as social. This, combined with the inefficient billing
and collection practice of HIDROCARIBE, produced largely insufficient tariff revenues.
10.   During project preparation, the referential residential tarif was increased in June
3        ~~~3                                       3
1995, from 17.5 Bs/m3 to 40 Bs/m3, and will be further increased to 60 Bs/m as a
condition of loan effectiveness (the social tariff will also be raised to 20 Bs/m3 and
industrial and commercial tariffs to 100 Bs/m3). These increases have been adopted by
the authorities to bring tariff revenues to levels that make the AdM financially viable, but
also to overcome the difficulty that tariffs increases would face due to the lack of a clear
regulatory framework and distribution of responsibilities in this area, should they be
I     This amount is calculated as 60% of the projected non-collectible accounts at the end of the year.
It is not included in the financial projection due to its uncertainty and in order to be on the safe side.



Annex K
Page 4 of 12
phased latter into project execution. It is also the policy of AdM to unify tariff levels in
the state, review those consumers classified as social and apply the formula provided by
the Resolution to update the referential residential tariff on a continuous basis to
maintain its value in real terms (this can be done without the intervention of the national
government, requiring only the approval of the Mancomunidad). Accordingly, the
financial projections assume that about 10,000 social consumers will be reclassified as
residential, newly billed consumers will be billed as residential and tariff levels will be
adjusted to reflect changes in the costs of labor, chemicals, energy and operation
contracts. Although not considered for the financial projections, an assessment will be
made during project execution of the tariff structure, the cost structure, the marginal cost
of the service and the provisions for adequate enforcing and tariff levels and structure will
be accordingly reviewed.
11.   Billing, Collection and Unaccounted-for-water. Present rates for billing,
collection, unaccounted-for-water and metering are forecasted to improve according to
the targets established under the Contract Plan (see table 2 below which summarizes
some of the assumptions used for setting these targets). These targets have been
developed during project preparation, on the basis of: (i) conservative estimations of the
improvements that are considered to be achievable in the commercial and physical
performance of the company; (ii) the help of a private operator to be hired under a
management contract;  and (iii) experience from similar water utilities.  Accounts
receivable are forecasted to be reduced to about two months from present four months
and special tariff reduction for municipal water consumption will be discontinued as a
result of project preparation agreements. Unaccounted-for-water is also estimated to
decrease to about 40% (from 70% in 1994) at the end of project execution and an
additional 10% during the following years.
Table 2. Performance Targets for AdM
..= .......9                                                 NS0
Residential users billed (%)    61        77       80        83        85
Collectionibilling (%)          64        73       76        78        80
Unaccounted-for-water (%)       66        52       47        43        40
Residential metered (%)          1        13       25        40        50
12.   To achieve these targets the management contract with a private operator will link
part of the payment to improvements in the rate of unaccounted-for-water and billing and
collection efficiency and AdM will concentrate the investments planned under the project
in the rehabilitation of the system and in the introduction of macrometering to production
and micrometering to about 50% of residential users and to 100% of commercial,
industrial and official users by the end of project execution. It is also assumed that the



Annex K
Page 5 of 12
volume of water consumed per customer is maintained at present levels and the
population will grow at a rate of about 2.8 percent per year.
13.   Other Revenues. Other revenues are estimated to be negligible in the financial
projections. However, with improved financial results and a liberalized financial sector,
treasury operations within AdM could begin to contribute to the revenues of the
company, particularly at the end of the project execution, when cash balances will be
substantially better.
Statement of Financial Results. Operational Expenses
14.   Impact of inflation. Average expected inflation and exchange rates are shown in
table 3 below. These projections reflect the impact of the recent devaluation. They have
been adopted on the assumption that local inflation will remain high for the next years,
decreasing the difference between local and international inflation rates only in the order
of 40% each year, from a 1996 inflation rate of about 80% to about 9% in the year 2000,
and that the official exchange rate will not reflect 20% of this difference. The effect on
AdM's financial performance that a possible worsening of the present unstable
macroeconomic situation of the country, over these already pessimistic projections, is
analyzed as part of the risk section of this annex (para 29 and 30).
Table 3. Estimated Macroeconomic Indicators Evolution
1nIktqr .1 ->   <;g-     9290- 10<- i9f        1,6                 2 0 
Inflation (%)                 80        50        27         15         9
Exchange rate (Bs/US$)*      480       632      1,070     1,450     1,730
*Average official exchange rate of the year
15.   Financial projections have been made on the basis of the operating costs and
efficiency indicators of the system under HIDROCARIBE's management, that are
considered conservative. Cost of energy, chemicals, labor and operation contracts are
expected to increase according to local inflation (see table 4). However, changes in the
cost for AdM of energy, chemicals and local purchases are only expected to reflect 50, 30
and 80% respectively of the variation of the local inflation (unit labor costs changes will
reflect 100%) due to the particular characteristics of the supply of energy and chemicals
in Venezuela (up to now, electricity was not paid by HIDROCARIBE and chemicals are
obtained from sub-products of large national industries) and to the rigidity of operation
contracts.
16.   Personnel Costs. After taking the WS&S system in Monagas over from INOS,
HIDROCARIBE operated it through annual contracts with local operating companies and
engineering firms (mainly formed by former INOS staff) that provided personnel,



Annex K
Page 6 of 12
equipment and some maintenance costs, while concentrating itself in the commercial
management and in the overall supervision and planning. As a result, HIDROCARIBE's
staff in Monagas was small (about 60 positions among management, commercial and
engineering departments). It is the policy of AdM to maintain this approach under the
overall management by the private operator. To this end, AdM received the commercial
management of the company in July 1995 after HIDROCARIBE had made redundant the
corresponding staff. Subsequently, AdM rehired only about 30 of the corresponding staff
on an interim basis, to be confirmed by the private operator. Additionally, one of the first
tasks of the private operator will be the reformulation and renegotiation of the contracts
with the local operating companies. As part of the Contract Plan agreements between
AdM and its stockholders, AdM, and through it the private operator, will have complete
autonomy in their hiring/redundancy policy and decisions. AdM staff is not unionized
and professional and technical staff wages are governed by public sector wage laws.
Table 4. Estimated Evolution of Operating Costs

* Inflation correction factor
17.   The financial projections assume that personnel costs will constitute about the
same proportion of O&M costs as today. It also assumes that personnel costs will
represent about 75% of administration costs.
18.   Depreciation. Fixed assets will be reevaluated annually according to inflation.
The average annual depreciation rate is assumed to be 4%, calculated using a straight line
method.
19.   Taxes. AdM pays the general tax to local purchases of equipment and goods
(12.5 %), income tax (30%) and assets tax (0.25% on fixed active assets). AdM is
exempt from municipal taxes.
20.   Financial Expenses. Since AdM does not have any outstanding debt service,
only interest charges corresponding to the subloan between AdM and the national
government for the transfer of project proceeds are included as financial expenses in the
projections. These peak at about 3,200 million Bs in year 2001 to diminish later. In
order to control AdM's indebtedness, targets will also be included for the debt service
coverage ratio, that should remain above 2 through the end of the repayment period,
considering additional yearly investments in the order of 5% of fixed assets after project
completion (these investment program has been built into the financial projection for the



Annex K
Page 7 of 12
project). Interests of the subloan will be capitalized and re-financed during the four years
construction period.
Flow of Funds. Sources
21.   Internal Cash Generation. With the conservative assumptions used in the
financial projections, AdM is expected to maintain adequate working capital and a
positive cash balance during project execution, and to generate increasingly positive
balances from this year onwards. AdM is expected to cover 12, 17 and about 33 % of
investment needs in years 1997, 1998 and 1999 and 2000 and 100% from that year.
22.   Government Grant Transfers. National, state and local support is expected to
continue in 1996 to cover cash operating deficits and into 1998 to contribute to
counterpart funds in 1997 and 1998, when it will cease. A grant contribution of 301
million Bs from the national government is projected as necessary in 1,996 to cover the
operation deficit and about 4,900 million Bs are projected to cover counterpart financing
of the planned investment program (about 12% of the roughly 42,400 million Bs
investment program).
23.   Loans.  AdM's debt only includes the subsidiary loan with the national
government for the transfer of project proceeds. Smaller bridge loans from commercial
banks have not been considered in the financial projections, although they might be
probably necessary. Other indebtedness of AdM will be limited by the agreed targets on
the debt service coverage ratio (equal or higher than 2 for the repayment period) that the
company must maintain.
Flow of Funds. Uses
24.   Debt Service. The targets given for AdM financial performance and debt service
coverage are relatively modest based on the improved profit performance and expected
stronger cash flow projected for the company. Additionally, the financing requirements
for the investment program (about 5% of fixed assets by year on year 2,001, increasing to
9% on year 2,006) represent a relatively pessimistic estimate of the investments AdM
will require to improve service quality and maintain it after project completion.
25.  Investment Program. AdM investment program is based on the analysis of the
works and actions that are required to improve service quality in Monagas and correct the
main causes for weak management of the service in the past. It also includes investments
in the order of 5% of fixed assets after project completion, raising to 9% from year 2,005
and onwards. Yearly investment projections are summarized in table 5 below. They
represent an increase of about four times over the normal yearly investment budget
allocated to the state of Monagas by MARNR (through HIDROCARIBE) and the state
government in recent years (on average, about 800 million Bs yearly).



Annex K
Page 8 of 12
Table 5. Yearly Investment Projections (million current Bs)
This Project       1,539   7,978   10,089   9,871   12,962
Other Investments                                               1,612    1,717
Financial Risks
26.   As a new company with a low level of permanent personnel and tariff levels
which have been adjusted in line with projected financial needs prior to project execution,
AdM starts off on a financial footing stronger than many similar companies supported by
Bank projects. Nevertheless, it faces initially other financial risks primarily related to
operating in a high inflationary environment for the next several years, including
expected devaluation of the currency and variable and high interest rates under the
proposed onlending arrangements. This environment will only make the movement from
a culture of non-payment for water supply and sanitation services to achievement of
financial autonomy for AdM more difficult.
27.   Also related is the risk associated to the availability of the funds that are expected
from the national government, both for the investments that are planned to be carried out
before project effectiveness (which are linked to the initial provision of working capital to
AdM) and for counterpart contribution. A possible worsening of the macroeconomic
situation might aggravate this risk, although it is mitigated by: (i) the strong commitment
of national and local government authorities to the decentralization process and to the
concept of financial sustainability in general and to the project in particular, evidenced by
budgetary allocations made for this project under the Ley Paraguas (despite stiff public
expenditure cuts');  (ii) the agreed obligations of the state government and the
municipalities under the Contract Plan, including the tariff increase adopted during
project preparation and the tariff increase to be adopted before loan effectiveness; (iii) a
financing plan that seeks to reduce dependency from national budget support early in
project implementation; and (iv) by the grouping of the works to be financed under the
project into three rehabilitation packages that can be independently carried out, linked to
the achievement of financial and operation conditions.
28.   The project has been designed to lessen the financial risks through: (i) the
agreement for the capital subsidy from the national government during the early years of
project execution justified by the serious state of neglect and disrepair that characterizes
the system being turned over to the local authorities; (ii) getting clarity on the project's
financial needs by holding discussions with a wide circle of stakeholders in the executive
I     Although these allocations have been approved and maintained in the recent budget cuts, they
have not yet been made available since they are linked to the issuance of the national debt bonds, which
has not been made yet for 1995.



Annex K
Page 9 of 12
and legislative branches of the national government and local governments and broad
consultation with the affected communities and agreeing on mutual obligations, formalized
in the project's Contract Plan; (iii) utilizing the services of a private operator to introduce
efficiencies before tariff adjustments are made, and provide tangible evidence of improved
services to make the tariff adjustments, when required, more supportable; and (iv) linking
the third package of rehabilitation investments with compliance with the agreed financial
and operating targets of the Contract Plan. In essence, therefore, the principal risk to the
financial viability of the project relates to the political willingness to: (a) make future
adjustments to tariffs in the context of high domestic inflation and adjustments to the
exchange rate;  and (b) maintain the agreed national government's counterpart
contributions. To highlight the impact of these key factors on the company's finances, the
following analysis looking at possible changes introduced by lower-than-expected revenues,
exchange rate and domestic inflation impacts and slower reductions in unaccounted-for-
water was carried out and is shown below.
29.   Internal Revenue Risks. AdM's most important risk is associated with lower-
than-expected revenues, either because of delays, reduction or lack of adoption of tariff
level adjustments, or because collection/billing/accounted-for-water efficiency is not
improved according to projected targets. Should either of these two factors take place,
they would have a large impact on AdM's finances and could make the company's
investment program not viable. This can be seen in tables 6 and 7 below, which show the
large impact that would have a fall in tariffs and collection rates of 5-20% below what is
projected on the net income, net cash flow (aggregated between 1997 and 2001) and
required government support (aggregated between 1996 and 2000 for investments). As
can be seen in these tables, decreases of just 5% in the tariff increases that would result
from the application of the adjustment formula would reduce net income and cash flow
by 29 and 47% respectively, and require an increment of grant support of 23%, with
similar impacts for reductions in the billing efficiency below the agreed targets.
Decreases in the order of 20% would make the company and the investment plan
financially un-viable.

30.   However, this risk in unlikely because it is expected that restrictions on national
government expenditures during the project execution period will not likely permit a
return to the centralized pattern of funding of investment of the past, forcing local
authorities to comply with the provisions of the contract plan and ensure than tariffs are
maintained at the agreed levels, in line with the recent tariff increases adopted before
negotiations. The involvement of a private operator to manage AdM will also contribute
to mitigate this risk through the introduction of better management and commercial
practices.
31.   Exchange Rate Risk. AdM's principal exchange rate exposure is linked to the
impact it would have on the cost of imported goods and services to be financed under the
project and on the debt service of the bolivar-denominated subloan that this increase of
costs would generate (exchange rate risks for loan repayment will remain with the
national government). The impact on net income, net cash flow and required government
support of 5-40% increases of the expected official exchange rate at the end of 1997 are
presented in table 8 below. As can be seen, the impact of further devaluations is small
(mainly due to the automatic adjustment of tariffs), and this risk's impact might be further
mitigated by reductions in the proposed investment plan or small increments in the grant
contributions from the national government.
Table 8. Estimated Impact of Changes in the Expected Exchange Rate at the End of

formula, the impact could even be positive on the net income figures in current bolivares.

3X3. Interest Rate of Subsidiary Loan. The interest rate for the subsidiary loan to
AdM is assumed to respond to changes in the inflation rate. If interest rate increases are
above these projections, AdM's main risk will be associate with its difficulty in
transferring the incremental cost to consumers without a tariff increase, since this element
is not included as part of the updating formula. In the following table 10, changes for the
net income, net cash flow (aggregated until 2001 and 2006) and required government
support for the investment plan are presented for different variations of the projected
interest rate. As can be seen, since the interest will be capitalized during the construction
period, the impact of increases in the interest rate will be mainly felt on the cash flow and
income after project completion and on the ability of AdM to repay the loan to the
national government. This risk will be minimized through intensive and close supervision
and the expected continued political commitment and support for the sustainability of the
company and the project objectives, and through these to the adoption of tariff increases
when these are necessary to counteract exceptional circumstances that are external to
AdM's management capacity.

34.   Unaccounted-for-Water Risk. AdM's risk of the failure to achieve the reduction
targets established for the unaccounted-for-water are associated to reduced revenues and
increased operation costs that can not be transferred to the consumers through tariff
updates. In the following table II the impact on the net income, net cash flow and

water against the projected targets for the year 2000.  The risks associated to reduced
targets for unaccounted-for-water will be minimized through the involvement of a private
operator with proved experience in this field and the adoption of an investment program
that will concentrate on rehabilitation and on metering.

VENEZUELA
WATER AND SEWERAGE DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
IN THE STATE OF MONAGAS
Economic Analysis
A. Summary
1.    Without the proposed project, it can be expected that the distribution network in Monagas will
continue to deteriorate, current shortages will become more severe and losses will continue to increase
due to lack of maintenance. With the project, a gradual improvement is expected, generating benefits
through the rehabilitation of the distribution network, better operational and financial management and
reduction of losses from waste, excessive consumption and the system's disrepair. The economic
benefits of the project will be derived from (i) the reallocation of water among users and (ii) the
reduction of physical losses. In order to measure the impact of reallocation, it is assumed that a
gradual shift from areas with unlimited consumption to shortage areas will take place in the next 5
years as a result of the proposed project. This shift will reallocate as much as 10% (5.5 Mm3 per year)
of maximum demand from a "market" which consumes excessive amounts at almost zero price, toward
a "market" which currently has permanent shortages due to low pressures or distribution problems
(deteriorated pipes, non-working valves, in-house leaks, etc.) and where water has a higher economic
value (as detected by the high willingness-to-pay of the population). This reallocation will result in net
gains with a Net Present Value of US$ 10.2 million'. Physical losses are estimated now at about 20
Mm3 per year or nearly one-third of production. The impact of system repairs and operation
improvements on the reduction of losses was estinated assuming that in the next five years, these
losses are reduced by 50%, that is, 10 Mm3 are recovered yearly by the year 2000 and that such
volume will be available to areas with current deficits and to new consumers. The Net Present Value
of the recovery of physical losses is calculated at US$ 20.5 million, resulting in a total benefit with a
Net Present Value of about US$ 30.7 million. The evaluation of benefits and costs of reallocation of
water among users and reduction of losses, based on the existing residential reference tariff rate of Bs
60 per cubic meter  results in an economic rate of return of about 15.2% for the rehabilitation
investments (65% of project costs). Taking into account consumer surplus, the rate of return is 27%.
2.    Additional justification for this project can be provided by the expected impacts at: (a) the
macroeconomic level, where the reduction of public transfers will positively impact the fiscal deficit
without reducing the level of service, as AdM assumes service responsibility and water tariffs are
rationalized. Savings could eventually reach more than US$ 200 million annually in IDROVENs
budget if this approach is expanded nationwide; and (b) the sector level, where the proposed project
will have an important impact not only on promoting a rational structure to administer water supply
systems throughout the country, but will also contribute to the performance of other sectors such as
public health. In the Monagas region, deficient water and sewerage service is regarded as a "public
In this case, there is a zero net transfer in terms of water volumes. Gains are discounted at 12% over a 20
year. As there are no estimates of the marginal cost of capital to use as the discount rate, 12% is used as
point of reference.



Annex L
Page 2 of 14
health problem" by local health authorities, which estimate that 42,000 cases of water-related illnesses
(with a public health cost calculated at US$ 2.4 million annually) can be prevented by improved water
and sanitation services. Concomitant losses in productivity as a result of absences from work due to
illness have been estimated to amount to 56,000 work days per year, at an average additional cost of
about US$ 0.6 million per year
3.    Alternatives. The project design was selected over other alternatives which would have
attempted reform of the sector more rapidly and broadly, because the government of Venezuela
wants to test innovative approaches on a small scale, and because state and local authorities in
Monagas are highly motivated to improve the water utility's performance. Engineering designs
are based on least-cost options. The criteria used for the selection of the rehabilitation works and
their packaging is described in annex F.
B. Background
4.     The state of Monagas has a population of about 540,000. Piped water coverage is over 900/o.
Water production statistics indicate that 64 million m3 of potable water were produced in 1994,2
sufficient to provide over 300 liters to each person daily.3 In some residential areas of Maturin, the
state capital, normal consumption is over 500 liters per capita daily. Yet, during the dry season
(September to May), water scarcity is severe. In the 1994-95 dry season, some areas of the city had no
water 70% of the time; many people in urban areas had to walk long distances to obtain small amounts
from other parts of the city where there is an abundant supply even in the dry season; and many
residents in areas connected to the network had to go to the river to wash their clothes. Water quality
is deficient; excessive counts of coliforms have been found in the water supply, and the incidence of
water-related diseases treated at local hospitals is quite large.
5.    The system is so poorly run that unaccounted-for-water is estimated at 70%, although, it is not
clear how much water is actually produced, and billing has little to do with consumption. It has been
estimated that, in some years, as little as 14 million m3 of the estimated 64 million m3 produced are
billed. Only a few water meters are read and revenues cover only 25% of operation and maintenance
costs. The system has been heavily subsidized by the central government.
Supply
6.    Potable water to the municipalities of the state of Monagas is supplied by a combination of
surface and ground water systems. The main systems are the Amana-Maturin and Guarapiche which
supply water to the city of Maturin. The rest are small surface intakes and deep wells. A summary of
the total available supply of potable water is presented in Table I below.4 There is no information
This is after losses in transmission mains estimated at 20 million m3. Reported production was
approximately 90 million m3.
1000 liters per day for a family of 5, or 200 liters per person-day, is considered sufficient to meet basic
requirements.



Annex L
Page 3 of 14
available on private production but it is probably significant given the limited number of industries
connected to the public network.

7.     Capacity. The system has considerable installed capacity in some processes but it is short in
others. The Mundo Nuevo water treatment plant (at the intake of the main Amana-Maturin system)
has a capacity of 4 m3/s but it has never produced at higher than 1.5 m3/s and, in the dry season, it
operates at close to 0.5 m3/s. The lack of a reservoir. (planned but never built) and the growing demand
for irrigation upstream from the plant does not allow for more than 0.5 m3/s as input to the Amana-
Maturin system during the five dry months of each year. Moreover, close to 60,000 people who live
along the 115 km transmission line betWeen the treatment plant and Maturin use water from this line.
8.     Table I shows installed capacity by municipality. Except for the Amana-Maturin system which
would require considerable investments to make its treatment plant capacity available, there is sufficient
capacity in most systems for the medium term. Even during the dry season a minimum of 1,100 I/s are
available for the city of Maturin, while demand (at 200 lpd) is only 747 I/s. Lack of adequate planning
4      Aguas de Monagas, C.A., Informe sobre la gestion del servicio de agua potable y saneamiento en el
Estado de Monagas. Afio 1994.



Annex L
Page 4 of 14
could be a main obstacle to guarantee water supply from the current systems,5 but expansion will be
facilitated by underutilized processes.
9.     Production. There are no reliable production data for the system. Estimates range from 34 to
62 million m3 (Mm3) per year for Maturin, and from  51 to 94 Mm' for the State of Monagas.
According to Table 1, production in 1994 was approximately 90 Mm3. These estimates are based on
capacity ratings and operating penrods rather than on actual volumes produced. There is no macro-
metering operating in any of the systems. If figures on table I are reliable, there seems to be sufficient
water in most systems. The main problem could be storage capacity to resolve seasonal variations in
surface water systems. Only three municipalities (Piar, Cedeilo and Caripe) may have to expand
production in the short term.
10.    Distribution. Aguas de Monagas reports approximately 45,000 customers connected to the
water supply network, of which 42,000 are residential consumers. Since coverage is over 93% state-
wide, the number of persons per connection is nearly 13. This is relatively high and it may indicate that
a number of connections are not registered.
11.    According to various studies,6 the distribution network needs considerable improvement and it
may be the source of substantial losses. The main problems in distribution are: poor separation of
pressure zones, lack of consumption metering, and deterioration of pipe capacity in the network. The
commercial system (metering, meter reading, billing, collection, claims, and new service installation)
needs a complete overhaul.
12.    These problems are responsible for the deficient distribution of water, especially in Maturin,
where some areas experience severe shortages even when there is sufficient production while others
can consume seemingly unlimited quantities. It has been estimated that as many as 120,000 people are
affected by severe shortages (70% of the time) during the 5-month dry season.
13.    Unaccounted For Water (UFW). Since there are no reliable estimates of production, it is
impossible to estimate UFW with any degree of accuracy. For example, according to March figures,
annual billing for 1995 could be nearly 25 Mm3. This is between 27%  and 49%  of production
(depending on the range of production estimates mentioned above). Therefore, UFW could be
anywhere between 51% and 73%.
14.    Physical losses have been estimated at 20 Mrn3 per year.7 Billing, therefore, would be between
6 and 49 Mm3 short, a considerable loss of revenue for a highly subsidized system. As shown in Table
For example, unless an expansion of storage capacity at the Amana-Maturin intake is planned, the city
should not rely on this system which is delivering decreasing volumes as the city competes for the same
supply with other municipalities along the 114 km transmission line.
A recent diagnosis of the network was made in Tudor Engineering Co. and OBHIDRAConsult C.A.,

saneamiento del Fstado de Hfonagas, Venezuela - Evaluaci6n de las condiciones existentes de los
sistemas. Enero de 1992.

2 below, less than 25% of costs are covered by revenues collected. There is much work to be done on
this problem some of which is included in the proposed project.
15.   Sewerage. Sewerage services should be treated as part of water supply services under a
modem concept of integrated water management, since all consumers must dispose of used water
whether they are connected to the sewerage network or not. In fact, economic costs may be higher if
they are not connected.
16.    Sewerage service coverage in Monagas is much lower than water supply coverage. However,
because of the area's topography and tropical climate, drainage is a main problem and untreated sewage
is often found in flood waters which become stagnant and are breeding grounds for disease-carrying
insects. Due to leaks in the distribution network, drinking water can also become contaminated.
17.   Supply costs. The water supply system in Monagas is relatively inexpensive. Table 2 shows
its cost structure for altemative estimates of production and for its current level of billing. Even at this
low level of billing (25 out of 64 Mm3), cost per m3 is only Bs 31 (US$ 0.18). This is due to its low
energy costs (the main system is powered by gravity) and, possibly, because the system's operations are
under-budgeted. For example, the use of chemicals is low - which results in poor water quality.
Moreover, commercial costs are low since many of the costs associated with meter reading, billing,
collections, suspensions, etc. are not applicable; in four of the 10 municipalities there is no billing.
Table 2 shows actual costs for 1994 for the combined operations of Monagas water supply.
18.   Although depreciation may be underestimated probably due to a low evaluation of fixed assets,
Table 2 shows that cost per m3 (Bs 31) is less that the new average tariff of Bs 36 per m3. This implies
that an increase in billing would generate net revenues which could help finance important rehabilitation
requirements including the commercial system itself. It must be noted that maintenance costs are
relatively high, yet maintenance is deficient. This is due to deficiencies in the supervision of
maintenance contracts. Such problems could be resolved by the new institutional arrangements.
19.   Total costs are not expected to decline under the new organization; however, average costs
should decrease significantly as water losses decline. For example, most probably, actual consumption
is at nearly 51 Mm3 which, according to Table 2, would have a cost of Bs 15.2 per m3. Cutting water
losses by 20% will increase available production to 64 Mm3 and average cost will fall to Bs 12.1 per
m3. The last column of Table 2 shows average cost per m3 actually billed; even at this low level of
billing, cost per m3 is only Bs 31.

C. Economic impact of the Proposed Project
20.    The main impact of the proposed project is the improvement in overall service quality and
distribution. The main real implication of the deficiency of the current system is the fact that in some
areas water is abundant and used carelessly while in other areas there are substantial shortages, even
during the rainy season when there is sufficient flow in the Amana-Maturin system, in spite of the high
demand and economic value attributed by the users to the service, as measured in the willingness-to-
pay studies carried out (see annex D for details). The economic rationale for this project is based on
achieving two simple objectives: (a) establishing market, organizational and incentive structures that
are more likely than the present ones to generate economically efficient results; and, (b) rehabilitating
the physical system to ensure that water is treated and supplied in an efficient manner, reducing losses.
If properly executed, the project should be highly productive: water consumption ranges from less than
70 liters per person-day (Ipd) in some areas and over 400 lpd in others; the observed marginal value of
water could be as high as Bs 1000 per m3; and water losses represent roughly 20 million m3 per year.
Therefore, the proposed project will generate important benefits in terms of improved water
distribution, reduction in water losses, rationalization of consumption, and overall economic efficiency.
21.     Cost-benefit analysis.  Economic efficiency gains may be measured by comparing the
outcomes of the system under the current and proposed organizational structures; and, given the new
structure, the additional (marginal) impact of the proposed investments is measured by the improved
availability and quality of service. It must be noted that the two levels are not independent. The best
management structure could not do much to improve water delivery, at present, without some
rehabilitation of the network and facilities.



Annex L
Page 7 of 14
22.   The economic evaluation of this project is directed at comparing the economic efficiency of the
current and proposed structures for operating the water supply system for the state of Monagas. On
the benefits side, this includes a comparison of the current cost structure with that which is likely to be
effective under the proposed management arrangement, as well as the effect of better service and
improved distribution of the current supply. The cost includes all investments necessary to normnalize
the system; that is, all works and procedures that would ensure adequate distribution of current
production including adequate application of the price (tariff) system.
23.   It also includes the costs of all that is related to the future operation of the system or the
rehabilitation of intake structures, treatment plants, transmission mains, and the sewerage network
including wastewater treatment plants. These investments will ensure the reliability of future supplies
to meet growing demand for water. Benefits are given by the value of additional demand satisfied (the
value of increase in quality and extemal effects, such as those on health, have not been valued to remain
in the safe side of the analysis).
Demand
24.   Consumption. As indicated, water consumption is low in areas where pressures are too low
or pipes have lost most of their capacity because of age or leaks or both8; and it is high in other areas.
There is no reliable data on consumption since billing is based on a flat rate which, only formally, is
related to a rate per m3 and an assigned monthly volume per household. However, estimates of water
production (51 to 94 Mm3 per year) and estimated physical losses (20 Mm3 per year) indicate that
actual consumption could be between 31 and 74 Mm3 which is between 151 and 459 lpd. Most
probably, consumption is between 50 and 55 Mm3 per year, or as much as 270 lpd. However, a main
characteristic of the current system is its poor distribution, with some areas consuming over 1,000 lpd
and others less that 70 lpd. Non-residential consumption is only 16% of total consumption, according
to billing figures.
25.   Characterization of the demand function. With available information, it is not possible to
estimate the demand function in a conventional manner. However, several aspects are known about
consumer behavior in the system which allows for an approximation of the demand curve, or at least of
its boundaries.
8      Due to inadequate chlorination, a considerable build-up of bacteria-produced iron compounds are
reducing pipe sections to as little as 15% of its original size.
