26.    For example, it is known that consumers pay as much as the equivalent of Bs 1000 per m3 for
water delivered by truck in 200 liter barrels. This is the rate charged by private operators. The
municipalities have their own trucks to provide this service, although they do not charge a fee. In the
recent dry season, a similar rate was paid in some areas of Maturin by consumers in shortage areas to
other residential and commercial consumers. This price applies both to small quantities in areas where



Annex L
Page 9 of 14
consumers are not connected to the network as well as to marginal quantities used to supplement
limited supplies from the network.
27.   Results of a contingent valuation survey indicate that consumers are willing to pay much higher
rates than they are now paying for a reliable water service. According to these results, 91% of
consumers favor a reliable system at a tariff of Bs 2,000 per month, and 78% favor the system at a
tariff of Bs 4,000. The first figure is equivalent to a price of Bs 50 per m3 for a consumption of 40 m3
per month. These figures are consistent with responses in situations in which service is inexpensive but
deficient, and they are certainly affordable. The new residential rate is Bs 1865 per month per
connection, or Bs 60 per m3, and Bs 800 per month per connection or Bs 20 per m3, on average, for
the social group.
28.   Currently, prices are low (marginal price is zero) and the corresponding quantity demanded is
either constrained by the state of the distribution network in some areas or as much as customers can
consume in others.
29.   Total demand may be as much as 55 million n3 at a zero marginal price. This is obtained from
42,000 connections, 12 persons per connection, and 300 liters/day. At these levels, demand is highly
inelastic and behaves differently from a conventional demand function. Only a significant increase in
prices would reveal regular demand responses. This could take place immediately when meters are
installed and a per m3 rate is applied. At present, for example, consumers do not have any incentives to
make the most simple repairs to reduce water leaks at home.
30.   From the above facts, the demand function should be approximately as shown in Table 3 and
Figure 1. Quantities demanded, shown in Table 3 are determined by the following equation: Q = 55.0 -
0.055 P, a simple straight line function where 55.0 Mm3 is the assumed maximum volume of domestic
consumption at current prices (zero marginal price) and supply restrictions for some consumers. The
slope (-0.055) is determined by the relation between maximum consumption and maximum
"willingness to pay" (Bs 1000 per mi3) as stated above. Results from the contingent valuation survey
consistently fit in this range.
31.   If it is assumed that consumers pay a maximum of 5% of their income on water service, then
Table 3 (income-constrained consumption) shows, at different prices, the amounts that a household
with a total income of two minimum salaries could get. It must be noted that only at prices above Bs
300 per m3, a household could only get below 100 lpd. However, as mentioned before, some
consumers already pay the equivalent of Bs 1,000 per m3 for small quantities.
Impact of the proposed project
32.   Current and expected situation without the project. Without the proposed project, it is
expected that the distribution network will continue to deteriorate, current shortages will become more
severe and losses will continue to increase due to lack of maintenance. In areas where there is sufficient
supply, there will be no rationalization of consumption as long as adequate pricing or rationing.are not
implemented. Total costs may remain constant but average costs will increase due to a reduction in
production.



Annex L
Page 10 of 14
33.   Expected situation with the project. With the project, a gradual improvement is expected.
The main benefits will be generated when it becomes possible, through improvement of the distribution
network and better operational and financial management, to reduce losses from waste and excessive
consumption in some areas and allocate them in shortage areas. Total variable costs may increase due
to improvements in production and commercial processes but average costs may decline due to
increases in production.
34.   In order to measure this impact, it is assumed that a gradual shift from areas with unlimited
consumption to shortage areas, will take place in the next 5 years as a result of the proposed project.
This shift will reallocate as much as 10% (5.5 Mm' per year)9 of maximum demand from areas with
excessive consumption.
35.   This measurement is illustrated in Figure I using the estimates of Table 3. Consumption will be
decreased through price or non-price means (rationing, better controls) from a "market" which
consumes excessive amounts at zero price. This volume will be shifted toward a "market" which
currently has permanent shortages due to low pressures or distribution problems (deteriorated pipes,
non-working valves, in-house leaks, etc.) and where water has a higher economic value. In this case,
there is a zero net transfer in terms of water volumes. 10
36.   In the market with excess consumption, the water is valued at between zero and Bs 60 per m3.
In the market with shortages, this water could be valued anywhere between Bs 100 and 1000 per m3,
depending on the location where service would be improved. Since for the system as a whole, water is
not scarce even during the dry season, new customers and households that purchase water to
supplement their insufficient piped supplies will benefit the most since they normally pay the barrel
price of Bs 1000 per m3. Other households, with restricted supplies, who normally would not purchase
additional water, would benefit the least depending on the level of their supplies. In any case all
households vvith restricted supplies are likely to benefit from an improved distribution network and
operation.
37.   Therefore, there is a substantial net gain from this reallocation. As seen from Figure 1, the loss
- in terms of consumer surplus - to the first group is offset by a gain in the same amount to the second
group, while there is a net value of 5.5 Mm3 at Bs 100 per m3 to the second group. This is a
conservative estimate since, the distribution of consumption is likely to eliminate shortages in the dry
months. Therefore the gain in consumer surplus will be much larger than the estimated here, since here
the shift in consumption is assumed to be at the next inframarginal level. That is in Figure 1 the shift
should be from consumption at the right hand of the demand function, to consumption near the left
hand side of the demand function where the consumer surplus is much larger.
9     This volume could provide the 120,000 people in Maturin affected by severe shortages an additional 20
m3 per household per month. Assuming 6 persons per household, this is equivalent to 4.8 million m3 per
year.
I 0   There could be a net gain if some of the transfer is from physical losses.

38.   The value of this net gain is US$ 3.24 nillion per year (5.5 Mmr3 x 100 Bs/m3). If it is further
assumed that in 1997 only 20% of this effect will take place, 40% in 1998, 60% in 1999, 80% in 2000,
and 100% thereafter, net gains per year are shown in Table 4. These gains are discounted at 12%"
over a 20 year period to yield a Present Value of about US$ 16.94 million.
39.    Another important effect that should take place as the system is repaired and its operation
improved is the recovery of physical losses, estimated now at 20 Mm3 per year or nearly one-third of
production. If in the next five years, these loses are reduced by 50%, that is, 10 Mnm3 are recovered by
the year 2001 - a feasible goal - such volume will be available to areas with current deficits and to new
consumers. The value of this additional supply, assuming a constant value of Bs 60 per m3 is US$ 3.53
million per year (a net present value of US$ 205 million in 20 years; see Table 4). This level is reached
gradually by the year 2001 and increases thereafter with population growth (assumed at 2.5% per
year).

40.    Project costs. The total cost of the project is estimated at US$ 71 million. If taxes, price
escalation and interest charges are subtracted the project's economic cost is US$ 54.35 million, with a
net present value of US$ 38.05 million, while the cost of the rehabilitation investments over the project
execution period is US$ 36.5 rillion (65% of total costs, with a net present value of US$ 25.62
million), distributed as shown in Table 4. No shadow valuation or other adjustments of financial costs
were deemed necessary.
41.    Evaluating benefits at present tariff level (residential rate Bs 60/m3), the economic rate of return
of the project's rehabilitation investments (as said above, about 65% of project costs) is 15.2%. If
consumer surpluses, based on a marginal social value of Bs 100/mi3, are also taken into account in the
calculation, the rate of return rises to 27%. The main contributor to project benefits is the reduction in
physical losses. A significant number of consumers are willing to pay as much as Bs 1000 per m3, and
the results of the contingent valuation study show that 91% of consumers are willing to pay Bs 50 per
m3 for an adequate supply while 78% would pay Bs 100 for the same volume.



Annex L
Page 13 of 14
Sensitivity analysis
42.    The most critical parameters in the calculation of the project's net benefits are the marginal
value of water, the volume of water which can be recovered and the amount that can be redistributed.
The above estimates may be considered a conservative scenario, since the value of water (Bs 60 per
mi) is the current price; the 5.5 Mm' per year redistributed is only 10% of most probable consumption,
and the 10 Mm3 of physical losses recovered to be reached in 5 years is only 10% of production and
50% of estimated physical losses. This goals should be easily achieved if the project is properly
executed.
43.    If the price of water is increased to Bs 150 per M3, the IRR of the project increases to 42%
However, if losses are reduced by only 5 Mm3 per year, the IRR for the project falls to 8.5% and is nil
if no physical losses are recovered. Other things equal, water recovery should be no less than 7.5 Mm3
per year in order for the IRR of the rehabilitation investments not to fall below 12%. Similarly, if
investment costs increase by 50%, the IRR would fall to 8.5% (as most of project's investment costs
are going to be local and tariffs will be adjusted in relation to the cost of the service, the sensitivity of
the analysis to exchange rate changes is low).
E. Additional Rationale for Project Justification
44     The proposed project has important impacts beyond its direct economic benefits. it provides
the opportunity to demonstrate the gains, generated at various levels, of a decentralized water supply
sector and contributes to the performance of other sectors in the state of Monagas, as the health sector.
This project will be the first experience in Venezuela in the process of decentralizing the structure
controlled by the central govemment and managed by the regional agencies at considerable costs to the
central government and large economic losses in the supply of services to the population. The success
of the project will induce replications across the country, thus reducing government losses and
increasing quality and quantity of water supplies. At the macroeconomic level, there will be clear gains
in the reduction of public expenditures to cover the HIDROs deficits without reducing the level of
service. There will be, in fact, a shift from deficit financing to revenue generation as water tariffs are
rationalized, and the service itself is improved. At the sector level, the proposed project will have an
important impact not only on promoting a rational structure to administer water supply systems
throughout the country, but it will also contribute to the performance of other sectors such as public
health. As shown below, the deficient water and sewerage service is being regarded as a "public health
problem" in the Monagas region. These further benefits are not being accounted here, when assessing
the rate of retum. Thus the evaluated rate of return here assessed is a lower bound of the real rate of
return, since those benefits are also derived from this project.
45     Macroeconomic effects.  The central govemment subsidizes the water sector directly
through HIDROVEN's budget and indirectly through arrears with electricity and other suppliers. It is
estimated that only 40% of the water sector's operating costs are recovered through tariffs This figure
is only 25% for the State of Monagas. This transfer is estimated at between US$ 100 and 150 million



Annex L
Page 14 of 14
annually, in constant terms, which is part of the public sector's operating deficit.12 Although, this is
relatively small compared to the public sector's operating deficit of nearly US$ 4.0 billion in 1994, it is
an impoitant contributor to any deficit reduction strategy because it does not require a reduction in
services, in fact, it is expected that service will improve under a decentralized, self-financed system.
46.    The central govemment could also make substantial savings in investment costs. According to
fIlDROVEN's investment budget for 1995, Bs 23,497 millions (US$ 138 million) will be spent in
water and sanitation infrastructure. However, from the State of Monagas projections, the system could
repay its investments from its own tariff income.
47.    Health sector impact. An improvement in the performance of the water and sewerage sector
will have a significant impact on the health sector. According to the Regional Directorate of Health for
the state of Monagas, water is considered a "health problem" in the state. Increases in the incidence of
water-borne diseases have been reported by all health districts due to poor water quality which is
associated with deficient treatment which, in turn, is associated with lack of an adequate budget for
operations.
48.    The analysis of the value of water did not include the health impact of potable water. This
conservative approach was used since in this case there are public health benefits which are not
captured by this parameter because, among other reasons, most health services are free in the state of
Monagas. That is, consumers do not consider, in the price of water, any benefits from savings in health
costs which they do not pay. However, local health authorities assumed that 15% of the population
are affected annually by water-caused health problems, and that about one-half of these cases (42,000)
can be prevented by improved water and sanitation services and, further, that each case costs the health
system Bs 10,0003 . Based on these assumptions, it can be estimated that the annual savings which
could be achieved through this project would be around Bs 420 million, or approximately US$ 2.4
million. In addition, losses in productivity as a result of absences from work due to illness amount to
56,000 work days per year, at a cost of about Bs 100.8 million per year (US$ 0.576 million per year)14
Thus, the total impact resulting of improved availability of water and quality of service was estimated at
Bs 520.8 million (US$ 3.0 million) annually, which would be reached gradually as the project is
implemented.
12     It is estimated that in 1994, water and sewerage subsidies reached Bs 15,024 million which, at the average
cxchange rate for 1994 (150 Bs/US$), is equivalent to US$ 100 million. This is relatively low since it was
calculated using Bs 1,252 as operating cost per person (the average for the State of Monagas) and it does
not include areas with electricity suppliers.
13     This estimate is based on the average cost of one visit to the doctor and of prescription medicine. For most
people, in the State of Monagas, this is provided free by the public health system. This cost is, therefore,
the cost to the public sector.
This is estimated assuming an average monthly salary (productivity) of Bs 45,000, or Bs 1,800 per day,
and one-third of the affected population in the labor force and 2 days lost per occurrence on average.
